Dr. Me PERKY,

SPECIALIST for the Treatment of Nervous
Complaints, Diabetes, Tumors, &c.
Registered by the Medical 80-. r lof jy.S. W. Late House Sur-
geon t) the Great Northern Hospital, London.
I am aware that I am not strictly following the paths
of professional etiquette by advertising myself as a
Specialist for the t' eatm-nt of this class of disease, but,
unfortunately for the suffering public in Australia, the
apathy shown by Qualified Men in the treatment of
Chronic and Nervous Complaints has induced, men of the
lowest grade of intellect (whose only qualifications are
unlimited assurance) to spring up, and by specious
advertisements and false promises trap the unwary.
It is very evident that sufferers from these diseases
have been scandalously treated by these unqualified
persons The evidence taken at the Medical Enquiry
held in Sydney (published in the Daily Telegraph , Nov.
6th 7th and Bth, 1890) plainly shows the disgraceful
manner in which patients are duped. Not only are
they robbed of their money, but what is of infinitely
greater importance, their health is injured also. It is
therefore time that regally Qualified Men entered the
field to fight these pests of society with their own
weapons, viz., Advertising.
It is utterly impossible for those who are suffering
from these diseases, who are scattered over nil parts of
Australasia, to ascertain which Qualified Practitioner
makes these diseases his speciality unless he advertises.
I consider, therefore, that by advertising I not only
strike a blow at the Quackery which now exists , but T also add
to the usefulness of my profession and in no way lessen my
personal dignity.
My Qualifications I Invite the fullest investigation Into. For some considerable time I was House Surgeon to the
Great Northern Hospital, London, where I had special opportunities of observing the many phases of the Nervous and
Sexual System, and to become acquainted with the most scientific methods of effecting their cure.
My sole attention is given to the treatment of these diseases, and when a person consults me lie is informed at the
commencement whether I can effect his cure or not. The patient is not deluded with false and specious promises, and
if 1 undertake the case
I Absolutely Guarantee a Complete ami Permanent Cure.
My great success in the treatment of these diseases enables mo to give this guarantee without fear.

instances is sufficient t> check the progress of di case. This Hook will ho forwarded Free on application.

This eminent Specialist, Founder, and Chief
Surgeon of the long-established and favourably-
known Dispensary that now bears his name, re-
ceived his medical education in the leading colleges
and hospitals of Europe. He has devoted a life-
time to t he study of
PRIVATE, NERVOUS, AND SKIN
DISEASES.
He has by profound research thoroughly investi-
gated their origin, traced their deadly sources and
insidious dissemination, and ascertained the surest
and safest methods by which they may be eradi-
cated, with these widely recognised qualifications
combined with unswerving faithfulness to his
patients, he has during his practice, extending
over FORTY YEARS, attained a well-deserved
pre-eminence in their treatment, iiis practice
embraces diseases which probably cause more un-
happiness than any other class of troubles that
afflict mankind.
YOUNG MEN
who suffer from the results of early follies, trans-
gressions or excesses of more nature age, bladder
difficulties, a whitish or dark ropy sediment in
the water, accompanied by slight burning or
smarting sensation, Headaches, Drowsiness during
the day, circles round the eyes, Giddiness,
irregularity of the bowels, specks before the eyes,
bashfulness, discontentedness, weakness or pains
in the back, Liver or kidney complaints, dreams,
buzzing noises in the ears and head, pimples, a
fear that something dreadful is about to happen,

Noble-street, Newton, Geelong,
10th December, 1888.
To H. F. Kugelmann, Esq.
Dear Sir, —This is to certify that I, Patrick Keenan, was
taken ill about three years ago with a severe cold, which
turned into Bronchitis and became chronic. I consulted
more than one doctor, and during that time took a great
quantity of medicine without it doing me the slightest
good, but I became worse every day, wasting away until
I became a perfect skeleton. About February last I began
to spit up blood, and what with the cough and severe
pains in my left lung, and the quantity of bad matter I#
was expectorating every day, I became very much alarmed.
Hearing of your professional skill I consulted you, and
you told me you could cure me for a given sum. I agreed
and went under your treatment in March last. At the
end of three months I was taken bad one morning, and
vomited about two pints of blood, matter and stringy
phlegm. From that time I commenced to improve, and
was gaining in weight every day. About a month ago the
blood ceased coining, the cough disappeared, and the
lung healed completely, and thank God and your pro-
fessional skill, I am as well as ever.
P. KEENAN.
Declared and subscribed before me, one of Her Majesty’s
Justices of the Peace, for the Southern Bailiwick, in the
County of Grant, this 10th day of December, 1888.
Peter Smyth*, J.P.

SATURDAY, JANUARY 10, 1891.
State Servants’ Citizenship.
The question at issue between the New
South Wales Railway Commissioners and
Mr. H. C. Hoyle, President of the New
South Wales Railway and Tramway Associa-
tion, and until the other day an employe in
the service of the department, is much
obscured by the correspondence and reported
interviews which have appeared concerning
it. The principle which gives the dispute a
national importance is buffeted about and
sometimes completely hidden by odds and
ends of asseverations as to what words the
Commissioners used to Mr. Hoyle and Mr.
Hoyle to the Commissioners. Incidentally
we may say that we accept the apparently
contradictory statements of Mr. Hoyle and
the Commissioners as being both veracious,
inasmuch as veracity, not being always
identical with truth, denotes merely a rela-
tion of agreement between what a person
says he said and what he thinks he said.
Beyond this, we dismiss from view the entire
wrangle about the accuracy of phrases as
being of interest only in the limited sphere
of the personal characters of the contestants,
and we turn to the question of fact which is
really one of the widest and highest impor
tance.
* * *
Hitherto the employes in the New South
Wales Railway Department have asserted a
right to take the ordinary citizen’s partin the
discussion of public affairs ; and although this
part has been frequently prominent and ag-
gressive neither Government nor Parliament
has thought it necessary to interfere. The
employes in the other State departments
have so far put forward no organised claim
to such a right, and it is customary, there-
fore, to consider that they continue in their
positions on the voluntary sacrifice of certain
democratic rights. Here again the practice
of Parliament and of the Government is one
of non-intervention. It thus appears that
of the several arms of the New South Wales
Public Service, the Railway one, while willing
to work for its bread in the Service, has
refused and refuses to forego the right to
express publicly opinions on public affairs,
while the others, for the sake of the employ-
ment which they obtain, are content either
not to think at all on public questions or to
keep their mouths closed in the presence of
the public when such questions are dis-
cussed & This departure of the railway and-
tramway men from Civil Service traditions
is demonstrably a democratic gain, and the
tacit assent which Parliament gave to the
new practice proves that if other branches of
the Service move for equal rights they are
likely to obtain them. The State workman
who dares not publicly discuss any public
question would be quite Justified in declining
to vote on that particular question. Indeed,
(ho logical corollary of a prohibition to
discuss is a prohibition to vote, and this
is so acutely felt by many officials that they
profess themselves prepared to accept
absolute disfranchisement as an honorable
escape from their present position.
* * #
Discipline must, it is contended, be pre-
served in the State departments. Nobody
seeks to deny this. But to possess and
declare one’s opinions is not necessarily
insurrectionary. Further, discipline is much
more safe, in so far as it is a true instru-
ment for good and not a mere martinet’s
fad in the hands of the thinker and
reasoner than in those of the man who has
succeeded in putting his faculties asleep. Cases
of the abuse of rights will arise, but where
they arise they are to be treated as abuses
of rights and not as things in their origin
wrong. If a railway-man criminally mis-
represent or calumniate the heads of his
department or of any department he should
be liable to punishment for the proved ex-
cesses, but not for the fact that he addressed
the public from a balcony. He has a right
to do the latter; those whom he maligns
have a right to keep him from doing the
former. This does not appear to be clear to
the Commissioners. They do not, indeed,
formally prohibit their employes from dis-
cussing public affairs, but they maintain that
in the discussion of railway matters em-
ployes may not express opinions at variance
with those of the heads of the department.
# # #
A little reflection will show that this is
tantamount to a suppression of the right of
public speaking altogether. If it be a
punishable disloyalty to the Commissioners
to say that there are better ways of managing
affairs than the present, then there is no use
in public meetings or public discussions on
that subject at all. The subject is effectually
closed. This view of the Commissioners’
attitude is intensified by their admitted
allegation that Mr. Hoyle sat at a public
meeting and listened to their actions being
condemned. But the essence of a public
meeting is that freedom of opinion be
allowed. Had Mr. Hoyle gratified the
Commissioners he would have negatived the
discussion of thesubject absolutely. The Com-
missioners thus leave it to be inferred that in
their eyes the only legal public meeting
which their employes can attend is one in
which the Commissioners’ public conduct
will be approved. But it was not worth the
while of the railway and tramway men to
emancipate themselves from the sterilizing
traditions of the general State employe to
attain a share of citizenship so transparently
a sham.
# # *
The national question is: Shall the de-
parture made by therailwayand tramway men
be maintained, and pursued to even a larger
freedom, or are the men to be throttled and
turned back into the narrow grooves of
the past ? Are they to be taken as
showing the way to a platform of higher
citizenship, or are they to be whipped
back and yarded in with the other Civil
servants who, for the sake of a living, allow
the seal of silence to be stamped upon
their lips ? Is a gag to be fashioned out of
their daily loaf of official bread? Which-
ever conclusion is arrived at, it is Parliament,
and not the Railway Commissioners, who
must have the last word. Mr. Hoyle has
sat on platforms during several years, and,
with the knowledge of his department and
of Governments, has given his opinions
freely on public affairs.' Two of the mem-
bers of the Legislative Assembly sit there
now largely by his electioneering efforts.
And Mr. Hoyle is only one of those in his
class who thus spoke and acted in the open.
The absence of official interference over this
period constitutes, we maintain, a prescrip-
tive right, which Parliament alone can
abrogate, to continue in the path taken;
and officers like the Railway Commissioners
should be vested with power to punish only
for specifically-proved excesses of speech on
the part of their employes, while only the
tribunals appointed to deal with such matters
shall have power to decide as to what con-
stitutes an illegal utterance on the part of
a State servant.
The Collapse of a Cause Celebre.
The last and most important of the Premier Per*
manent Building Society prosecutions—the one
from which the shareholders in that shattered
institution expected to get a little moral satis-
faction for their money—has terminated in a
scandal worthy of such a bad-smelling business.
After two days of earnest consideration,
assisted by Christmas goose and bottled beer,
an abnormally unintelligent - looking jury
repudiated the summing-up of Chief Justice
Higinbotham by announcing that they found
none of the defendants guilty and six of them
innocent. After which these twelve apostles of
justice rushed headlong from the box to draw
fees amounting to £6O odd a-piece for having
made themselves objects of derision to every
nineteen out of twenty sensible men in Mel-
bourne. That there might be no public doubt
concerning their mental calibre some among
the twelve promptly divulged the secrets of
the jury-room, and explained that at the outset
only three of their number were in favour
of releasing the whole boiling of de-
fendants from any criminal responsibility
in the ruin they had wrought. Little by little,
as the Christmas dinner-hour drew nearer and
thoughts of peace and goodwill began to steal
gently into their addled brains, other jurymen
went over to the original trio. In a word, the
tail began to wag the dog. By order of the
Court an official picnic was tendered to the still-
conflicting deliberators on Christmas Day, and
under l lie benign influence of bottled bier
eleven jurymen decided to letoffwholedamlot.
Only the foreman held out for the conviction of
at least two defendants. Some of his colleagues
did their best to alter the foreman’s resolution by
praying that his “ soul might burn within him,”
and advancing towards him in threatening atti-
tudes with beer-bottles, possibly, in their
large, honest, right hands. But this obdurate
person held tightly to his opinion. He even
refused to remember that his conduct might
“put the country to the expense of another
trial,” and so there was nothing left for the
jury but to return to Court and wrangle in the
box until the fact that they convicted nobody
had been clumsily explained.
* * *
Therewith ended, as the Age had delicately
insinuated in its Christmas Day issue, all pros-
pect of any alleged conspirators being brought
to account. The Chief Justice, trained to re-
ceive jury-verdicts without a wince, proceeded
to sentence Doherty and Mikams, both ex-
secretaries of the Association, to fairly substan-
tial terms of imprisonment for offences venial as
compared with that which was “ not proven ”
against Doherty and Gourlay on the more
sensational charge. He echoed the sentiments
of Melbourne in his kindly remarks to Messrs.
Aim mo and Stewart, and kept a discreet
silence about the rest of the batch, of whom
two at least seemed bound to be acquitted on
the summing-up. The case having petered out
thus miserably, it only remained for the Vic-
torian Government to announce that no further
proceedings would be instituted against
Doherty (already sentenced, subject to appeal,
for his misdemeanour) and Gourlay, the gentle-
man described by Purves as “the Age’s blood
relation.” Nobody who had studied the
attitude of the whole Legislature towards
the P.B.A. cases, and who remembered that
former Ministers of the Crown had been im-
plicated in these serious. charges, imagined that
the present guardians of public interests would
fail to jump at the first opportunity to let
public interests be blowed in this particular
connection. Land-boomer doesn't bite land-
boomer except in the way of business, nor does
the director of one building society perceive
much urgent necessity for attempting to prove
that the director of another building society has
waxed fat on the nmi of its shareholders. Not
as public life in Victoria goes, anyhow. Colonel
Templeton’s plucky prosecution of sundry
“ representative ” persons had alarmed the
Legislature, and in refusing to further carry on
the business which was forced upon the “State”
a few months ago, and which the “State”
hadn’t sufficient brazen impudence to wind up
at the time, Messrs. Munro and Co. are acting
according to their lights.
* * *
True, the Argus has said that “ a gross
failure of justice will occur unless the question
of the criminality of Doherty and Gourlay is
finally set at rest, either by conviction or
acquittal,” and the general public are muttering
expressions of unqualified disgust at street
corners. The unusual circumstance of the
Argus being on the popular side counts for
nothing in this fishy case. Besides, the Age—
that influential, disinterested and deeply-
respected friend of the taxpayer—has never
ceased to point out that the prosecution of its
“ blood relation ” and the other defendants was
a shameful waste of money, and an outrage upon
political economy. At first the Age shrieked
for the stoppage of proceedings on the ground
that they were bringing more ruin upon im-
poverished widows and orphans, depending
upon the mangled assets of the P.B.A. for the
means to bury themselves respectably. When
the Government had to stand the racket it
became necessary for the Ag e to shift its solici-
tude from the widows and orphans on to the
main body of taxpayers. And, as if deter-
mined to “give itself away” altogether, the
organ of Mr. David Syme has affected
to faint over the discovery that the
taxpayers of Victoria have been victimised
to the extent of £2o,ooo—or, say, two
shillings and ninepence each. Consider-
ing that Gillies has but recently finished
pouring hundreds of thousands of pounds down
the political sink, and that a cool million of
money is to be expended altogether upon Par-
liament House, few taxpayers would have
objected to spending another shilling or two on
the “conviction or acquittal” of Doherty or
Gourlay, a goose-gorged jury having put every-
body else beyond the reach of retribution.
* * *
One word more. Whilst the Court was wait-
ing for the verdict a newspaper reporter ap-
proached Purves, Q.C., and asked whether
further proceedings would be abandoned in the
event of the jury disagreeing. To which ques-
tion Purves replied that the Government
couldn't drop the charge until it was satisfac-
torily cleared up, and that, in the event of the
Government doing what it couldn’t do (but has
done) he, Purves, would have some pertinent
remarks to make. We are now panting to hear
those pertinent remarks from Purves.
The Tyrant from Brummagem.
Mr. Willis, M.P., having failed to “draw”
Premier Sir Henry Parkes by a series of
Sydney newspaper diatribes which would have
evoked legal thunderblasts from any other
public man beneath the sky, now announces
that he is about to stab that virtuous old
gentleman under the fifth rib by printing a
pamphlet containing a full and accurate
account of all that is known about H. P. and
distributing the same all over the earth—more
particularly in the quarters concerning whose
opinion H.P. is particularly sensitive : among
editors of the various British newspapers and
journals and members of the ’Ouse of Lords and
Commons. Lord Knutsford, the British Secre-
tary of State, to whom Mr. Willis is appealing
concerning his removal from the New South
Wales Commission of the Peace, is, we read, to
be fully instructed in Parkesiana.
# * *
When W illis friend Bloxham became a
•1.P., lie swore allegiance to Queen Victoria,
but not to the Prince of Wales ; nevertheless
Parkes has removed him from the Commission
of the Peace for having said nasty things about
that bloated boon companion of the infamous
Earl of Aylesford, the not less contemptible
Duke of Marlborough and others of a like
kidney. As the glorified turner from Brum-
magem and his colleagues have extended to
Albert Edward of Wales a consideration
generally understood to appertain to reigning
royalty alone, it would be well to know where
Parkes draws the line ? Is it, for instance,
penal in New South Wales to disparage that
State-fed pauper, Battenberg V Is the
memory of the late J. Brown a sacred thing in
Botany Bay ?
** * #
Parkes should have mailed Willis’ alleged
libels to the British Treasury solicitors, with a
view to their taking such steps as they might
deem proper in vindication of Tummy’s charac-
ter. As Premier of New South Wales he was
in no way bound to protect the smudgy reputa-
tion of the heir-apparent to the British mon-
archy. Parkes, who has, as the world remem-
bers, himself vainly attempted to obtain from
an Australian jury that vindication which, as a
rule, a jury is only too ready to give to the
character of any honest and decent man, would
do well to look after his own reputation instead
of using such practically irresponsible powers
as he unfortunately at present possesses as
instruments of toadyism and paltry tyranny,
* *
Mr. \\ illis cannot legitimately growl at
being dispossessed of his J.P. ship. As his
newspaper, to which lie had bumptiously
attached his name in big black letters after the
fashion of journalistic amateurs, had been the
vehicle of a frantic appeal to the people of the
country to take up arms against the existing
legislative authorities, of which he himself was
a paid unit, Parkes hit him at once, just as a
man hits you when you challenge him to fight.
But the removal of Bloxham is quite another
matter. If you summarily penalise a J.P. for
maligning the Prince of Wales, you might
just as well penalise him for speaking ill of the
Czar, who is brother-in-law of the Duke of
Edinburgh, the brother of the Prince of
Wales.
* * *
The Bloxham incident casts a lurid light on
the sort of relations which the arch-apostle of
“ Federation under the Crown ” would fain
establish between the free and independent
people of Australia and the commonplace and
selfish brood of Germans who so long have
fattened on the stupidity of the British people.
Australia, we think, will hardly accept any
scheme of Federation held out to it in the right
hand of a vain old humbug who, in his left hand,
clutches a gag to be applied to the right of free
speech. We hope to see Albert Edward come
to Australia and prosecute Editor Bloxham. If
he does, he will get just as fair a trial as was
given to H. Parkes on the occasion when the
latter so dismally failed in his attempt to have
Editor \\ ebb put into gaol. The illustrious
personage who wrote to his friend Lady Mok-
daunt to “ lay up for another fortnight ” will,
we say, have a much fairer trial than that given
to the lunatic who, when Parkes was in office
many years ago, s''ot the Duke of Edinburgh
at Clontarf.
* # *
T’he conduct of Parkes in connection with
the O’Farrell case, the Proctor case, the
Walker case, the Bloxham case, the Socialist
lecture case, and a score of other cases has been
such as to brand the shallow old posturer as a
conscienceless political tyrant and as a sneak of
the very first order. His interference vyith the
Socialist lectures on the ground that they are
“ blasphemous,” is an effectual exposure of his
own ignorance. Even were they what is known
as blasphemous, it is difficult to see how that
could warrant Governmental interference in a
community where Church and State are happily
not united. Parkes belongs to that breed of
tyrants who carefully select their victims from
among those presumably incapable of serious
resentment.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
For Ever and Ever.
There is no end to the drivel of the cablegram
fiend, and his parochial intelligence rises-
superior to all considerations of time or space
or ordinary human probability. For instance,
the following mighty hunk of lore comes out as
a recent sample :
H.E.H. the Duke of Clarence, who was yesterday
installed as Provincial Grand Master of the Freemasons
for Berkshire, warmly welcomed Lord Cari.noton back
from New South Wales. Lord Carington, in reply, pre-
dicted that Australia would for ever remain loyal to
England.
For ever and ever ! Lord Carington has said
it with a weary yawn upon his open counten-
ance, and the flabby little duke, whose face is
as expressionless as an African’s feet, has
accepted the statement with a profound “ haw,’’
and the cable has endorsed it, and the S.M.
Herald has published it; and therefore Time
petrifies and Progress falls into a cataleptic
state, and Change becomes unchangeable for
ever and ever. Everything else in earth
and sea and sky is unstable and
transitory. The hills are being fast
frittered away and washed into the sea, and the
sea is silting up into uew r mountain ranges, and
England itself is dissolving and sinking into the
Atlantic, till in course of time there will be
nothing but water from Labrador to the Skager
Rack, and even the Pole Star is drifting away
into the far recesses of the heavens ; but when
everything else is lust and gone the kawlinies
will still remain loyal to England, for Caking -
ton dropped in as he was going past and
mentioned the fact. Somewhere about a century
and half ago most of North America was a’s >
in a state of eternal loyalty to the British
Crown, and most of South America was as
devoutly attached to Spain ; and 200 years ago
England was the perpetual pensioner and
dependant of France ; and (100 years further
back she was the humble slave of Normandy
and was to remain so for ever and ever ; and
in a still more remote era she was loyal to Rome
with a loyalty that was to endure through
all time and eternity, but all these
things died away, and every “ loyal ”
sentiment that ever bound one country to
another since the first beginning has proved


more transient than a snowball in Gehenna, or
a vague hypothesis written upon the sea in
letters of foam. But though nothing else is
permanent, the loyalty of the colonies is to keep
on through all ages, for, as already mentioned,
Carington has said it, and the flea has shaken
its foot at the stars and told them to keep
where they are till further orders, and the fly
lias grabbed the tail of the universe and is
holding it steady. It is a joyous thing to
possess so much information, and when we con-
sider what a heap Carington knows, we feel
sad over the ignorance of the prophets and the
desperate stupidity of the apostles, for they
could only see a few years ahead, while Baron
Smith glares down au unending futurity, and is
either three parts omnipotent or else an ass.
The Maoriland Labour Victory.
Despite much hard lying to the contrary, the
recent elections in New Zealand have resulted
in a gigantic victory for the cause of Labour.
The last Parliament, elected under the old
system of plural voting, contained no Labour
party whatever ; in the present Parliament 24
out of the 74 members have been returned on
this issue alone, and thus the new party is
already as strong as either of the old ones.
Under these circumstances there is already
much talk about the necessity of forming a
Coalition Ministry out of the wreckage of the
Atkinson and Ballancb factions, as the only
means of carrying on the government—which is
a simple but high-class mode of stating that it
is time for Property to sink its own differences
and devote itself to squashing the common
herd. For a good many years the present
Government and the present Opposition have
raged unceasingly at each other and called
down all the demons of thunder and political
smash upon each other’s heads, and each has
proved conclusively that the country Avas lost,
and could only be saved by Atkinson keeping
his billet and his salary or by Ballance
getting them, as the case might be.
There was no visible difference between their
policies, and they both issued a big placard
Avhen an election was in progress stating that a
judicious system of retrenchment Avas badly
Avanted, and when Atkinson announced, amid
tremendous excitement, that another £2O a
year had been saved by reducing the salary of
the metropolitan dog-drowner, Ballance would
manifest his economy by moving for a reduction
of £5 more ; but they got on very Avell and drew
their honorarium satisfactorily even on these
scanty materials, and each was resolved to fight
to the last gasp and fall nobly amid the
applause of a frenzied earth Noav that a third
party has appeared on the scene, hoAvcA’er, this
old arrangement has fallen through; the two
original combatants are both bidding for the
support of the Labour members, but if neither
of them can get it they intend to resolve that
they have held exactly the same views all along,
and that their apparent enmity Avas merely an
optical illusion ; and then they Avill weep tears
of joy and toss up for the jobs and the salaries.
But if, on the other hand, either Atkinson or
Ballance can get the support of the Labour
party by making a general promise to
do something of a large, hazy, democra-
tic character at some unspecified period
in the future, and can thus secure the loaves
and fishes for himself by giving the shadow of
the crusts and the dim reflection of the fins and
tails to his new supporters, then he will issue
a manifesto stating that his rival is practically
a liar and a hog, and there Avill be no Coalition
for the present. But by-and-bye the Labour
representatives Avill find out that they have been
sold, and will fall aAvay from the alliance, and
then Atkinson or Ballance, as the case may
be, Avill explain that the liar was a misprint and
that he was wrongly reported in the matter of
the hog. Whereupon Ballance or Atkinson,
Avhoever may be on the other side, will accept
the explanation eheei’fully—provided He can’t
get the Labour A r ote himself ; but if he can get
it then he will denounce his opponent—
whichever it a loathly worm, and
Avill set up for himself, and thus he
will either advance or retreat with great
brilliance as the affair may turn out, and a
great conflict Avill be either brought about or
aA r erted—it doesn’t matter which. The moral
of all this is that the Labour party has gained a
great victory in securing 24 out of 74 seats, but
until it has au absolute majority of its own the
results will not amount to much. Its opponents
may curse and wrangle a good deal among
themselves, but they are always solid on the
great cause of Proputty, and though they may
scream for a good many other diverse principles
the great principle of land and hard cash and
bricks and mortar Avill invariably find them
unanimous.
The Amalgamated Blackleg.
The cheap, unassuming blackleg, who enabled
the capitalist to squash the cause of Labour in
Melbourne on the understanding that he was to
receive eternal gratitude and constant employ-
ment, has been so completely thrown overboard
that he now proposes to form an organisation of,
his own, and announces his intention of
suppressing the thankless boss who has ejected
him into the gutter. In his capacity as a loose
and unattached groveller he finds that he is
only a thing to be kicked, and therefore he has
awakened to the necessity of being an amal-
gamated groveller instead. He does not intend
to become an ordinary unionist, however, for
bis only claim upon the capitalist is that he is
the enemy of unionism ; it is merely proposed
to form an “Association of Free Labourers : ”
and as his principles forbid him to be faithful
to any association, even his own, this league is
likely to prove an unattached combination of
loose atoms, and the devil will take the hind-
most. Moreover, it is impossible that he can
be a free labourer and yet a unionist, and
equally impossible that he can belong to his own
association and yet be a free labourer, and
therefore he seems to have abolished himself
and become nothing at all—not even a black-
leg. What liis precise status will be in the next
dispute remains to be seen. The old unions
decline to recognise him because he fought
against his own class and Avreeked his own
•ause for the sake of a mere temporary adA'an-
tage, and the masters refuse to acknowledge
him because they have won, and are in a posi-
tion to throw their used-up instrument away.
If his new organisation sends an ultimatum to
the employers, the brief response will be, that
the said organisation may go to Hades. Its
members are not wanted by either side, and if
they strike, nobody will get any more excited
over the event than if it was a strike of associ-
ated cannibals, who refused to pursue their
avocations unless they were furnished with more
succulent missionary. Possibly, some eight or
ten months hence, there may be another great
labour difficulty, and then —if the association of
“Free Labourers” is still in existence —it may
get a chance to go blacklegging in its individual
capacity, but if it attempts to act as a
corporate body, the floating labour of the
province will take its job, and it will be left
out in the cold. The amalgamated blackleg is
an anomalous individual with no fixed status.
He jumped the claim of the unionist, and is
surprised to find his own claim jumped in turn,
and he has to form a union as the only means
of asserting his position Us an unattached ‘ ‘ free
labourer ” who objects to unionism, and in his
capacity as an organised loose non-unionist and
a member of a disunited society in which every-
body scrambles for himself he complains of the
still more disunited blackleg who does to him
exactly what he did to everybody else. He is a
compound of contradictions a dismounted
marine horseman in the air—an individual who
rises up sitting in a horizontal attitude to unite
separately as a dissevered combination for the
suppression of unity ; and the one lesson to be
derived from him is that blacklegging is a
failure, as it always has been, and that the
capitalist only keeps his promises when it suits
him to do so—an event which happens at long
intervals and not always even then.

The first beginnings of Constitutional Govern-
ment in West Australia were celebrated in
Berth by the old, inevitable banquet, and one
or two ominous symptoms turned up in the
course of the feed. The guests cheered for the
Queen, as usual, but they cheered in a dull,
uninterested way as if they were cheering for
dry bread, or hash, or were applauding their
creditors, or performing some other act that
must be got through with difficulty. Also they
drank the health of the Governor in a solemn
style as if they were drinking cold tea to the
memory of their aged grandmother, and made
speeches about him with just as much enthusiasm
as if they were making speeches about the
necessity of putting on their clothes in the
morning. But when a member rose unexpectedly
lian Commonwealth, the applause that followed
and talked of the future destiny of the Austra-
nearly blew the roof off the premises. Even in
the slow-going West the alleged ‘ ‘ tie ” that binds
is evidently getting worn down veiy thin, and
the invisible “ bond” is a hazy thing of fluff and
uncertainty.

Melbourne Argus includes Australia among
“ the scattered branches of the English race,”
thus supplying an instance of a very common
misapplication of terms. Americans form as
much one of “the scattered branches of the
English race ” as do Australians, but we do not
suppose that even the magnificent ignorance
or wilful perversity of the Argus would apply
such a term to the people of the United
States with their ten millions of Germans, ten
millions of Irish, ten millions of niggers, and
the millions upon millions of French, Dutch,
Spanish, Italian and Polish people who are in-
corporated in the citizens of the roll of the
Great Republic. Further, when it is applied (as
it is done in the case quoted) to Australia as
something to be considered “ nationally,”
then the Argus might as well and
probably would—call India a “scattered
branch of the English race.” As a fact,
Australians are not English. A very respect-
able minority are anti-English. As one
instance, there are whole communities, both
in South Australia and in Queensland, whose
citizens are Germans first, Australian second,
and English not at all. A Queensland ex-
Premier once publicly accused a Teutonic colo-
nist of coquetting with the Fatherland to sell
Queensland to Germany. It was a burlesque
lie, of course, but the fact remains that such an
absurd statement should be possible with even
the most excitable of responsible politicians.
Australians are cosmopolitan as yet and omni-
absorbent. We have not yet developed the
full Australian type, because we are growing
and sucking in fresh pedigree sustenance all
round. But we deny that Australians are
English. The English deny it also. Ask
Toorak or Potts Point.
The Hawk.es Bay Herald (N.Z.), a newspaper
owned by a few land-monopolists has been going
into hysterics over the appalling iniquity of
the editor of an Auckland labour-organ, who
refused to pay back rent for an old office which
lie had occupied for a few weeks under a mis-
apprehension, thinking it was part of the
premises where his paper was printed, and that
it consequently belonged to it. The Herald
sees in this little incident the awful conse-
quences likely to result from the teachings of a
stormy Democrat should men like him be re-
turned to Parliament. A striking commentary
on the Herald’s remarks is to be found in the
bare fact that several of its own proprietors or
shareholders arc included in a list of 38 persons
in Hawkes Bay who are in illegal possession of
nearly a million acres of land for which they
nave been vainly attempting to obtain titles
from the colonial Parliament for several years
past. When they are called on to pay their
back rents there will be some big cheques
wanted.

Balfour, M.L.A., a cast-iron Christian with
an egg-shaped head, recently informed a
gathering of Melbourne pietists that “the
success of his countrymen is attributable to the
fact that the Bible is the spring of all their
actions.” In reply to which characteristic
snuffle an infidel might point out that the
Biblical definition of “success” is hardly the
same as James Balfour’s. It may be laid
down as a general rule, however, that the
Scotch in the bulk do use the Holy Book as a,
kind of spring-board, after having carefully
noted that the 12 fishermen of old made little
or no money in business. But one very impor-
tant reason why Balfour’s countrymen prosper
above the average is practically illustrated
every New Year’s Day. A pure-bred Scotch-
man can drink an Irishman or Englishman into
the gutter, without ever experiencing a desire
to throw his money about. Anyone may save
his saxpences by the un-Christian device of
swearing off it, but no other class of man in the
world can grow rich whilst he is steadily paint-
ing his nose. The Scotch are awful hard in the
upper storey.

Op one thing in connection with the Victorian
Premier Permanent cases Australia has reason
to be proud : the attitude and demeanour of
Chief Justice Higinbotham throughout the
lengthy trial.’* The clearness, fairness and
manliness of his Honor’s charge to the jury,
and the conspicuous way in which humanity
and strict justice were combined in the anti-
sentence remarks made to the convicted defen-
dants, form a model for all time to occupants
of the judicial bench. In this respect, at least,
the trial will certainly be “historical.”
A lady who wants the franchise badly sends
along to The Bulletin the old complaint that
manhood suffrage puts women of no matter how
exalted genius below the most illiterate boor ;
and as she finds this illogical, she calls on the
civilisation of the 19th Century to sweep the
barbarous system into the dust-bin of the past.
This is very fine, it is rather old ; and it just
illustrates one of the strongest arguments
against giving votes to women. A woman’s
mind is so constituted that she believes in the
possibility of finding a system that would not
be illogical, and she would always vote to upset
any that didn’t come , up to her standard. No
doubt our correspondent thinks that it would
be easy to draw up a plan of electoral repre-
sentation which would distribute voting power
in exact proportion to the fitness for it. It
would never occur to her that even her system
of female suffrage would make no distinction
between a woman of bad character and the wife
of a Minister of the Crown.
The collapse of the strike of railway employes
in Scotland was sufficiently explained by a
cable message—“ Engine-drivers are being lent
to the companies by the Midland Railway
Company.” It is only reasonable to suppose
that the Southerners were praying in sequestered
nooks and corners for the success of this hasty
Scotch attempt to get touch, however slight, of
an eight-hours system, yet at the bidding of a
bloated board of directors the men pocketed
their sympathies, and went off to perform what
was virtually “blackleg” labour. Probably
the Midland magnates netted sixty per shent
interest on the temporary loan of their easily-
driven drivers, or perhaps they obliged their
brethren for love, pure love, of the great cause
of Capital. The cable is silent on this point.
It merely says that the necessary loan was
floated, and until further particulars come to
hand, we can but remark that the English
engine-drivers might as well have been omnibus-
horses, placed by one company at the disposal
of another company that had happened to get
the glanders into their stables.
The vaccination dispute has received au
additional impetus by the arrival of the ship
Kelton at the Melbourne Heads with 31 souls
aboard, all of whom have had or still suffer
from small - pox. Dr. Elsnek, the chief
champion of the compulsory vaccinators, de-
clares that his position is confirmed by a refer-
ence to this especial ease, as each seaman had
been vaccinated, and only one man, i.e., 3*J 0
per cent., succumbed. It is a great pity that
the anti-vaccinators do not define their attitude
properly. No one doubts for a moment that
vaccination gives almost perfect immunity from
virulent small-pox. What the dissentients to
compulsory vaccination should stand or fall by
is this : That the operation causes a specific
disease known as variola, which malady is
rarely fatal to life, but which inevitably im-
pairs the constitution as a whole, and leaves the
patient unduly susceptible to all the diseases to
which the flesh is subject, with the one solitary
exception of small-pox. The question, there-
fore, summarized, is this : Is it worth while
debilitating the average national constitution in
consideration of the immunity against small-
pox gained, and are the numbers of indirect
diseases caused by variola of less account in
proportion ?

One of the most shamefully-coddled classes in
New South Wales is the junior barristers’— i.e.,
such of them as happen to be “fashionable”
and in the good graces of the Crown Law*
authorities. From all we hear, the Parlia-
mentary Draftsman, admittedly a clever officer
who, considering his abilities and his work gets
a very moderate salary indeed, has from time to
time thrust upon him the alleged “ services ” of
one or other of the army of well-tailored poor
creatures—chiefly imported—who under the
guise of practising at the “Baw” are in reality
the involuntary sucklings of a much-milked
State. To these youths are from time to
time given bills to *‘ draft. ” The Parliamentary
Draftsman smiles a bitter and cynical smile
when he hears that Mr. Slopechin (whose
professional experience has exclusively consisted
in moving probates and defending niggers on
Crown briefs) is to gel 300 guineas for drafting
a bill for the purpose of Suppressing Con-
structive Blasphemy on the part of Cock-eyed
Grasshoppers, but he can’t well protest, and
meekly, revises (which generally means re-
writing the whole of) Slopechin s bill. And
with what celerity is Mr. Slopechin’s huge
honorarium for the Strabismussed Insects Anti-
Blasphemy Bill rushed through as compared
with the cheque claimed by some poor devil
of a Government contractor up to his eyes in
bank-overdrafts !
There is trouble in (he Y.M.C.A. camp in
Melbourne. Certain imported members wrote
complaining that Secretary Southwell does
not sufficiently exert himself on their behalf,
and ignbres the interests 6f new* arrivals when
they come into conflict w ith those of the native
article. The secretary rejoin that no class-
distinctions are observed, and repudiates “a
predilection for mashers.” New comers bring-
ing letters of introduction from the old country
expect to be provided with situations right off
the reel. They arrive in large numbers and are
mainly composed of clerks and drapers’ assist-
ants, of which Victoria has more than enough
for its requirements already. There is no cool-
ness shown to these gentry, whose main com-
plaint is that the giving of f ree meals is unduly
limited. As a final retort, Mr. S. concludes
with the remark that ‘ ‘ there is a ring of hearti-
ness about the Association which makes every-
one feel at home if it is possible for him to do
so.” The whole affair throws a lurid light en
the functions and inner life of the tame-cat in-
stitution.
The proud boast of the new Victorian Govern-
ment that it will conduct its future railway
operations “on commercial principles” evokes
admiration among the moderately intelligent.
When a community, finding that its railroad is
not bringing in profits, “sacks” forty general
hands whose total salaries are below the pay
of a single commissioner, in order to “cut
down expenses,” it usually means that to save a
cash deficit in the balance-sheet comprehensive-
ness and efficiency of railway conveniences are
to be diminished. It is a strange philosophy of
economics that bases its raison d’etre on the
principle that to increase production the
number of producers must be decreased. It
would seem more reasonable that one man
drawing a big salary should receive his walking-
papers, than that hundreds of men should be
thrown on the unskilled labour market to
become a burden to that society which Govern-
ment presumably represents. The labour of
the porter being quite as exhaustive of tissue
as that of the commissioner or inspector, no
difference should be made in the remuneration
supplied, and until the State realises that the
true principle of economic working must be
prefaced by fairly proportioning the salaries of
the top employes to those of the bottom, their
false economy will provoke jeers and dis-
content. Just noAv it suggests the tactics of the
drunken man who thinks that by walking fast
he may deceive the bystanders into considering
him sober.

The resolution moved by Mr. Ha when, M.P.,
to the effect that the notorious carvings on the
fa§ade of Sydney General Post-office be not
removed except by the authority of Parliament,
having been carried by 54 votes to 5 in the
New South Wales Assembly, that body may
be taken to have approved by the majority
indicated of the unspeakable brutalities in
question. There are no words in the
English language, nay f there is no language
or combination of languages in which could be
adequately expressed the artistic degradation
of a country whose chosen i - epresentatives could
lend their countenance to such blood-curdling
atrocities. The carvings are to Australian
sculpture what the froggy - loggy - doggy verses
of their arch-apologist, Premier Sir Henry
Paekes, are to Australian poetry. There is
but one consolation in connection with these
hideous affronts to the spirit of civilisation :
there is nothing Australian about them. They
were'originally conceived by a Scotch gentle-
man in a haggis-nightmare, and they were
executed (which, in connection with Botany
Bay sculpture, is an excellent word) by an
Italian carver bearing the suggestive name of
Sant.

Premier Munro, of Victoria, has declared for
the principle of “ one man one vote,” and next
session will probably see the beginning of a
great battle between the old Conservatism of
Gillies and the newly-acquired Liberalism of
the Government. It is the first time for over
seven years that any Victorian Ministry has
possessed an idea or a policy, and but for the
restless versatility of Shiels, who keeps the
stagnant boss-capitalist of the Cabinet supplied
with thinking power, it is moi’e than probable
that Munro would never have known how to
commit himself either on this point or any
other. He is essentially a dreary individual of
commissions and percentages and advances on
good “shecurity,” and his idea of statesman-
ship consists chiefly in loafing, after Gillies's
fashion, in a comfortable chair, and drawing his
salary, and affably promising the whole earth
to every deputation that asks for it. But,
unhappily, Gillies, though he does nothing
while in office, and is constitutionally incapable
of drafting even a resolute policy concerning
the removal of a mud-punt, raises perpetual
Sheol while in Opposition unless his opponent
keeps in constant motion; and Munro, who
nearly died of old age in the frantic effort to
make Duncan do something, will now find it
difficult to get time to eat and sleep through
Duncan's frenzied demands that he should
bring forward at least one insurrectionary
proposal every day, and a deep, profound
and comprehensive scheme of reform three
times a week. The difference between this
old, smiling Conservative while at the
head of a department, and the same
individual at other times, is precisely that
between a flash of lightning and a pint of mud.
The grass and the green moss grow on Gillies
while in office, and he loafs along wearily like
an exhausted mass of fat and wretchedness, but
when some one else is in office he devours time
and space in his frantic desire to make the
other fellow abbreviate a straight line so as to
get at bis object. The new Ministry already
suffers from the fact that its opponent is
shoving violently behind, and as it must do
something, the abolition of plural-voting is as
good a thing to attempt as anything else. The
Munro Cabinet already feels that it is likely to
fall against something at an early date, and it
is a matter of comparative indifference which
hard substance it may concuss upon.

Mv Dear Moorarinda,-
At Sydney Criterion they evidently have an idea that
the eloping married female and the bold bad lover are a
suitable Xmas symptom, for last year at this time
“Fribnds” was the prevailing distress, and this year
“ Impulse ” is revealed behind the “ rag.” Maud William-
son made her Sydney debut in the limp r6le of Mrs. Mac-
donald, and isn’t so damp and repentant as the tearful
part permits, but she is placed in a contemptible light
from the commencement, and one really can have no
sympathy for a gasping creature who bestows her versatile
affections on a mounseer who so obviously paints his'
erotic nostrils a pale shrimp-pink. In the first act the
Williamson wears a mossy-green garment in velvet and
silk in the “ art ” style ; Enson—who is always Enson, but'
always lovely—in a neat gown of dove-grey with a white
vest. In the third act Miss Williamson, who is rather too
pokerish for draggy tea-frocks, and whose pull-the-st riug-
and-the-figure-sits-down poses are as sudden as a regi-
mental drill, comes forth in pale-ivory figured pongCe, the
full sleeves and front of pale-pink mull caught at the
waist with a brilliant buckle ; Enson wearing lobster-red
completely covered with soft grey chiffon and finished off
with black ribbon velvet—a strikingly Prenchy dress. In
the last act Williamson is in cream silk and mull flounces
that ought to be handed over to the laundress and the
crimping-iron, and Enson wears yellow and sage-green
as confidently as if we had never seen it before.
There was nothing very lovely in the d.c. on New Year’s
Eve. . A well-known doctor’s wife was there with a big
grey ostrich fan, and the sentimental angle of the latest
theatrical bride on to the starched bosom of her clean-
shaved Poppet suggested what a blissful honeymoon they
must .have had.
In a "paragraph chopped brief,” who can show in ink
relief the splendour, the riches, and pomp of “ Gin
derella?” All through the scintillating scenes everybody
has on a wedding garment—Vera Patey’s purple velvet
and satin, mauve silk and gold filagree and her black satin
and coral turnout with very slick black fleshings in the
last act: Lena Salinger’s flesh-pink and silver filagree and
lace suiting with the pale orange and silver thrill of the
other dandy, Sophie Harris, being especially worth notice.
Then we have pretty Aggie Kelton in white satin and
silver front, and long court train of silver brocade and
the glittering little shoelets, of course. Harry Benham,
as King Quartz—the villain of the trouble, who rushes in
and strikes a menacing attitude at virtue and goes away
crushed by.a fairy with a cockatoo profile in a stunning
rig-out—was a blinding sight with his marvellous golden
beard, and mantle of rich gold powdered material, and a
glittering vest to protect his “tuppenny” of golden
sequins ; really one ought to look at him through a piece
of smoked eclipse glass. The mythological “ transforma*
tions” are all beautiful, too—especially the concluding
one where the tense pose of the ascending Phoebus helps
the tableau amazingly—but I wonder why, in the third
change, does Charon, when he comes back from ferrying
Pandora over the Stygian river, assume the dejected
attitude of somebody hopelessly sea-sick ?
That was a whole-souled, forgiving, angelic sort of
bride aS.A. tnan took to be the guiding-star of his exist-
ence, in Adelaide, the other day. Hardly had they settled,
down after a blissful honeymoon than one fateful morning
there came a knock at their cottage door and a fine
healthy bantling was handed in, absolutely undressed, to
the care of the master of the house. Explanations were
deeply embarrassing, of course, for though most women
are willing to forgive past “ wild oats ” on the part of their
Petruchio when these cereals are but vaguely alluded to,
still a tangible “wild oat” weighing nine pounds handed
carefully in through the main door when it is least ex-
pected materially alters the complexion of things. Not
so, however, with this bride. Instead of sending the un-
welcome brat hurtling out on its bald head into the
gutter, talking shrilly for two hours and going hack to
her mother, she accepted the infant at once as one of the
family, placed it in a silk-lined cot, and said no more
about it! After all, women are ever so much more for-
giving than men. I wonder what he would have done if
three fat babies in a bassinette had presented themselves
at his front door three weeks after marriage and she had
confusedly confessed to there being romantic incidents in
her pre-nuptial career. Yes, I wonder if he would have
given them nursery-room?
Which reminds me, inversely, of an incident that once
happened at Manly, when two personages (then a second
Damon and Pythias, but afterwards to squabble over the
Pickwickian glory of being the originator of the Soudan
donkey expedition) were saved from dire dilemmas by
a marvel of a cook. At 6 p.m. Damon rushed in upon
Pythias, gasping: “My dear boy, I am simply on the
borderland of insanity ! I’ve actually invited six people
to dine with me to-night at seven o’clock, and then
forgotten all about it! It is now six o’clock. Suggest
something anything, for Heaven’s sake.” “1 can’t
suggest anything,” said Pythias, “ but 1 can lend you my
cook—if she’ll go to your house.” In a second cookie was
run up from the depths “ What have you in the house?”
was her first professional enquiry of the distracted host,
who Gushingly confessed that the ruins of a goose and
some milk were all there were to go upon. The female
Savarin smiled confidently, and said they might leave the
tiling to her. Then she went forth and bought within
ten minutes a heap of raspberry-tarts, some oysters, and
a fish. She concocted delicious oyster-soup within a
quarter of an hour more, scooped tiie jam out of the tarts
ami in vie them into oysler-patds for a second course;
cim ied l ie fish in some tricky way, until it smelt like the
Exchange at mid-day ; the goose-wreck she conjured into
toothsome entrees. She had dinner quietly on (lie table
three quarters of afrdinnr from warning, too, and the way
in which the dishes emptied proved the entire success of
her culinary jugglery. No wonder that—well, her master
is dead now, to the great grief of everyone who ever set
foot inside his house, and he used to tell this story himself
to lots of people—no wonder, I say, that when the cook
and he finally parted, he, after a vain endeavour to
re-engage her, “kissed her on both cheeks and gave her
his benediction.”
I read that they have just “ made a minnit,” or set up
a bye-law, or something, somewhere in Norway to the
effect that no fair Norwegian shall be united to the man
of her choice until she is “proficient in the arts of spin-
ning, knitting, and baking.” They ought to adopt that
imple little regulation throughout Australia. In my
mind’s eye, Horatio, I see the member who foster-fathers
the motion wept over and called blessed by a throng
coltou-socked, undarned, buttonless, and bilious Bene,
diets, mute sufferers from the fact that the cold-s vied
local " has merely to state she is sixteen to be
eligible to lay waste the wardrobe and ducks and
drakes of the digestive apparatus of the man who rashly
puts his trust in her.
People are wondering whether the youngest member of
an irrepressible Sydney family will behave as frantically
over the arrival of the “ Jarsey ” as she did over the land-
ing of the Carington? On that dim historic day she
rushed down to the wharf hearing a huge bouquet with
fluttering streamers to present to the lady, but-finding
that she could not squeeze up close enough personally to
offer the flowers, she galloped up to Government House
in a cab, stampeded past the astonished menials up to
Lady C.’s room, and dumped the bookay down on the
vice-regal bed. As mamma frequently avows, “her darling
child is so enthusiastic over everything,” so perhaps she
will go through the same performance for the benefit of
Lady J.
With a note of pained enquiry running through her
dulcet voice, yours truly rises to ask how it comes that a
troop of presumably rational girls are ever persuaded to
play cricket in public? The “Sydney ” and “ Probable ”
teams which met on Sydney Ass. Ground on
Boxing-day and the day following were a sorry sight
from their sunburnt cheeks to their heelless shoes, and as
they swaggered (doesn’t a woman just wag her back
draperies when she thinks she is mannish !) out into the
shimmering heat at the hot hour of noon each day one
couldn't help wondering where the fun of the thing
came in. And the attitudes ! The pose of one energetic
she-fielder suggested the “honey-pots” of childhood
with the suggestion of the last position of a leap-frog
back, and the way the batswomen careered from Dan to
Beersheeba drove home the truth of the saying that
women can’t run—“ Women and cows,” once wrote Mark
Twain (or was it Josh Billings?) “shouldn't run —they
can’t.” One cricketeress in (I think) navy-blue made a
very decent score of 195 on the first day, and 101 on the
second, and as she cleft the air, bat in hand and half
a yard of stocking showing at every convulsion, one felt
that Australians are evidently not all born tired. The
“Probables” won with 239 runs, and there were only
seven goose-eggs made on both days. The attendance
on the last day was very good, but society didn’t air its
summer complexion in the ladies' reserve, and the only
decent frock was a lilac delaine peppered with pansies
and topped with a string hat trimmed with heartsease.
Female cricket isn’t going to be fashionable.
The next she-tournament will probably be a “ladies’
billiard-match,” but as it has been on the cards for several
months the promoters may yet repent in season. I hope
they will, for the “lady players’” sakes. How could a
short fat woman be dignified when making a spread-eagled
dive at her ivory lying high and dry in the exact centre of
a full-sized billiard table ?
Several Australian musical professionals want to know
what a certain somewhat “literary ” lady meant by rush-
ing behind at a recent benefit concert and loudly lectur-
ing the performers, one and all, who weren’t too indifferent
to talk back. She nagged them for taking encores, said
she had no inclination to sit there all night j she quarrelled
over the length and quality of the progamme, and she
lectured a leading fiddler as if she were his mother-in-law
for giving an encore piece. Indeed, her angry shriek was
audible in the front seats, and sounded as if the soprano
and contralto were at last “ having it out.”
In reference to the steady tide of English professional
mediocrities who are capering about Australia while
hungry native ability sits twiddling its thumbs, a leading
Australian actress once said in reply to my puzzled query
why this was so;—“ This is how it is. Lots of managers
think the salaries they pay too enormous altogether, and
so they want to bring the price down by creating what
brokers call an ‘ artificial glut.’ When this market is
crowded out down go the salaries as they have done in
London.” “And what will become of the imported
sticks?” said I. But the leading actress simply shrugged
her shoulders and asked me as if I took sugar in my tea.

Has “ Bona Fide ” had her young affections blasted else-

evening in the small back parlour with the gas turned
down, and after all went off and married a soap-boiler's
fat widow? Or is it merely she doesn’t wish to jolt her
maiden meditations at the breakfast-table by the sight o
a row of fierce, hungry, commonplace men seizing all the
“ aigs” and bacon before she can wave a fork? “Bona
Fide” will probably be a long time hitting the exact
mark, unless the extreme suitability of a convent sud-
denly occurs to her.
The eighth annual meeting of the Sydney Nurses’Home
people occurred recently, with the Lieutenant-Governor
in the chair and the usual convoy of blushing philanthro-
pists in the front row, including Mrs. Gurney, who
tendered her resignation as hon. sec., as she shortly goes
abroad with Professor Gurney, In the submitted report
it transpired that the Home is looking rather blank just
now—more nurses leaving its tram-haunted precincts
than apply for admittance, and, as the Board must have
thirty nurses to build a dividend on, it was announced
that “ they had decided to try a system of profit-sharing,
which would give each nurse a pecuniary interest,” &c.,
&0., and the idea was received with considerable rapture
by the Sister Doras present. The Home won't have to
advertise any more for those thirty inmates, but I hope
the cloaked and veiled sisterhood have an eye to the
wordin') of the bond. “My dear,” says the good, kind
Board, “owing to an unsuspected juiiip in pork and -an
unforeseen rise in butter, combined with a strike on the
part of the laundresses for a further penny per dozen, wo
have to regretfully announce a slight deficit.”

Says “ nursey ” with a frown,
“And now I am a-goin’
To read as you lie down.
Its title is ‘The Wattle,
The Stockman, and his Grave.’
(There’s one draught in the bottle
1 think I’d better save.”)

But when she finished reading
(This was the last sad line :
“ The patient gums keep seeding
Where the curlew doth repine ”)
She turned congratulatient—(new word)
To tire sufferer at her side,
But that pome-martyred patient
Had curled him up and died !

How does your constitution bear up
against the pantomimes? Badly? Then take
Marshall’s Tonic Nervine.
It is alleged that bank-manager Bennett
leaves behind him a shortage of £13,000. The
detectives say he did not escape to ’Frisco, and is
still in Australia.
Someone in a position to do so should
institute a searching enquiry into the reasons why
Mary Curran was recently released from gaol.
Last November she and her daughter were charged
with being accessories to an indecent assault com-
mitted by a Chinaman named Ping Song on a
child eleven years of age. Judge Windeyer, in
sentencing the female prisoners to five years’ im-
prisonment each, said that “ their crime was an
infamous one. For a few shillings they
had Consented to sell this child. Tile elder
prisoner, Mrs. Curran, who was a mother and an
Englishwoman, should have been the first to pro-
tect the child, instead of which she not only con-
sented to the infamous proposal made by her
daughter, but did all in her power to enable it to
be carried out.” At the time of the trial rumour
hinted that a certain relation existed between (lie
elder woman and a prominent Civil servant. His
photograph was found in her house, and many
other circumstances seqined to point to his intern t
in herself and family.

“What is the dance, Mister?” said a
hayseed who leaned over the back of the Sydney
Royal circle while the ballet shivered, jelly-
fashion, on fat fantastic feet. “That,” said
we, judicially, “ is the cow-heel gavotte,” and he
was satisfied. As a matter of fact, J. Wilkinson
is the only one in the pantomime who displays
the slightest idea of dancing, and he, despite the
presence of Messrs. Greville, Forde and Boulton,
seems to be the only one who gets much fun out
of his part. This pantomime is also particularly
weak on the feminine side. Aggie Kelton has
little idea of handling Cindei’ella, and the others
are equally dotty. What are the Firm thinking
about? Here are 'the new importations—the
Misses Carew and Galbraith, both of whom, we’re
told, can sing and dance —lying idle, while George
Leitch has nothing to but pose as a block orna-
ment. The management announce an early
withdrawal of “ Cinderella ” in favour of Grattan
Riggs, who opens on January 31, probably in
“ Arrah-na-Pogue,” also underlined at the other
house.
*& * *
George Rignold is so enamoured of the
profits of pantomime that he has already put for-
ward “Babes of the Wood” as the bill for next
year, but whether he puts up that or any other
pantomime unless he has a little Maggie Moore in
it, we should want at least 80 per cent, of the
gross to stand in. Judicious excisions make
“ Dick Whittington ” go much easier, but we
think if Mr. Ayrton had a little less “fat,” and
Messrs. Hoyte, Tolano, Sweeney, and Harcourt
had a little more, things would hum more con-
sistently. By the way, Ayrton and Harcourt
lose a big show in the postal-pillar scene. Ayrton
might affect deafness and Harcourt could then
drop pink notes into his internals, or he might
open a glass .door in his side and ring him up by
telephone, or, drop pennies in his slots for taffy
and cigarettes. Instead of which they stand
round and talk vulgar drivel. A matinee per-
formance will be given on the 14th, and before the
season’s close the impecunious little ones of
Sydney will take their annual treat. When
“Dick” is wound up, date unfixed, “Arrah-ha-
Pogue ” will be produced, with Maggie Moore as
Arrah, and George Rignold as Shaun. Dion
Boucicault lends original MS. and music, and
may, also, appear in the cast.
** » »
“Moths” has drawn good houses at
Sydney Garrick, but nothing like the business
such a good show deserves. A second visit con-
vinced us that Olga Nethersole’s voice is responsive-
to every touch of feeling, and not the mere two or
three stringed instrument that the nervousness of
first night led us to believe. The lady’s chief
fault is her inclination to gasp. That’s a fatal;
habit; anyhow, one female gasper is enough in a
city. Laurence Cautley has a manner of treating
his terminal sounds which is particularly annoy-
ing, and the extra chew he gives his s’s is
neuralgia to us. Says the horse-reporter on a
contemporary : —“ Miss Lyons tries hard to appear
natural as Fuschia Leach, but the American;
accent is not appropriate to her style nor to the-
part. ”
A ton of ignorance is a dangerous thing,
And drinking deep makes dunces drivel sling. ,
“ Middleman,” the strong play by H. A. Jones, mi
which Willard made such a hit as the inventor
during its lengthy run at London Shaftesbury,
will be put up on Saturday next. Of course Chas..
Cartwright is the Blenkarn ; Harold Russell (first.
Sydney appearance) plays Sir Seton ; Hans Phil-
lips, Jesse Pegg ; Bill Leake, Bully Todd ; Mary
and Nancy, Olga Nethersole and Nellie Lyons
Mrs. Chandler, Jenny Watt-Tanner ; Maud!
Chandler, Alice Deorwyn, and last but not least,.
Harry Jewett, to fill an interregnum, plays a small!
part, Captain Chandler. One likes the new folk
at this house all the better because they do note
pose as stars and have made no attempt to earwig;
the press. This reminds us that we saw a Sunday
paper man once coming up the Royal stairs slob-
bering over his grimy paw. “ Sore hand ? ” said'
we, interrogatively. “No,” said he ecstactically
between kisses, “she squeezed it.” And we went
down thinking how much more appropriate ai
squeeze from a strait waistcoat would have been.
** * *
“ Impulse,” a weak play in the original,
has been further emasculated in English out
of compliment to “the bud.” The whole
plot of the piece turns on Mrs. Macdonald’s
(M iss Maud Williamson) exclamation, regard-
ing the arrival of her husband ; “ I dare not
see him ; take me away,” an acknowledgement of
her guilt with de Riel, and yet, afterwards, all
reference to this family unpleasantness is carefully
cut out. This straining at gnats weakens several
not over-strong situations at a later period.
“Harvest,” a not over-strong, but highly original,
play by H. H. Hamilton, in which Dot Boucicault
will make his re-appearance, will be put up on
Saturday. Scenery will be its trump-card.
At Madame Patey’s farewell Sydney con-
cert the Town Hall was nicely filled, but badly-
lit, and vocal chestnuts were very much in season.
By the way,'we would advise Mayor Burdekim
to provide the gallery with seats whose backs'
would prevent boors resting their feet on the-
spines of those who sit beneath them. The weak-
ness and bad enunciation of Patey’s vocal support
were never more in evidence, and Mr. Allpress was
so dissatisfied with the accompaniment to his first
item that he shirked the second. Perhaps the
accompanist had also cause to complain. Mr.
and Mrs. Patey, accompanied by Bertha Rossow,,
Jack Lemmone, and accompanist Morris, have
sailed for China and Japan. On return they play
in Queensland and Maoriland first.

reveal.

Johnny Toole, who did not do good biz-
in Newcastle, has since played Parramatta, and is
now doing the three show towns on the way to
Melbourne. He then does Ballarat, Sandhurst
and Geelong, and a short season in Melbourne
and Adelaide, where he will be picked up by the
Victoria, en route for London. John Billington
went ahead in the Britannia to get the theatre
ready.
** * *
“Dandy Dick,” the frisky, thoroughbred
comedy that convulsed Sydney three months ago,
has been making the pace warm at Melbourne
Bijou since Boxing Night. The sternest stickler
for turf truth can find little to grumble at in the
story of “Dandy,” which hangs well together and
glitters with unconventional jokes. To the un-
thinking it may seem something of a pity that the
gaitered apostle doesn’t win his “monkey ” on the
“dead bird” after, all, yet Pinero doubtless
ordered all things for the best. He foresaw the
possibility of Anson gambling away his immortal
soul if that first essay at “ punting ” had not been
rendered abortive by the providential idiocy of the
family butler. Intoxicated with success the Dean
might have presently dropped the New Organ
Fund over a “certainty” that got “pipped on
the post,” or taken a short price about a “ stiff
’un ” with his next Hospital Sunday collection.
Under present conditions the moral that the
children of this world -as represented by sweet
George Tid and her loving baronet—are a
heap “downier” that the children of light
is driven right home by “Dandy Dick.” It like-
wise embodies a solemn warning to cooks who
woo carrotty-headed policemen with cold potatoes,
and is the incidental means of teaching us that
the horse is a nobler animal than an army-officer.
Miss Emma Temple, as one of the" Dean’s
daughters, is the only change from the original
B. &B. cast. Anson’s ripe performance possesses
special local interest, in so far that he makes up
outwardly on the lines of Dr. Bevan, although he
must despair of emulating that good shepherd in
the matter of spiritual grace. Brough makes a
welcome re-appearance, after quite a long absence,
as the muddled myrmidon of the law, and if Mrs.
Brough seems a trifle too fresh and charming for
a real “Daisy of the Turf.” she errs on the right
side. “ Dandy Dick ” will continue to run for the
Melbourne money, and romp in every evening
until further notice.
** * *
“Aladdin,” at Melbourne Royal, is more
or less of the pantomime seen in Sydney last
Christmas, and exchanged for “Cinderella” this.
Money has been expended upon the present pro-
duction with a liberal yet discriminating hand.
The public get as much splendour as they want,
but there is no wa.ste, and the Transformation
Scene comes to a mild artistic conclusion at the
very point where it is expected to begin. There
is a strange absence of gurgling fountains and
gushing fairies from this portion of the show, the
flying angels of ordinary pantomime life being re-
presented by painted figures upon which the
average old gentleman finds a difficulty in feasting
his filmy eye. The literary source of an
Australian “ annual ” was ever a mystery,
although the necessary localising is commonly
reported to be slung in promiscuously by a scene-
shifter, or somebody, who gets paid ten shillings
overtime for the job, Topical allusions without
the least suspicion of “snap ” in them, and songs
destitute of point or humour’, make “Aladdin”
hard to bear at times, but many of the lines—
probably by a man who is dead and can’t charge
for them —are unusually neat and rhythmical. So
is a well-appointed Fan Ballet, which supplies
Miss Mary Weir with another opportunity to
show how much she benefited by the example of the
Gaiety girls. Collectively considered, however,
“Aladdin” might have been given a better chance
to keep the Royal crowded after the holidays
are over and audiences have ceased to roll up
without fetching. Only George Walton, the
Widow Twankey, whose grotesque dancing and
fooling never, or hardly ever, grow wearisome,
seems to make anything of a hit. Jennie Lee and
Royce are merely at home in their very ordinary
business, whilst most of the others are chiefly re-
markable for a lack of “go.” It is worthy of
remark, though, that Miss Florence Young
appears happier and less constrained in tights than
when she wore skirts and wrestled with witty
dialogue in “The Gondoliers.”
** * *
Owing, perhaps, to the misfortune that
it is neither a comic opera nor a panto-
mime, “ Chilperic,” which failed to boom in
Sydney, is doing little better at Melbourne
Opera House. All the same, the management has
put forth a splendid effort to carry the town by
storm with Hervb’s extravaganza, and in artistic
excellence of production this glittering show holds
its own with the first setting-up of “Paul
Jones.” Maybe the strength of the com-
pany occasionally gives “ Chilperic ” away, inas-
much as that anyone who fully appreciates the
perfection of the quarrel-scene between Woodfield
and Nellie Stewart must weep anon over the
miserable foolery of Fredegonde’s departure from
Court, accompanied by a pile of bonnet-boxes on
a hand-cart. This is an irritating incident, which
goes far towards spoiling the charm of a perform-
ance that is bright and polished in the main,
assisted by a crowd of popular favourites dis-
played to tine advantage in their respective parts,
scenery, dresses, and legs of the best, and an
orchestra which deserves even the extravagant
praise heaped upon it by the dailies. There must,
be something seriously amiss with
“ Chilperic ” to account for the want of heartiness
in its reception. fAudiences don’t always trouble
to applaud items of undeniable merit. The subtle
sweetness of “The Bogie Man,” now worked into
the first act as “ The Druid Man,” and admirably
done by Snazelle, orchestra, and chorus, has been
very tamely received up to date. Probably the
Melbourne people are suppressing their hysterics
until the Gaiety Company come along with their
version of this droll song and dance.
** * *
“ The Phantom Ship,” at Melbourne
Alexandra, is a nightmare too disjointed and
gruesome to be reverently treated within the
limits of a paragraph. In reviving this alleged
supernatural drama (which is stated to have first
shaken its gory Jocks at Sydney), Dampier lias
made so many allowances for the festive season
that the non-appearance of a clown with a red-hot
poker causes a wave of mild regret to sweep
across the shattered understanding. Everyone
expects the grand final transformation scene to
dissolve into a butcher’s shop, where the Flying
Dutchman would be found driving a brisk
trade in wooden legs of mutton, but no-
thing of the sort occurs. Yet the drona.
abounds in Christinas joys so far as it goes. A
first-rate comic Baron, attired in full armour,
prowls around the streets of Amsterdam in pursuit
of Miss Lily Dampier, who inflames the warrior’s
tin-clad soul by persistently wearing the garb of a
Moorish maiden. A weird, mysterious gnome,
vaguely understood to be the primitive Dutch
notion of Nicodemus, appears to pursue the
wicked Baron except when he is spi-inging upon
Lily, but there is not much sense to be made of
this person anyhow. Dampier lias a pretty bad
time of it, popping up on icebergs and things and
discussing his awful doom in cold, impassive
tones, but in the end he seems to come out all
right through his son dying, and the Baron being
boofed off the old gnome. Take it altogether,
“The Phantom Ship” is a thrilling bit of fun,
with sundry flashes of song and dance thrown in
by way of adding serious interest to the tout
ensemble.
** * *
Meanwhile “ Marjorie,” pulled together
since the opening night, shows a disposition to
grow on ’em at Melbourne Princess’, and business,
after looking sickly on several previous occasions,
was immense on Saturday night. The solid
splendour of this old-Englishy ballad opera
received its due meed of gush in last issue. One
can find nothing fresh to say about the perform-
ance except that the orchestra, under Mr. Leon
Caron, goes occasionally mad and drowns the
vocalists, especially the baritone, in torrents of
brazen fury. The big drummer, too, would be
improved by dropping dead every now and then.
** * *
Matters are fairly lively at all three halls.
Frank Clark has got Perry and Lulu Ryan again
at the Gaiety, the U.S. niggers work hard at the
Victoria, while Harry Rickards’ farces and some
of his songs are new to Melbourne people, some of
whom are astounded to discover ’Arry’s capacity
for keeping a farce on the move without the
extraneous assistance of a roi'ty chorus. The
Cyclorama and other matters—such as the Exhi-
bition alligator—are raking in holiday returns,
but the Easting Man, having finished his 40 days
without degenerating into a ghastly spectacle,
is still on view at the Waxworks for the
moral benefit of all who take delight in
seeing a human being
take soup. Weather has
favoured Dr. Carver’s
“ Wild West Show,” at
the Friendly Societies
Gardens, and apart from
the buck-jumping—which
is suggestive of inferior
coals brought to New-
castle —this open-air en-
tertainment seems to
make a favourable im-
pression. Hopetoun has
given it his august
approval, and the man-
agement fires off a cheer-
ful conjecture that the
Sioux Indians engaged
have lost several relatives
and friends by the recent
slaughter in America.
This is a crafty advertise-
ment, because people na-
turally hanker to behold
any noble savage whose
uncle, for all they know
to thecontrary, may have
been recently buried in
another hemisphere.
