A person named Sandow, who exhibits
himself in London as “the strongest man on
earth,” has been fined £4 12s. Gd. for damage to
property. He took a respectable _ four-floor
lodging, and spent his spare time practising with
a pair of dumb-bells which weighed 3201b5., but
one unlucky day he dropped them on the floor,
and they crashed right through into the dining-
room and flattened out the dinner and nearly
brained all the other inhabitants.
** * *
That interesting gentleman, Rev. Andrew
Gardiner, takes The Bulletin’s advice about
going on the lecture-platform a little late in the
day, for the bloom of his little affaire du cceur has
been rubbed off, and the first fragrance of his
Divorce Court reputation has evaporated. His
best show would be to transgress again and then
repent profoundly in public. What collections
there would be, to be sure !

The Melbourne Cyclorama people are set-
ting to work with their new cyclorama of the
Eureka Stockade Battle, which will do a lot for
the Australian Republican cause. Meanwhile the
Crucifixion Cyclorama is shown in Adelaide, and
Gettysburg will be moved to Melbourne, Waterloo
to Sydney.

TO Employers. —The Federated Employers Council
have a List of Good, Reliable Men, who have served them
during the Strike ; amongst them are Carpenters, Stone-
masons, Gardeners, Farm Labourers, Carters and Black-
smiths. Anyone wishing to engage such labour is
requested to apply to the undersigned. None but men
who have proved their reliability will be recommended.
Jas. Bhinsden, General Secretary.
This, we presume, is all that is left of the noble
free labourer, who “stood by” his employer All
through the late strike, but who now finds that
the noble employer aforesaid has gone back on
him when he can get better men from the Unionist
ranks.
At Poverty Point.
There is no word in English quite to correspond with
the French Taf, which means a peculiar stage-fright
attacking experienced actors and actresses before their
first appearance for the evening. The best performers
are the most subject to it. Lemaitre would drink a
bottle of Burgundy “ to drown the Taf.”
A correspondent clears up about Chas. Mathews, now a
prosperous London barrister, and the adopted son of old
Charley Mathews, the comedian, who was twice married,
first to the gay Vestris, secondly to Mrs. Davenport,
divorced wife of an American comedian, and she yet sur-
vives. The proper name of Mathews, the barrister, is
Davenport, son of Mrs. Mathews by her first husband,
who gave poor Charley such an unmerciful beating in the
street in New York ; but yet Charley loved the boy. The
comedian has been dead 12 years. His widow yet
decorates his grave, in Kensal Green Cemetery, with
flowers, and she also takes a pride in decorating the
grave of her predecessor, Madame Vestris, dead over SO
years.
Ada Cavendish, who was said to he dying a year or two
ago, has just made her re-appearance on the London stage
as Mercy Merrick. She “created” the character.
A Texas journalist named Keith has a beard seven feet
long. He declines to go into the show business, though he
might make a fortune eating soup in public.
Beerbohm Tree has been saying that the proportion of
good plays to bad in those he reads is as one to one
hundred and fifty, and that the art of writing good plays
depends upon a technical acquaintance with the working
of the stage. But it is not unlikely that should other
managers go through the same lot of plays, they might
select several plays and ignore that selected by Mr. Tree.
Then, if stagecraft be the only essential, how is it that so
few actors are play-weights? What about the dozens of
good plays written by men who have scarcely stepped on
the boards ? What about the failures put together by the
stage-experienced ? As a matter of fact, stage knowledge
is useful to a play-wright, but is by no means indis-
pensable. A good manager can soon straighten out kinks
at rehearsal.

A brief mention that Nellie Leamar, of the London
music-halls, had married a son of the Earl of Feversham
appeared in last issue. The glad tidings were not news,
but as seven long years had elapsed since Nellie’s
nuptials were first announced in The Bulletin, it seemed
just about time to announce them again. The sisters
Leamar were the originals of the Sisters Bilton, and con-
sidering that one girl from each pair is now safely landed
amongst the old nobility, the advisability of such maidens
hunting in couples is quite obvious.
Painful surprise in Melbourne show circles over the
death of R. W. Cary, known as New Zealand Cary, not to
be confounded with W. G. Carey, the tragedian, or G. P.
Carey, the comedian. Little R. W. Cary gave up a pros-
perous news-agency in Richmond (Melb.) to embark on
precarious theatricals, after success as a penny-reading
manager. His baritone singing in New Zealand got him
some fame, and he even took parts like Mourzouk in
“ Girofle-Girofla.” He was luckless of late.
A bit of American swindling creeps up in Melbourne
show business. One travelling combination trading on
the names of eminent performers in America, and one of
the Melbourne music-halls puts out six character por-
traits of a favourite comique who has gone to Europe.
Everyone remembers that cantankerous little tenor*
Dimitresco, the Roumanian, who was here with Simon-
sen’s last company, his wife being the attractive contralto
Madame Lablaohe, who sang “ Carmen”,so well. Dimi-
tresco left the stage in the middle of the last scene of
“Carmen,” at Melbourne Opera House, through a dis-
agreement as to tempo with Lombardi, the conductor,
and he played a like prank in Sydney, but such tricks
seem to be concomitants of getting on, for Dimitresco is
now a star of the London Italian Opera, Covent Garden,
under Signor de Lago.
Amazing is the way your Sims, Pettitt, Buchanan,
Gilbert, Grundy, Pinero pile it on. Compare them with
the last generation of* dramatists. The tariff when
Douglas Jerrold wrote for the Mirror was thirty shillings
for a complete play ! Davidge was one of these pay-
masters. At nine o’clock one evening Jerrold was told
Davidge had just died. “Impossible!” said Jerrold.
“ He couldn’t die before the half-time money came in.”
(At and after half-past nine in many British theatres
half-price is charged (or admission.)

“ Cut-throat” is the game play'ed by certain managers
at this festive season. Most of the theatres are over-
manned. Each entrepreneur lias snapped-up all the
available people, fearing rivals should make profitable
use of them.
Miss Wallis, manageress of the Shaftesbury Theatre
(London), recently appealed to her audience for advice as
to continuing Buchanan’s new play, “The Sixth Com-
mandment.” When she remarked that she had not had
good houses during the week, a gallery voice yelled,
“No, but we hope to Gawd yer will have”—a wish that
wasn’t fulfilled, by the way.
Poor “old” Johnny Brian, whose “Dancing Mad”
song used to be so popular at (he London music-halls in
days of yore, has gone over lo the majority. Johnny
took “Dancing Mad” as far ns Constantinople in his
younger days, and some nine years ago lie performed
with one of the Raynors, the two brethren of that ilk
having quarrelled and parted company for a while. Many
Bulletin readers must renumber Brian’s lean legs—or
perhaps they cherish fonder recollections of Ms wife’s
Amazonian understandings.

According to the daily papers, Postmaster-
General O’Connor, backed up by Premier Parkes,
refuses to obey the injunction granted by the
N.S.W. Supreme Court restraining him from stop-
ping the passage through the post of a certain
newspaper declared by a stipendiary magistrate to
be obscene, until the publishers’ appeal be decided
by the Full Court. Defying the authority of the
Supreme Court appears of late to have become
quite a favourite game in high official quarters.
When once an open and formal challenge shall
have been made The Bulletin hopes that Chief
Justice Darley will have the necessary spine to
accept it by issuing warrants and [clapping into
gaol those alleged guardians of public morality
who claim to be above the law.
The lugubrious fiend who relapses once
per annum into long, piteous wails concerning
‘ ‘ the dying year, ” seemed to be taken rather worse
than usual on Wednesday of last week. He
rolled the old familiar “dirge,” or “lament,” or
“retrospect ” off his chest with exceptional vigour,
and its melancholy rumble resounded through the
cavernous gloom of the Australian Press like a
consumptive cough in a catacomb. He began, as
a rule, by groaning “The Year is Dying Fast,”
and then the wretch went on to gloat over its rapid
dissolution as though he were an undertaker.
Sometimes he stai-ted off with the gruesome chest-
nut “Hark! ’Tis Midnight!” He occasionally
varied the rnontony of his croak by letting us
know that “The Clock Strikes Twelve,” but he
was a mournful miscreant in any ease. It is about
time that editors left off encouraging the bard
who rhymes year with bier, drear, or tear, and
exhorts us to consign it to the sepulchre of the
past. He has been hanging like a pall over every
31st December since time was, and when the
present year dies we shall hope to see the would-
be warbler of a requiem wrapped in a winding
sheet of his own miserable screed, and buried with
the corpse of that defunct twelvemonth.

The Land of the Crocodile.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
One of the men employed in Brisbane Botanic
Gardens was lately instructed by the Curator to
clean out a pond full of lilies and dirty water.
The person employed in this aquatic scavenging is
a Teutonic gentleman who has not made lethyo-
logy a special study. Consequently, when he dis-
covered four fishes of somewhat remarkable aspect
his first impulse was to cook and eat them. He is
probably related to that German colonist named
Meyers who turned back from Leichhardt’s second
expedition impelled by the paralysing dread that
he would not be able to obtain sufficient grub !
The Curator, however, was just in time to rescue
three of the fish from the ravenous Teutonic man,
and at once forwarded a couple, to DeVis, the
able Museum Curator, who recognised them
as “Burnett Salmon,” or Ceratodus Fosteri,
one of the most interesting life forms on this
planet. This extraordinary fish is only found in-
the Mary and Burnett Rivers on the East Coast
of Queensland, and nowhere else in this world.
The mystery of how these four came into the
Botanic Gardens is thus explained. About 18 or
20 years ago a German settler on the Upper Mary
River (named after Lady Fitzroy) caught several
Ceratodi in a net and took them down to Mary-
borough where the late Percy Ramsay purchased
the lot and was then advised by R, 13. Sheridan
to send them on to the Brisbane and Sydney
Gardens:. Four were sent to Brisbane and placed
in a Botanic Garden pond where they were for-
gotten for nearly 20 years until discovered by the
aforesaid Teutonic gourmand. They weigh now
from five to eight pounds each. The two sent to
the Museum were sent back at once to the
Gardens, where they are now living with their
third mate in a fresh pond near the duck-
lagoon. This Ceratodus represents one of
the earliest forms of life on this earth. He is
identical with the Ganoid fishes of the Triassic
and Jurassic Periods. His ancestors were con-
temporary with the Efepkas Prim-igcnius, with the
Megaceros, with the cave tiger, with the Icth/jo-
saurus, the Plesiosaurus, and the great leather-
winged carnivorous bat, the Pterodactyl , whose
fossils are found in the limestone of the Mesozoic.
This wonderful fish possesses not only the ordinary
breathing-gill of all fishes, but the lung of the air-
breathing animals. He is the connecting-link
between the cold-blooded fishes and the warm-
blooded mammalia. The scientific world first
received news of Ceratodus with the derisive
incredulity that greeted the announcement of the
platypus. The one had ceased to exist for 200,000
years and the other was an impossible absurdity !
The Ceratodus is, when full-grown, about 30 inches
long, and weighs from 81bs. to 121bs. His scales
are large, like those of the Barramundi of
the Dawson, but without the red spots
of the Dawson fish. They belong to totally
distinct species, though often confounded by
people who don’t know one from the other. The
sight of a live Ceratodus inspires me with the
same feeling that would arise if I took a walk
in the forest and met a Mastodon, or a Tichorine
Rhinoceros, or fell over a sick Pterodactyl. This
fish shares with the present Aurochs of the
Lithuanian forests, Bos/ongifrons, the entire
honour of representing life forms separated from
us by geological ages. Always, of course,
excepting Man, who dates back to the unicellular
Amoeba, which, according to Epigenesis, was the
primordial atomic globule from which, by long
process of development, heredity and adaptation,
have been evolved the book-agents, politicians,
land-sharks, and other human representatives of
the bowelless Protozoa and sea-sharks of a former
age.
* # *
One of the three Queensland Civil Service
Commissioners is Michael O’Malley, for many
years C.P.S. at Ipswich, afterwards Police
Magistrate at several towns, and Sheriff of North
Queensland. There is no truer man in the
civil service. He is a Hibernian of that good
old school which thought that “ bating a foe ” was
just as imperative a duty as “ Gating a frind. ’
O'Malley is an old colonist, and saw many a
stirring event in the early days. He is one of the
most genial of men, and laughs boisterously at a
good joke, but he can also be as stern as Rhada-
manthus, and many a bully of the old days carried
the memory of O’Malley’s right upon his lower
jaw. Here is one episode told by a man who was
present on the occasion. At that time in Ipswich
there was a police officer, built on a large scale,
and of an offensively aggressive disposition. This
local tyrant was walking along with an old lame
Irish gardener who carried a stick to assist his
progress. Suddenly, and treacherously, the officer
struck the ancient horticulturist a heavy blow on
the side of the head, knocked him do%vn, took
away his stick, and walked on to meet O’Malley
coming up in the opposite direction. “You are a
cowardly scoundrel to strike that old man ! ” said
O’Malley. “ I’ll give you a lick with this stick,”
said the bould trap—who found himself unex-
pectedly lying on his back, and the stick in the
hands of O’Malley, who belted him with great
impartiality over the entire length and breadth of
his anatomy, after which the wielder of the cudgel
walked joyfully home, at peace with all mankind.
* # *
Last week I called to see C, F. Chubb,
father of one of our best Supreme Court judges.
This genial old colonist resides in Ipswich, where
he practised as a solicitor for many years. I met
him first in 187 b, when ho was one of the leading
citizens, writer of prologues, poems, and addresses,
moving spirit in all progressive movements, and
general good fellow all round. He is the author
of that smart Laconistie prophecy concerning Mr.
Justice Real who studied for the Bar while work-
ing as a carpenter in the Ipswich railway work-
shops. Chubb said : “That man has gone from
the bench to the Bar, and will ultimately go from
the Bar to the Bench.” It was sad indeed for me
to find this old legal disciple of Democritus- the
merry sage who laughed and grew fat—confined
to the house by paralysis, with the clear
memory and sparkling vivacity partially
suspended. So pass the worthy pioneers
slowly or abruptly like vanishing figures in
the kaleidoscope of life. Instinctively I re-
called that saying of Cyrus, the son of Cambyses :
“If neither generous friends, nor the spirits of
departed heroes, were to assist in perpetuating
our fame, our very names would soon be banished
from the memories of men.”

Among some of my old papers I find a
curious memento of a visit to the Woogarno
lunatic asylum some 12 years ago. One of the
patients, a man of good family and classical
education, imagined he had been appointed
“ Imperial Inspector of Asylums,” and he conli-
dentially entrusted me with one of his despatches
for delivery to Sir Arthur Kennedy. The follow -
ing is an exact copy :
Lunatic Asylum, Woogaroo,
19th May, 1877.
To the Secretary of State for the Colonies,
Lord Carnarvon.
My Dear Lord,-—I have the honour to enclose you my
final report to the Royal Commissioners appointed to
enquire into the working of the Asylum and the Recep-
tion House, and you will perceive that instead of being a
lunatic asylum it is a Fenian Protection Asylum for me
purpose of raising up a rebellion similar in character to the
one in Canada in 1838 when Lord Durham was sent out as
I -ord High Commissioner. I attribute this solely to Sir
Maurice O’Connell, President of the Legislative Council,
and the late Colonial Secretary, Sir Arthur Macalister,
who just before resigning appointed himself Agent-
General in London. My dear intended bride, lam with
me in affectionate love to her ladyship and your dear,
darling children. J.F.G., Imperial Inspector.

I met, last week, a schoolmate of Captain
Withers, who was in command of the unfortunate
Quetta. He tells me that Captain Withers’ father
was a sea captain who lost his vessel in a voyage
to Australia, sailed for England as a passenger
from Melbourne in the Royal Charter, and was
lost in the wreck of that vessel.

New nations spring up like Jonah’s cala-
bash. It seems incredible that only 44 years have
passed away since Dr. Browning, in March, 1846,
enquired in the House of Commons “ If any steps
had been taken to provide steam communication
with Australia ? ” And Cardwell in reply said
the question required grave consideration as it
“ involved serious financial difficulties!” And
now, in 1890 ! - Maroogaline.
'l'he N.S.W. Agricultural Society wants to
prefix “ Royal ”to its name. Who ever heard of
a royal agriculturist ? George 111. was called
“the farmer,” but only in jest. “When Adam
delved and Eve span, who was then the gentle-
man?” There is no congeniality between the
craft that constitutes the foundations of society
and the useless, gilded puppet that tops the social
pmnacle and is the antipodes of the farmer. The
N.S.W. Agricultural Society is moribund now ;
when it becomes “Royal ” it will be dead.
It was in Burketowu (Q.) in 1868, and the
thermometer would have stood, as usual, at 117
degrees in the shade, if there had been any shade
to stand it in ; time, 11.30 a.m. : scene, McLen.
nan’s big room, which at that time did duty a s
courthouse. The P.M., Government
&c., Mr. Landsborough, presided. The first and
only case set down for hearing was Swan v t
Thor]). No lawyers were, retained on either side,
for the good and sufficient reason that none
resided within 040 miles (happy Gulf-country !).
Captain (by courtesy) Swan deposed that he com-
manded a steamship plying to Sweer’s Island,
and carrying Government mails. (On cross-
examination he admitted said steamer was only
45 tons burthen.) Employed accused in the
capacity of cook, steward and first mate. Ac-
cused got drunk and neglected his duty, con-
sequently the whole concern had to be delayed for
one calendar day. Said the P.M., addressing
accused : “ Well, Joe, I think ye’d better go back
to work, or the ‘Pioneer’ won’t be able to get
away.’’ “But that won’t do,” quoth the gallant
Captain Swan —“ I want him punished.” “What
am I to do with him? ” mournfully enquired the
P.M., “ I have no lockup and no police.” “ Fine
him,” said the ready-witted captain. “H’m !
well, Joe, you’ll be fined a day’s pay,” quoth
Landsborough; “the court’s closed.” And as
they adjourned for refreshments, the Government
Resident, P.M., &c., said: “I wouldn’t stop it
from poor old Joe if I were you, Swan !” Joe’s
quick ears intercepted this remark, and as they
came out he accosted Swan with: “Mind! he
told you afterwards not to stop it.”
Several years ago
the members of the N.Z.
Parliament reduced their
own screws on the ground
that the country was
hard-up and required
economy. Then they
reduced the number of
legislators because there
were more of them than
were actually essential,
and they pointed out to
each other that by abol-
ishing 20 per cent, of
themselves they would
save a lot of needless
expenditure. Next they
abolished the property-
vote, though most of them
had property-votes them-
selves, and decreed that
all elections should be
held on one day, so that
a candidate who is de-
feated in one constitu-
ency has no longer a
chance to get in for
another during the same
election. And now the
country has heaved out
51 of these liberal-minded
senators in one batch,
and chosen new men in
their places, and among
the Oppositionists in par-
ticular there are only
about two prominent in-
dividuals left. The old
fossils have gone down
with a fearful and won-
derful smash, and among
the defeated men none
seem to be much regretted,
unless it may be Vincent
Pyke. Pyke’s constitu-
ents have been trying to
decide for 12 years past
whether they should pre-
sent him with a large
estate or shoot him at
•sight, but they have evi-
dently postponed the
decision too long, for he
i-> gone now, and the
chance is lost. Also Grey
has resigned, and the
harmless Stout lost cour-
age at the last moment
and kept out of the
contest, and Atkinson is
slowly dying, and Vogel
is in England losing
money over the most
idiotic and ignorant novel
published since the days
of Moses, and of the an-
cient land-marks there are
very few remaining. For
the politician is an un-
certain thing, who leads
a precarious existence,
and draws his salary at irregular intervals, and
then fades unexpectedly away, and only Parkes
endures for ever.
S. Crawford Pearson, the first discoverer
of rubies in Central Australia, died recently.
Haddon Chambers, the Australian play-
wright, whose play of the ‘ ‘ Idler ” is being pro-
duced at Prohman’s New York Lyceum, has been
stuffing the interviewers with yarns about his
forsaking the N.S. Wales Civil Service for cattle-
raising in the bush. He states that he lived in
London on Ins. a week before he struck ile with a
story published in Society, and a little play, as
yet unacted, called “ A Mere Cipher,” sold to
Prank Dietz. This success was followed by that
of “Devil’s Caresfoot,” written in conjunction
with another writer, from which, so says
Chambers, they derived little, owing to the
hands it got into. He then wrote “Captain
Swift ” for Beerbohm Tree, but found no oppor-
tunity of reading it to him until he saw him go
in-to a Turkish bath. There Chambers had him
up a tree, and reading the play, got it accepted.
Which reminds us that a certain N.S. Wales’
barrister will never read a long brief, no matter
how highly it is marked. The solicitor who would
have him thoroughly primed with the case usually
calls on him ere he rises on the morning of the
trial, at ones invades his bedroom, reads him out
of bed and into the bath, howls out instructions
above the din of the shower, continues reading
while he drosses and breakfasts, reads him into
town in a cab, and winds up by firing a summary
of the case into him at the barristers’ table. To
do the learned counsel justice, he makes few
mistakes, and always gets a verdict when one is
obtainable by artistically humbugging the jury.
A member of the Stanley gang says “Life
is very cheap in Central Africa, and girls may bo
bought in every village where we were at the rate
of four girls for a piece of 12 handkerchiefs.” Not
cheap at all. It is sufficient to throw one hand-
kerchief to the average Australian girl and she
is yours till death.
Frawncis Abigail, ’aving returned from
Hingland chock-full of “informations,” loses no
opportunity to air it with his French accent and
Bond-street duds. In a letter to Sydney Daily
Telegraph he asserts that :
“It has been reckoned that there is (sic) in London
more Jews than in Palestine, more Scotchmen than in
Aberdeen, more Welshmen than in Cardiff, more Irishmen
than in Belfast, and Germans, Italians, Frenchmen, and
others are legion. The numbers being Jews, over 30,000 ;
Scotchmen, over 50,000; Welshmen, over 30,000; Irish-
men, over 90,000,” &e.
Referring the much-travelled and damnably-
inaeeurate Abigail for information concerning the
population of Palestine to the proverbial school-
boy, let us point out to him that the population
of Cardiff is 108,570; that Belfast has 185,000
inhabitants, and that he likewise ridiculously
under-estimates the population of Aberdeen. A
little travel is a dangerous thing.
Some months ago when the puzzle-com-
petition broke out very badly in Sydney, The
Bulletin exposed the system of the “chork-
pops,” nom-de-plume, £5-gifts, gentry who chose
two simple words, transposed their initial syl-
lables, and sat down to await postage-stamps.
These being received in numbers, there the matter
ended and they recommenced the game from a
different address and under a different cognomen.
But a fresh breed has sprung up who select high-
sounding titles, deposit sums in the hands of
public persons, and brazen the matter out,
generally on the perfect rectitude “lay.” They
work the thing differently : they select a number
of letters capable of construction into various
words, and should all these be spotted, in order to
keep the money in the family they invent a new
one. Thus from the combination of letters
which legitimately form only “Pinch-gut, thing-
cup, pitch-gun, and tug-pinch,” these gentry
produce “gun-chipt,” having invented the latter
word to save the expense of “parting.” In the
hands of another guileless genius “muriatic”
becomes “citarium,” and so on. As we said
before, we have no sympathy for the credulous
owls who expect to gain £5O in return for ss. by
exercising their shallow wits in a manner befitting
the occupants of an idiot-asylum. But the crop
of fools is perennial.
A Great Triumph.
The following case has come under our observa
tion :
Miss (daughter of a prominent physi
cian who won’t allow us to publish names, because
“it would he infra dig, and unprofessional”) ha, 1
been in delicate health for the last five years.
The cause was sin-ply anaemia or poorness ol
blood, her father had treated her for all sorts ol
complaints and she had been also attended bj
several “ ladies’ ” specialists without benefit. The
case came under our notice quite accidentally
some months ago, Miss was then just
20i years of age, five feet five inches in height and
weighed exactly 101 pounds. She had the usual
symptoms of anaemia, excessively pale and pasty
skin and lips, dark circles round the eyes, soft and
flabby limbs, complete loss of periodic functions,
was excessively nervous, timid and weak, no
appetite and very restless at night. We micro
scopically examined the blood and by dilution
with distilled water, and careful examination,
arrived at a very accurate estimation of the
number of red blood corpuscles per ounce, what
there were of them appeared all shrivelled and
shrunk like dried fruit and were almost destitute
of vitalising properties. We advised the use of
Clements Tonic in doses of a dessertspoonful after
meals, and this treatment was adopted without
the father’s knowledge. Four weeks afterwards
when five small bottles of the remedy had been
taken we again examined the -blood, the red
corpuscles had increased by 70 per cent, as near as
we could estimate, and under the microscope had
the appearance of over ripe red currants almost
bursting, the patient had gained five pounds then
(September 7th, 1889) weighing seven stone eight
pounds. The appetite was much improved, the
limbs were becoming firmer and the muscular
tissue more defined and pronounced. She could
walk considerable distances without undue fatigue,
could eat and digest almost any kind of food, the
lips were pink and firm and the dark circles under
the eyes had almost disappeared. We advised a
continuance of the use of Clements Tonic and she
took a further course of four large bottles, and
then as perfect strength and vital energy was
restored, a longer treatment was not considered
necessary. She is now fully restored to health,
strong, vigorous and weighs 10 stone 3 pounds,
can do any kind of work or walk five miles with-
out fatigue, eats well, sleeps well, and has a vigor-
ous appetite. What a change from her condition
four months ago !
Now, reader, here’s a case that has baffled the
best medical and therapeutical skill for years,
which Clements Tonic has positively cured in
three months, but we are not allowed to publish
names-because it would not be orthodox. Man-
kind may suffer, hundreds of curable cases must
be neglected, scores of lives must be sacrificed,
because “it would be infra dip. and unprofes-
sional ” for a medical man to recommend or give a
testimonial to Clements Tonic because it is “a
proprietary article or patent medicine.” The
mere fact of its being a patent medicine is the
greatest protection for the public, for were it made
otherwise its therapeutical excellence would be
subordinate to the profits of its various makers;
while the manufacture is in the hands of one
maker, it is to his interest to make a genuine
article.
Full particulars and endorsements on appli-
cation to F. M. Clements, Newtown, N.S.W. ;
at Melbourne, George and George ; at Adelaide,
S. A. Mutual Stores, 96 Waymouth-street; at
Wellington (N.Z.), Sharland & Co., and Laun-
ceston, J. D. Johnstone ; and all dealers every-
where.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Ten members of the “ six families ” have
been elected to the new Assembly of Western
Australia. ■
“Dick” Haynes,brother of “Mudgee,” was
defeated for West Perth at the W.A. general
elections.
If one-third of Britain were put under
cultivation all her people might be fed with the
produce. %
Glassey, the Queensland labour repre-
sentative, urges the total abolition of pensions.
The day-laborer gets no pension.
Says Louise Michel:—“The republic of
Germany will be better than that of France,
because the men are cleaner and less stupid.”
A Melbourne illustrated paper, published
by the Age proprietary, says that the “ Victorian
railways are a national white elephant.” “A
straw best shows,” etc.
It is announced per medium of a long
yaxm in one of the papers that “the first Catholic
cadet-corps has been formed in Sydney.” We are
sorry. We shall probably next hear of an Orange
cadet-corps.
South Australia is no longer in the Federal
Council. The S.A. Legislative Council has
rejected a bill for continuing the representation,
which expires early in 1891. Tins, probably, will
hasten the death of the Council.
One of the new members of the new W.A.
Assembly is an advocate of the one-man-one-vote
system. He stood alone among all the candidates
up for election recently in the advocacy of this
principle. And yet he got in.
“ Timotheus ” very happily says that
Premier Munro “ is the manager and not the star-
actor of the company,” and adds that “he is a
Premier of the order of J. G. Francis, who was
the ring on which the keys were hung. ”
Here is a peculiar political puzzle : First
of all Dibbs supported Parkes against Crick ;
then he backed up Crick against Parkes, and then
he faced round again and supported Parkes
against Crick. Puzzle : Find the consistency of
Dibbs.
The result of the elections in Western
Australia has been the return of one K.C.M.G.,
one C.M.G., seven squatters, four lawyers, nine
merchants, two financiers, two farmers, one doctor,
one contractor, one publican, leaving one contest
undecided.
Ireland, though having the lowest mar-
riage and birth rates in the world, makes up for
this to an extent by showing the highest average
child-productiveness per family. The average
number of offspring per family in Ireland is SJ,
in Australia 4L In France, which is the lowest,
it is only 3.
Italy, the cable indicates, bores ahead in
Abyssinia, emulating England’s swallow of India.
The late King of Abyssinia died last year, and
the new one signed an abject treaty of “mutual
protection ” with Italy. Mutual protection—
how English !
Blocker Warton, ex-Attorney-General of
W.A., has left that province for the more con-
genial atmosphere of London. Warton has never
been a success in the fair city of Perth, and when
the defunct Council reduced his pension from
£4OO to £333 before his face, he felt that life in
Australia was a mockery.
Mr. Justin M‘Carthy is leading the anti-
Parnell Party. Yet, speaking in Dublin on 20th
Nov. (before the party disruption) he said his
hearers “ might take from him the suggestion that
if Mr. Lockwood had 'been allowed to cross-
examine” (for Mrs. O’Shea) “ they would have a
very different story before them that night.”
The Tasmanian Treasurer has decided to
put hop-beer and dandelion ale on the same tax-
ation footing as malt liquors. One of two deduc-
tions logically follows. Either the Treasurer is a
shameless person who wages war against “soft”
drinks in the brewers’ interest, or else he has
found that the so-called “soft ” drinks are in reality
hard.
The real explanation of the Indian out-
breaks in America is the same as in the Maori
rebellions —breaches of faith by the whites. In
Canada the Indians are quiet, because they are
governed by people who devote their lives to it,
whereas in the States the work is done by political
carpetbaggers. The pressure Westwards leads to
continual perjuries against the poor Indians.
The old Council in W.A. consisted of
26 members, of whom 25 were rank Conservatives,
and the remaining one a Liberal. The Assembly
just elected contains no less than 18 members of
the old House (most of whom were returned with-
out opposition) and 12 new members. Thus the
Conservative element is still dominant, and is
likely to hinder useful and practical legislation.
The coal-mines of Chikeyo (Japan) are the
grimy prison-house of some 1700 of the Mikado’s
subjects who have run against some of the 429
sections of the Penal Code of 1880. The hours are
about 12 daily, and the lash is the chief incentive
to industry. Each man has to turn out 10 sou.
(3|d) worth of coal every time he enters the mine,
failing this he receives “solitary” and the lash.
This is the establishment with which patriotic
“ free labour ” Australian employers sought to open
up trade relations during the late strike.
What mighty contests arise from trivial
things ! A single pair of rabbits liberated in
Victoria have well-nigh wrecked all Australia.
Worked out as a sum in geometrical progression,
it has been calculated that in the course of only
three years the possible progeny of two rabbits
might attain the prodigious number of 13,000,000.
It is a matter of some consideration now as to
whether it wouldn’t be quicker and cheaper to
shift Australia than to shift the rabbits.
Under Parkes’ Electoral Bill an uncertifi-
cated bankrupt is to be disqualified for a seat in
the N.S.W. Assembly; but there is a crafty
proviso limiting the disqualificatiorrto people who
file their schedules after the Bill becomes law. If
by any mischance it should pass without that
proviso, there is one man in this country behind
whom the doors of political life would close for
ever. They have closed for ever several times
before, but this time it would be with a slam.
Last June two officers in the N.S.W.
Colonial Architect’s Department were suspended
in consequence of the faulty construction of cer-
tain defence-works at Bear Island, and rumour
said this business had something to do with the
Colonial Architect’s resignation also. Are we
ever likely to be favoured with the report of the
Royal Commission on this peculiar matter, Or will
it remain pigeon-holed until another accidental
fire cremates it and sundry other interesting docu-
ments now stowed away in the State archives ?
There is just this to be said about Parnell
and the Sydney papers. Admitting that his re-

Victoria Railway Commissioner Speight
has graciously accepted a seven years’ re-engage-
ment in “ the hope that Parliament will pay some
respect to the arrangement made between him
and the late Government as regards increase of
salary.” Parliament—bless its honest heart!
will pay that necessary respect, and Speight will
get from Munro & Co. the rise which they howled
against when in Opposition. Meanwhile Mr.
Speight, who has, for ample or insufficient
reasons, lost the confidence of the public, is sing-
ing decidedly small.
John Haynes writes that “it is a well-
known fact that England is not overrun with
manufactures from foreign countries !” Just as
if it had not been found necessary in that fine
Freetrade country to pass a law enacting that all
goods of foreign manufacture sold in the United
Kingdom should be branded with the name of the
land from which they came. As a consequence,
nearly everything you buy in England is branded
“Made in Germany.” Even the “English”
Christmas cards one buys in Sydney are labelled
“Printed in Germany.” '
Personal Items.
Mrs. O’Shea is 50 years old ; Parnell, 45.
Cardinal Moran has entered his 60th year.
Sir Hercules Robinson has been made a
baronet.
Sir Grey is the only one of the
old-time Australian explorers now alive.
Lady Rosebery held on to the religion of
her fathers, and was buried with Jewish rites.
Jameson, of African artistic-cannibalistic
notoriety, was a scion of the Irish whisky-distilling
family.
Miss Alice Cornwell and Phil. Robinson
are not prospering in London, and the Sunday
Times languishes.
Lord Carington was “best man ’ when
Earl Rosebery married Miss Rothschild. Beacons-
field gave the bride away.
The Hon. William Grahame, M.L.C.,
N.S.W., who died recently, aged 82, was a Scotch
mechanic, brought to the colony by Dr, Lang in
1831.
“If Mh'ams doesn’t look out he’ll find
himself in gaol,” was a common remark in Mel-
bourne for months before Colonel Templeton
started his crusade.
There will, The Bulletin predicts, be a
strong reaction in favour of Dismal Jemmy
Mirams, because nothing can make him out a
rogue in the eyes of the people.
Christie Murray’s discovery of his true
vocation has much amused the Londoners, some
of whom loudly assert that this is not the first
time he has tried the stage.
Mrs. John Sutherland died within twenty-
four hours of assent being given to the Appro-
priation Bill which contained her grant of £3OOO.
Her Only daughter will now inherit.
Parnell, it is pointed, out by United
Ireland, has “rebelled against the fashionable
code of honour of the English Divorce Court, and
has not attempted to shield himself by perjury.”
Someone writes Jos. Symes to “ send me
your vile publication no more.” Jos. meekly
replies ; “I have not sent you the paper. I rarely
cast pearls before swine, or give holy things to
dogs. ”
In Dublin, the other day, two Murphys
confronted one another, one from the bench and
the other from the dock, while nine jurymen of
the same name helped to find their erring relative
guilty.
The twelve good men and true who sat on
the Premier Building Association case were, with
about three exceptions, the dullest looking lot
that ever gladdened the eyes of counsel for defen-
dants.
An eminent Sydney Q.C. demanded 160
guineas and daily refreshers to defend an assault-
case at the recent Criminal Court. He did not get
it—a circumstance which gave a chance to the
junior Bar.
Mrs. O’Shea has, it is cabled, accepted
£50,000 in settlement of her claims in connection
with the well-advertised Wood estate lawsuit. The
cablegram has since been denied - like every other
cablegram.
Dr. Barnardo has the character of a sensa*
tion-monger “at home.” He used to have a way
of getting his “waifs ” up in artistic arrangements
of rags before photographing them for monthly-
circular purposes.
The Australian teetotallers call John
B. Gough, the “great American orator.” As a
matter of fact, Gough was an Englishman, and was
in his first manhood when he went to the States.
He learned to drink as well as to orate in America.
Chief Justice Higinbotham is the second
man who has received in person the thanks
of the Victorian Parliament. The other was
General Pratt, for having contributed a share in
the slaughter of the Maoris during the war of
1860.
One of the most effective pieces of sketchy
writing seen in the Australian press for a long
time is that column and-a-half in which “ Grains”
describes (in Melbourne Daily Telegraph) the
scene in Court at the conclusion of the Premier
Permanent trials.
The Duchess of Marlborough has had
Several judgments rendered against her in New
York on behalf of creditors in Birmingham. She
is, however, too well protected by the American
marriage settlement to square up her husband’s
debts, no matter how willing she be.
Apropos to Premier Parkes’ intervention
on behalf of the private character of the Prince of
Wales, we are reminded that on the front of the
new Government Works Office in Phillip-street,
Sydney, Tummy’s head appears in stone with the
English kingly crown on. A little previous !
Gladstone's birthday was on 29th Decem-
ber. The Bulletin prophesies that within a few
weeks, Parkes, K.C.M.G., will have published in
the Sydney daily papers a letter received by him
from Gladstone in reply to his (Parkes’) birthday-
congratulations. “Me and Mister Gladstone !”
Hon. G. B. Simpson, N.S.W. Attorney-
General, has gone to Melbourne with a view to
personally conducting the Earl of Jersey thence
to Sydney. As G.B.S. always looks like “A
Gentleman of the Reign of George IV.” he should
make a not unfavourable impression on the Tory
peer.
When J. E. Brown, the N.S. Wales
Director of Forests, was in the S.A. service, his
office was exactly opposite that of H. Y. L. Brown,
the S.A. Government Geologist. Through this,
one was called “Woody” Brown and the other
“ Stony ” ditto. The latter still retains his nick-
name.
Professor Koch, the German microbe-ex-
terminator, has a cousin in Victoria—a long,
plain, German farmer, who hoes potatoes and
takes no interest in scientific research. Another
cousin was the ex-sailor, publican, and miner,
Harry Koch, who discovered the famous Koch’s
Pioneer claim at Bendigo.
Arthur Forbes and Jack Woodyatt, two
Queensland notabilities, are exciting wonder and
awe in London “ sassiety ” just now; while W.
K. D’Arcy, the Mount Morganaire and ci-devant
Rockhampton attorney, is bidding for popularity
something after the style which has been immor-
talised by “ Nitrate King ” North.
The Pall Mall remarks that Admiral
Scott’s precedence claim is quite novel and wholly
untenable. His predecessors were perfectly
satisfied with the second place at table. The fact
that Admiral Scott is a scion of the house of the
“ bold Buccleuch,” and has wedded an Australian
beauty, can hardly be regarded as-giving him a
title to head the procession.
It is said that Mrs. (“ General ”) Booth
was the means of restoring the giddy wife of a
British Cabinet Minister to her husband’s arms.
The individual in question had prepared a petition
in divorce, with a young Prince as.co-respondent,
when Mrs. B. appeared on the scene, the petition
was withdrawn, husband and wife left for the
continent, and the young Don Juan for Gibraltar.
The Pall Mall speculates whether Mr.
Crick, of N.S.W., is a descendant of the gentle-
man of the same name, public orator of Cambridge
from 1836 to 1848, whose address, on the installa-
tion of Prince Albert, Thackeray parodied in
Punch ? The Bulletin thinks the question may
be safely answered in the negative. Mr. Crick is
so vigorous a speaker that he must surely be the
very first orator of his family.
Bishop Moorhouse, late of Australia, now
of Manchester, may succeed Dr. Thompson, Arch-
bishop of York, as Primate of England. Which
is only another proof of the fact that a man who
is big enough to be regarded by Australians as
really a big man, can hold his own with anyone in
England. If a man can impress Australia with
his ability he can go up top anywhere.
Leonard Courtney, M.P., was asked at an
English political meeting whether he was in
favour of the Bill to legalise marriage with a
deceased wife’s sister. “May I inquire,” said
Mr. Courtney, all smiles, “ whether the gentleman
who puts that question is married ?” “lam !”
“Has your wife a sister living?” “She has.”
“Is your wife present?” “No.” “Well, my
wife is present; she too has a sister living,” said
Courtney, resuming his seat.
The late Archbishop of York was con-
sidered to be too much of a thinker for the “ Can-
terbury ” billet. At the time of his appointment
he was generally spoken of as the heir-apparent to
the Primacy, but, ere long, “York” developed
ideas and earned a reputation for unorthodoxy.
So for thirty years he stood aside and saw smaller
men walk past him into the Promised Land which
lies beyond Lambeth Palace gates. The slighted
Archbishop amassed a heap of ointment, no doubt,
but there was a fly in it.
Mr. W. Clarson, the well-known, agricul-
tural writer, editor of the Mildura (N.S.W.)
Cultivator, has suicided by shooting himself in
the abdomen. Among his effects was found a
paper bearing the following words “ Quite
overwhelmed; mind gone; no hope; friendless.”
Mr. Clarson, formerly principal partner in Clar-
son, Massinaand Co., Melbourne, had lived a very
varied and stormy life; the divorce-suit in which
his wife figured is still fresh in the memory of
Victorian readers.
Thus “ Dagonet "; “Stanley is un-
doubtedly a man of great perseverance, and he
must be a man of some courage also, but he is as
little like a hero, using the word in its best and
correctest sense, as can well be imagined ; he is
a hard bargaining man of business, much more
like a bagman than a Bayard, with the keenest of
eyes for the main chance; he has about as much
to do with heroism and chivalry as the,captain of
one of Marryat’s slaving schooners, or one of the
lowest of his black-flag hoisting pirates-had to do
with Drake or Blake or Columbus or Vasco de
Gama.”

THR OFFICAL SCULPTURE AND THE OFFICIAL POETRY OF BOTANY BAY.
Pares thinks the Sydney Post-office carvings as works of art are quite on a par with the literature of the country. That our readers may judge for themselves, we quote a few passage.

Society
Now, ye bourgeoisie, what do you know
about the Grand Passion, be its object a Kitty or
a Cleopatra ? Have you ever felt it galvanise you,
salivate you, magnetise your spine, electrify you
to the tips of your fingers and toes? Mr. and
Mrs. Bethel Bolster are very good people in their
way, but none of your Grand Passion for them,
any more than for Mr. and Mrs. Tristram
Shandy.
* # #
Lady Hopetoun’s brother, Hon. F. R. W.
E. DeMoleyns, will not (says the eableman)
return to Melbourne as A.D.C. td Governor
Hopetoun. Bless you, we never thought he would !
He will be succeeded by an individual bearing the
oppressively-dignified “monniker” of Capt. the
Hon. Claud H. C. Willoughby. Willoughby is
understood to have an excellent moral character
and to be fond of staying in o’ nights.
# $5 #
Lady Hopetoun “continues to progress
satisfactorily,” but we don’t hear a word as to
how her brother is getting on. Satisfactorily, we
trust. Favourable accounts of Lord Hopetoun’s
health also come to hand from Government House,
Melbourne, the latest yarn stating that his lord-
ship has a habit of glancing at the vacant chair,
and then breaking forth into the glad refrain—
“ That’s when you feel all right.”
* * *
Lord Hopetoun’s two sisters, now at
Government House, Melbourne, areof the Sweden-
borgian persuasion, and for yeai’S have been
regular and devout attendants at the New Jeru-
salem Church at Netting Hill Gate, London.
They are very amiable, but somewhat “slow.”
However, they will be a decided improvement
upon Lady Hopetoun, who. is noted for her per-
sonal dislike of the colonies—writes a London
correspondent.
# # #
Sydney Truth simply deceives itself in
trying to palm-off part of one of Robert Brough’s
“ Songs of the Governing Classes ” as an original
“ Ode to a well-preserved fossil in the N.S.W.
Legislative Council.” Almost as well crib the
Lord’s Prayer and publish it as original prose.
# # *
And a word in the ear of the other Sydney
Sunday paper, tire Times, which, inexorable in
its virtue, prescribes the lash as the only effective
deterrent for assaults on youthful purity. Which
is the worse, let us ask, to debauch a single indi-
vidual, or to pander to the salacity of all the dirty-
minded little gamblers and prurient servant-
girls of a whole country by means of a dirty
publication issued from your office ? The
Bulletin has not a weak stomach. It could eat
its dinner at a post-mortem in a morgue, but its
gorge revolts at such transparent cant as th°t of
the Sunday Times avenger of virtue.

And shouts: “ The larrikin must feel the rigour of the
law ; «
The only way to tame those brutes who thump and swear
and smash
Is an unsparing usage of triangles and the lash.”
If so, the best prescription for all kinds of moral ailing
Is an outward application of the cat, in fact a whaling,
80, then, a truce to sentiment, farewell to mawkish lenity,

la the (London) Hawk of 18th Nov. last
appears a four-column article entitled “The
Avenger of Virtue,” in which the bull’s-eye is
partly turned on to the wanton and cruel massacre
following on a certain historical performance farci-
cally known as the Mount Rennie trial.
# * #
According to the Victorian gaol regula-
tions Mirams’ big beard, the now grey beard
which the little man has worn for so many years,
will have to be shaved. To the average man, who
has not a conspicuously large beard, nor a particu-
larly luxuriant crop of hair, gaol-shaving and
cropping involve no particular indignity, especially
as the hair and beard are allowed to grow for
three months before the expiry of the sentence.
But a beard such as Mirams’ does not grow in a
year, and it will be cruelly extending the little
man’s punishment should he be treated in the
same fashion as a short-bearded man. A man’s
sufferings should, so far as possible, be allowed to
expire with his sentence, and this can never be the
case where release from prison is attended by a
marked change in personal appearance. This plea
is offered on behalf, not alone of well-known and
comparatively influential men like Mirams, but
also for all obscure long-bearded men who may be
so unhappy as to come within the grasp of a law
which should, while no respecter of persons, be hi
all things humane.
* # #
It was with sadly unconscious humour that
Mirams, in pleading for a lenient sentence,
said “he might speak of the cruel length of the
prosecution.” Perhaps the poor, broken-down
little man had really forgotten for the moment
how a horde of counsel for the defence talked
against time in Melbourne City Court.
* * *
They have a regulation at Melbourne
Lying-in Hospital, which draws a distinction
between the married women and the unmarried,
the latter of whom are not allowed to see their
friends. Who are the tabbies that they should
take the functions of punishment into their own
hands ? —these luxurious tabbies, not one of whom
has ever to complain, as did Madame Roland, that
the wants of her nature were unsatisfied. The
unmarried mothers are only friendless because
they are moneyless : the most skilful doctors, the
best nurses, the most expensive delicacies, are
equally at the service of the rich married woman,
and of the unmarried mother who has money of her
own, or has a rich protector. It is not wickedness
which is disgraceful, only helplessness and poverty.
How fashionably feminine —to bite and scratch
and kick your sister and pull her hair out when
she has fallen ! The love which has placed the
servant-girl at the mercy of a lot of fashionable
dames, is often more holy than the business com-
pact which has sold a “respectable” woman to
her rich husband.

Several things occur to us in re Sydney
Echo’s prize yachting-song. Firstly, its senti-
ments are far too “ unobjectionable ” to suit the
calf-and-breastbone brigade ; secondly, not being
set to music it ain’t a song at all; thirdly, that it
ain’t suitable for setting to music; fourth, and
lastly, that nobody would ling it if it were—
except, perhaps, the people who don’t go down to
the sea in yachts. However, it might suit James
Fairfax, the Herald’s glum proprietor, who has
or used to have a yacht. He didn’t like the sea
over-much, for on the rare occasions upon which
the Magic left the Beautiful Harbour for Southern
waters, “ Mister James ” used go down to meet it
in the train.
# * *
A well-ku >wn Sydney journalist was, in
California the other day, living at an hotel in a
■“prohibitionist” town. On the first evening of
his residence at the caravanserai, shortly before
nine o’clock the waiter came to the room where
the scribe sat smoking with a friend and ex-
claimed; “Gentlemen, I am now going to the drug-
store.” “Well,” said the journalist, thinking this
communication most irrelevant and uninterest-
ing, “suppose you are going to the drug-store,
we havn’t got anything to do with that, have
we?” “That, gentlemen,” replied the waiter,
“is what I have come to ascertain. You see,
this hotel has a standing prescription, and I can
get you whatever you want. I was just going
down to get half-a-pint of whisky for an upstairs
guest and thought you might require some.”
And when it was put that way our friend, the
journalist, thought that he did want the standing
prescription of the hotel dispensed on his behalf.

The average reporter seldom tells the
truth except by accident, and not often even then.
Eor instance, when Mrs. Pearcey, the murderess,
appeared before the court, the London papers went
into details as follows :
Daily News.—She is a slightly-built woman of medium
height.
Standard.—She is a powerfully-built young woman much
above the ordinary stature.
Daily News.—Her hat, which had faded-blue trimmings
in it, &o. She also wore a cape trimmed with crape.
Standard.—The prisoner wore a green straw hat trimmed
with dark-green ribbons, and a short dolman of
grenadine.
Daily Telegraph.—She had on a black hat trimmed with
dark-green ribbon, and adark ulsterof rough cloth.
Echo.—Dressed in a hat of dark-green shade.
Daily Chronicle.—On her head was a black hat.
Star.—She wore a big, black cloak.
# # *
There is a certain eloquent pathos about
the new chum at times. The other day the
wreckage of an imported British dude—an indi-
vidual with haggard boots, and a coat that seemed
to have been habitually walked over by frogs and
snails, and an eyeglass, and a general aspect like
that of a duke who slept in a haystack—dropped
into a Dunedin hotel and ordered a drink. But
the publican looked at him with a relentless eye,
and asked in a cold, hard tone if he had got the
sixpence, and when he explained that he had
unfortunately left his cheque-book at home, on top
of the grand piano, and that his last £lOO note
was in his other pants along with the Koh-i-noor,
and consequently he couldn’t pay till he received
his remittance, the drink-merchant said briefly
“Go !” Then a bright idea seemed to flicker over
the new chum’s dreary face. “ Suppose,” he said,
in a broken voice, “I were to—haw —deposit my
eye-glass with you as seeuwity until my wemittance
awwives.” N.B. —He got the drink.
# * #
Dear Bulletin,— Re your meritorious
crusade against the belltopper-hat, have you not
noticed that the greatest supporters of this old
British abomination are the Americans? Walk
down Wall-street or Broadway in July, when
sunstroke is a power in the land, and you find a
sea of “nailcans.” Again, note the Yankee in Aus-
tralia (generally representing prison-made doors,
canned fruits and fish that we ought to be flogged
for importing when we have the raw material rot-
ting at our doors), and you find him with a highly-
spiced Down East accent and a still more pro-
nounced narrow-brim shiny stove-pipe tile ! N.B.
—The above is the result of an American who lost
a Lincoln and Bennett to me over Carbine having
lately made himself scarce.—Yours, H.Y.A.
* * *
A well-known -.writer recently sent in a
simple story about two sisters to a certain Sydney
weekly journal on approval. Two weeks later he
and found its beauties unexplored. He then called
asked the manuscript back, and, heading it the
“Salacious Sisters,” again sent it in. He called
back inside three days and found the counter-clerk
devouring it and ascertained that it had been read
by nearly everyone in the building from editor to
proof-reader. But they didn’t accept the yarn,
and it was returned with the remark that it did
not sustain the interest excited by the title.

# # #
“An Australian” writes to a Loudon
paper to express his astonishment at the strange
apathy of the spiritless people of Britain towards
the indolence of the classes. Thus, he points out
that, while the carriages of the rich are allowed to
monopolise the roadways in Hyde Park, the
vehicles of the poor and the cabs of the middle-
class are rigorously excluded. And this, too, even
when Society, so-called—that is, the sweating
classes- are absent from the metropolis.

Some years ago, one of the Victorian
Supreme Court judges condemned in severe terms
those dudes who patronised the dress-circles of
the Melbourne theatres on vice-regal nights, “in
order to get a sniff of governors' wives." Curious
to relate, the same judge was to the forefront in
leading off the last Cup festivities at Melbourne
Government House.

At Port Darwin the other day, Ah Ng, a
Chinaman, got married to a young girl of his own
nationality. After the marriage he didn’t speak
a word to his bride, but got up at four o'clock in
the morning, and said he was going fora walk.
Shortly afterwards his body was found floating in
the harbour. The local Celestial idea is that a
devil ran away with him.

BANNERMAN OF DANDENONG.
I rode through the bush in the burning noon,
Over the hills to ra’y bride ;
The track was rough and the way was long,
And Bannerman, of Dandenong,
He rode along by my side.
A day’s march off my beautiful dwelt,
By the Murray streams in the west .
Lightly lilting a gay love-song,
Rode Bannerman of the Dandenong
With a blood-red rose on his breast
“ Red, red rose of the western streams,*
Was the song he sang that day—
Truest comrade in hour of need —
Bay Mathinna his peerless steed—
I had my own good gray.
There fell a spark on the upland grass,
The dry bush leapt into flame ;
And 1 felt my heart go cold as death,
And Bannerman smiled and caught Ills breath,
But 1 heard him name her name.
Down the hillside the fire-flood rushed,
On the roaring eastern wind ;
Neck and neck was the reckless race—
Ever the bay mare kept her pace,
But the gray horse dropped behind.
He turned in the saddle—“ Let’s change, I say !”
And his bridle-rein he drew,
He sprang to the ground— “ Look sharp !” he said,
With a backward toss of his curly head,
“I ride lighter than you !”
Down and up—it was quickly done—
No words to waste that day ! —
Swift as a swallow she sped along,
The good hay mare from the Dandenong—
And Bannerman rode the gray.
The hot air scorched like a furnace blast
Prom the very mouth of hell—
The blue-gums caught and blazed on high
LHce flaming pillars into the sky ;
The gray horse staggered and fell.
“For your life 1” he cried—“ for her dear sake, ride?
Into the gulf of flame
Were swept, in less than a breathing space,
The laughing eyes, and the comely face,
And the lips that named her name.
She bore me bravely, the good bay mare—
Stunned and dizzy and blind,
I heard the sound of a mingling roar,
’Twas the Lachlan river that rushed before,
And the flames that rolled behind.
Safe, safe, at Waranga gate,
I fell, and lay like a stone.
O love ! thine arms were about me then,
Thy warm tears called me to life again,
But, O Ood ! that I came alone !
■We dwell in peace, my beautiful one
And I, by the streams in the West,
But oft through the mist of my dreams along
Rides Bannerman of the Dandenong,
With the blood-red rose on his breast.
A. WERNER, in A TIME AND TIMES.

•succeeded to a little fortune, was living at a R.C.
presbytery, whereat resided two priests. Gallagher
entered the place and claimed his wife, who refused
to go with him. A fight ensued, and one or both
of the two priests fell on Gallagher, beating him
terribly. The case came before the B.M. Court,
but was finally withdrawn “ to prevent scandal to
religion.” And now Mrs. Gallagher’s little child
tugs in vain at the blood-bedabbled dress of its
dead mother. Emile Zola understands this sort
of thing.

o * *
In the new Sydney lock-up the old and
barbaric system of crowding a lot of more or less
sober prisoners into one small, dark, noisome cell,
is to be abolished. The new cells are to be
brightly lit, and the occupants are at all times to
be visible to the officer in charge through doors
consisting of open steel bar-work. The brutal in-
difference which has characterised the N.S.W.
authorities in such matters may be judged from
the fact that when a man is sentenced to death he
is placed in a lighted cell from the outside of which
his every movement is visible; whereas when a
man who has been picked up unconscious in the
street smelling of drink, and who may, for all
that the police know, be suffering either from
mere alcoholism or from an obscure skull-fracture,
is simply pitched into a dungeon. The outcome
of this hideous system is shewn in the ghastly
cases of Hewett and his victim and in those of
many other poor wretches whose blood rests on
the head of the authorities.

# # «
The Sydney bank-manager who disap-
peared the other day leaving some of his clothes
and a pistol loaded in four chambers beside them
on the beach, also an alleged deficiency of £4000
in his accounts, appears to have made a bold
and sc’entific dash for freedom, inasmuch as he
departed by ’Frisco mail-steamer just as the
Christmas holidays commenced, and the mail had
got past New Zealand (the last point at which lie
could be arrested on an extradition-warrant ere
reaching Honolulu, where there is no extradition)
before the bank people discovered, on the
resumption of business, that he had departed. He
will find at Honolulu many congenial souls. So
far as Australia is concerned, the dominions of
King Kalakana form the only defaulters’ refuge
left on earth. When the steamer calls there more
than one Australian on whose head a price is set
always goes aboard to have a drink and see if he
knows any of the passengers. As we last steamed
away from Honolulu the chief accountant of a
Sydney bank, with whom we had been sauntering
round, pointed with his thumb at an important-
looking potentate in a very elaborate white suit:
“That’s one of our fellows,” he said. “He
shook £4650 from us six years ago, got away with
it, and is settled here now—married to a woman
with money,” On the same occasion we got a
glimpse of a cheerful Tasmanian well-known in
Sydney in the days of “Olivette ” and “ Tambour
Major.” He had skipped—with a palty £1000 or
so. After residing in Honolulu for some years he
obtained a splendid appointment—confidential
secretary to one of the fifteen richest men in the
States—and he holds that appointment now.
# # *
Dear Bulletin, —The usual bank-manager
went a-missing last week in Sydney, and the old,
familiarshortage turned up in the accounts, and the
customary clothes were found by the edge of the
ocean to show that he had gone in to wash him-
self from sanitary motives, and not in any way
owing to the fact that the cash had gone wrong
and had got drowned by accident. Also, a
revolver, with one barrel discharged, was placed
along with the clothes, to demonstrate that ho
had blown his brains out first, so that if he got
drowned his sufferings might be cut short, or else
it was intended to prove that he had committed
suicide in a naked condition on the shore and
some one had stolen the body, or possibly be
meant to imply that he had shot himself
at sea and bad then swum out and de-
posited the pistol on the shore and after-
wards drowned his dead remains, or else he
habitually shot himself before washing—the
theories were all the same price and the public
could take its choice. The first,impression of the
police was that a man who was both shot and
drowned must be very dead indeed, and they
looked round anxiously to find out if he had
hanged himself in addition to his other misfor-
tunes, and then pounded himself to death against
a wall and taken poison ; their next idea was that
he was probably too dead altogether, and they
rushed off to look at the list of passengers By the
San Francisco steamer. The suit of clothes
which is found by the.sea shore, with a tearful
farewell in the pocket, is about played out, and
when the cash won’t square it is advisable to hunt
up some fresher expedient. Some very good
results used to be arrived at by hiring a man to
come in at night, and tie up the manager, and
burgle the safe, and the innocent policeman of the
early days generally took it for granted that the
deficiency was entirely owing to this midnight
marauder, but 40 years of hard service wore tliis
dodge to pieces. Then another enterprising race
of officials arose, who simply set the bank on fire
when things wouldn’t balance, but the modern
bank isn’t combustible enough, and besides the
fire brigade always comes along in time to save

OVERHEARD AT THE TELEGRAPH OFFICE.
OLD LADY (to Operator): “Please tell my son Jim that I can’t get hack to-night, and
will you kindly send him this door-key at the same time?”
OPERATOR: “We can’t send him the key by telegraph, mum.”
OLD LADY: “Oh, very well, if you can’t manage that it’s of no use my sending the
telegram as he can’t possibly get in without the key. Good day!”
the cash-book and the ledger and all the shortage,
and even this scheme is practically extinct. In
fact, when things are hopelessly wrong there is
no resource left except for the manager to become
insane and hide himself in an asylum, unless,
indeed, he blows up the books and the cash with
dynamite, and leaves nothing for the auditor but
a few singed scraps floating about loosely in seven
adjacent townships. The bank swindle is getting
to be a very bard road to travel.
# # ■»
A blushing young creature went into a
Brisbane chemist’s shop and stated that' she
hungered after a tooth-brush. The shop was
crowded, so the suave young man behind the
counter indicated a box with about two dozen
brushes in it. Then there was a long interval
which was broken at last by a fresh young voice
that stated ; “ Oh, I’ve tried them all, please, and
I like this one the best. How much is it ?” And
she was quite surprised to see how mad the suave
young man became, and how another woman, who
also wanted a toothbrush, backed out saying that
she would call again in about 18 months.
# # *
Four people were fatally sunstricken last
Friday in the City of Sin, Sweat, and Sorrow—
Rockhampton. On the same day, in Sydney, one
Hebe sold (>7 bottles of whisky over one bar in
sixpenny nips. At Crookwell, N.S.W., also on
Friday, a 4ft. snake swallowed a 3ft. snake, but
this is hardly worth mentioning in a country
where people wouldn’t be surprised if you told
them that a 3ft. snake had swallowed a 4ft. snake.

Mias MTvnight, who suicided by jumping
overboard during the last voyage to England of
the Lusitania, had confided to some of her fellow-
passengers that she really didn’t care much for
the man whom she was being sent “ home ” to
marry.

# # *
The police raided a thieves’ haunt in Mel-
bourne the other day, and got into rather a tight
place in consequence, There were seven ladies in
an upstairs room, and the only means of escape
they could think of was to throw their clothes out
of the window. The officers burst the door in,
and then they began to realise the full dimensions
of the problem, but though they felt rather em-
barrassed they faced it boldly and eventually
pulled through. How they managed it has been
officially kept dark.
* * *
Henry Lawson, the poet, dropped in at
The Bulletin office for a moment last Saturday
just to mention that all the stories about how
Chinese cooks took cake-icing into their mouths
and then blow it over the cakes, were quite true,
as he had seen ’em do it at an hotel on the Western
line.

A Dunedin Scotchwoman’s “ hoosban’ ”
having been fined £lO, and locked up in default,
their friends and relations passed their blue
bonnets round and raised the money for his
release, The treasurer of the fund paid it over to
the gude wife with the remark that she would now
be able to restore her husband to liberty. “Na
na,” said she, “ 1 ken better than that. I’ll joost
keep the siller fur masel, and as fur Tamrnas —a
short while in chockey wull be vera benefeeshal to
his health. He needed a change, ye ken.”
# * *
A German sailor, who deserted from his
ship years ago and settled down and married an
Australian who has given him a family, hears
that proceedings are being taken for his arrest
and conveyance to Germany, and asks The Bul-
letin for advice ;
Please be kind enough as t-o inform me in your next
issue if I run any danger of being arrested and taken
back on board ship. I am now a married man with a
family and live happily in this glorious country, and it
would be a death-blow for myself and my wife and family
should any attempt bo made to put me back to that bar-
baric state of existence the very idea of which makes me
tremble. It is an unpardonable negligence upon my part
that I did not naturalise myself up till now, but which I
shall remedy by doing as soon as possible, if not too late.
I beg of you to be kind enough to give me, as soon as
possible, explicit information as to my very critical
position.
Our correspondent can, we believe, be arrested,
torn away from our countrywoman whom he has
married and his children, and removed to a Ger-
man fortress or hulk for half-a-dozen years.
Occasionally a British seaman deserts and marries;
his arrest means a reward to the police, and once
he is handed over to the Naval authorities he is
taken away from his home and conveyed to Eng-
land, his wife and children being left here to
starve. To their disgrace the Australian authori-
ties calmly submit to this kind of thing. Two
British deserters were the other day captured in
Maoriland and sent to England, their wives and
families being left behind to scratch out an exist-
ence as best they could. The German citizen who
now writes us will undertake considerable risk in
giving the required public notice of his intention
to be naturalised, as he will thereby bring his
name under the notice of the German authorities.
# # #
Probably one of the first cases to be tried
when the new N.S.W. Divorce Bill becomes law
will come from Queanbeyan. An ill-treated wife
will be the petitioner, her cause of complaint being
that on hot nights her husband deserts his lawful
couch and scoots off to the hay-loft, over the
stables, where it is nice and cool to sleep, and that
about 2 a.m., when the cold sets in, he comes back
to her side with his pyjamas full of hayseeds. It
is not so much his sleeping in the hay-loft she
objects to as the hayseeds. And she might have
tolerated them, but when she complained gently
about the annoyance the brute told her to “go
and leave her measure for a galvanised-iron night-
dress.”

A very well-known Brisbane magnate has
shaved his beard and now trots round with a
moustache only. Well, it was his own. He had
a perfect right to do as he chose with it. Never-
theless, ha ! ha ! Likewise, ho ! ho !
* * #
Haldane, Q.C., intends to introduce a Bill
next session to equalise the position of husbands
and waves when parties in the British Divorce
Court. A wife desiring a dissolution of marriage
now has to prove two offences, one of which—
cruelty—in some very gross cases cannot be
proved, while the husband has only to establish
one conjugal offence. One thing is, however,
clear : a woman knows for certain when she is the
mother of a particular child, but her husband
doesn’t know for certain w'hen he is the father
thereof, and upon this fact, which seriously affects
questions of succession, is based the present
custom of making adultery on the man’s part
only insufficient grounds for divorce.
* * #
Busts were out a while ago and hips in,
now positions are reversed. Such lightning dis-
placements of matter are more unaccountable to
science than any alteration in the earth’s surface,
and cause the uninitiated to look on a woman with
a what’s-real-about-you expression.

A deposit of ss. is a sine qua non of com-
petitions for the A.N. A. “Future of Australia”
essay, the prize for which is only £2O. It would
seem rather more just that the winner should
scoop the pool of deposits as his reward, for in
proportion to the number of competitors so is the
merit of the winning essay determinable. Tm
Bulletin predicts that when the A.N.A. essay
prize-winner is announced he will be a Tory-
Republican.

“Woman,” in Mrs. Lynn Linton’s opinion,
is taking an almost Junoic symbolism—grand,
supreme, all-embracing—while “lady” lias fallen
to the ground like an over-ripe plum, in the heart
of which the wasps have been at work. All-
embracing, in this connection, fits like a glove.

SOCIAL
A poetess named Miss Ham—not Ham-
mond Eggs, but only Miss Ham—publishes a
hundredweight of verse in the Christmas number
of the Queenslander, which is evidently in-
tended to appeal to the female love for the alleged
lancer and the policeman, and the mounted
hussar when he has got all his clothes on. It
starts thus :
She was a hero’s child, her father had fallen at Tel-el-
Kebir ;
And only a hero, she said, should ever be loved by her.
That is all the evidence. Possibly the old man
was walked upon by an ammunition-mule in the
rear, or was scared to death among the commis-
sariat, and it is likely that Tel-el-Kebir was the
last place on earth that he wanted to be in, and he
was only there because he couldn’t help himself;
and m any case he didn’t fall voluntarily, and to
be shot involuntarily with a gun at Tel-el-Kebir is
much the same as being shot involuntarily with a
brick in Woolloomooloo, but he was a hero all the
same. And when the Australian girl reads this
heartrending l bosh she sheds a sorrowful tear, and
forgets that her grandfather was boomeranged by
an aborigine, which amounts to just the same
thing, barring the horse and the banner and the
yelling and the big, vociferous drum.
* » #
The Austral Salon held a discussion on
“Humour” one evening recently, and in the
course of the proceedings the chaplain of the
French warship offered a few remarks in his own
language. It would have been $ll right only the
Salon took it for granted that he was talking about
humour, and laughed till it lost its voice and dis-
lodged all its back hair, and as the reverend
gentleman was merely thanking his friends for a
very enjoyable evening it was presently felt that a
hitch had arisen somewhere. When he had
finished there was an embarrassing pause. Some
of the members said “ Oui ” in an uncertain sort
of way, and some of them groped in the dim
recesses of their memories for some disjointed
scrap of the French they had learnt at school
and said: “ Avez-vous Ic can if demon ancle ?”
The kindly Frenchman felt in his pockets and
replied that he hadn’t got it about him, and then
the Salon got into a perfect agony of perplexity,
and, fixing its harassed eye upon the visitor, it
exclaimed: “ Polly-vans Frongsay .” It is im-
possible to guess what might have happened after
that, but luckily a male friend came to the rescue
and cried out; “A r ux vomica! Nil dcsperanduni!
Nihil fit! Casus belli! Fiat justitia mat caelum;
similia similibus curantur /” and these timely
quotations tided over the difficulty. The Salon
is now of opinion that it is rather backward with
its Hebrew, but otherwise its education is all
there.

Wolfe’s Schnapps—lt is grand.
A Townsville clerk, compelled to do lump-
ing-work under pain of dismissal, gave his earn-
ings to the Union.
Wolfe’s Schnapps has no rival.
Neil M‘Neii, one of the “ Hopeful ”
prisoners, died at Townsville the other day of
heat-apoplexy. Gaol and leg irons couldn’t effect
what freedom and bad grog speedily accomplished.
Wolfe’s Schnapps—a mild stimulant.
“The Earl of Euston is in a precarious
state of health,” says the cable. This is tele-
graphed out, probably, from the same motive that
prompts the cabling of the arrest of “Jack the
Ripper. ”
Buy Wolfe’s Schnapps by the bottle.
It is cabled from New York that the new
Assurance written by the Equitable Co., of the
U.S. in 1890, amounted to over £40,000,000 sterling
(the largest new business any Life Co. has ever
written in a single year), and that the assets
amount to over £24,000,000.
If asked to drink, call for Wolfe’s Schnapps.
Wherever Governor Hopetoun goes either
he or his suite) comes to grief. At his last cattle-
show experience the aide-de-kong resolved to be
affable to the degraded inhabitants, so he ap-
proached a wild-looking bush-gentleman and said ;
“ Haw, my deah friend, can you oblige me with r
match.” “You can have fifty flaming matches,
you gory beggar, and welcome,’’responded the son
of the soil cheerfully, and then while the high-
born person was staggering under this remark he
asked him to have a drink in such a blast of
friendly profanity that the officer dropped his cigar
and escaped.
A good bed companion—Wolfe’s Schnapps.
Guinness has been raised to the British
Beerage:—
Guinness, Alsopp. Bass, and Meux
Hobnob with the lords and dukes;
’Tis the custom to exalt
Men of hops and yeast and malt.
Why not (which would be a crusher}
Make an earl of Andrew Usher,
Or (as will be done perhaps)
Alter Wolff to Baron Schnapps ?
Advise your friends to drink Wolfe’s Schnapps.
The world has not yet heard the whole
truth about the Parnell-O’Shea divorce-case, and
there are very likely other reasons for Parnell’s
silence besides his guilt. In the past, there has
been no contemporary politician who maintained
over his tongue so complete a mastery as that
preserved by the Irish leader, whose memorable
silence in the House of Commons on the evening
after the Phoenix Park murders, when Chief
Secretary Forster fiercely denounced him as a
murderer, and challenged him to speak, alike
angered and astonished the British nation.
[f out’of sorts try Wolfe’s Schnapps.
“Nothing is more pitiable,” writes a well-
informed correspondent to The Bulletin, “than
the aspect of the Labour Party in England. They
have been kicked under the table by the classes
who are still kicking them, while they themselves
are gorging, and the poor devils underneath are
for tearing one another’s throats out! Hyndraan
and the Social Democrats, after a stormy rupture
with Burns and the Moderates, are playing a lone
hand, while the old organised Trades’ Unionists
are trying to down Burns and the other ‘ agita-
tors,’ Hyndman and his friends have been
charged again and again with being in Tory pay
to split the progressive vote, and there seems
something in the charge. Meantime, Capital is
organising with astonishing rapidity, and strike
after strike is failing.”
Wolfe’s Schnapps—for the liver and kidneys.
