The Referee.
Does anyone remember having heard of one
Henry Searle, who field the sculling championship of the
world sortie time ag<j? He has been dead just one year,
and is apparently as fully forgotten as if he had'beon
drowned with Pharoah’s hosts in the Red Sea. And, by
the way, what has become of * the money subscribed for
the once miioh-talked-of memorial monument on the
hank of -the, Parramatta River? Searle, senr., is very
lucky, indeed, tjiat it was not erected and the bill sent
alpng to him for settlement.
'*! * *
The great feature of the match N.S.W. v.
S. Australia w'as Alec. Bannerman’s barracking, which
created considerable amusement, especially as the little
man was accompanied by one of the best-looking damsels
in the sacred city. As for the cricket, Iredale and Blin-
nian showed the best batting form, Iredale being; voted to
be the coming cricketer. Ferris and Giffen bowled mag-
nificently, the latter with hard luck. The New South
Welshmen were great favourites over in Adelaide, and
enjoyed themselves immensely.
* * *
The “ Gladstone appeal ” case will give the
V.R.C. committee an opportunity to disentangle a couple
of stupidly-intertwisted racing rules. Rule 42 orders the
age of a horse to be stated at time of entry, whilst Rule
45 relieves owners of the necessity of giving a horse’s age
after the quadruped has once appeared on the Fleming-
ton course under a full and proper description. The
horse’s age is then supposed to be a matter of history in
all racing-club offices, and the clerk duly inserts it in the
entry, even if the nominator omits to supply it. Glad-
stone, a five-year-old brother to Sedition, was entered
for a race at Moonee \ alley without any age being
stated at the time. Mr. Cox’s clerk, in a careless moment,
put “aged” against the name of the horse, who, being
similarly described on the card, won the race. An objec-
tion \vas lodged, and the Moonee Valley stewards dis-
qualified Gladstone on Rule 42, and dead against Rule 45.
Naturally, an appeal has been made to the V.R.C.
almighties, who must inevitably reverse the former
decision. The latter Rule modifies the former, just as one
short codicil may nullify all the conditions of a thunder-
ing long last will and testament. The Bulletin ’3 a
whale for legal complications.
* * *
Under an Australian sun many things—fruit,
vegetables, men and women for instance—ripen over
quickly. It is not so, invariably, with scullers. Searle
and Matterson ripened early, but Beach, Kemp, M‘Lean,
like some_ of their predecessors, bloomed late. It now
seems evident, as was announced at the time, that
M'Lean should have defeated Kemp (The Bulletin tipped
him) the first time they met. Says Dan Mayne: “ When
lie was defeated by Kemp some time back he (M'Lean)
was not in anything like trim ; his trousers were all
patched behind, and his boots wanted soloing and heeling
besides, he used to hoard at sixpenny restaurants, at
some of which all the good has been cooked out of the
meat at large hotels before the hoarding-house pro-
prietors purchase it for their customers. Since being
taken in hand by the Spencers, Mac has been provided
with good clothes, boots, and bed, and well fed on fat
pork, taters and pudden.” Which, Daniel, is all tommy-
rot-. Mac. had been up the river for months living
high, and was just as well cared for, if not so well
boated, as now. The reason was a mental, not a physical
one. J
# * *
Starter Hagerty, at Moorefield, is not an un-
qualified success. He waits his grey steed backwards and
forwards in front of the field until afl the horses are toeiiv
the chalk-line and then drops the flag. As a rule he is
immediately in front of one of the horses, who has to go
round him, thereby losing all chance of a good start.
’• * *
A writer attributes the higher price given for
Mons Meg to the fact that the Musket blood is helm--
appreciated in England. As a matter of fact, the filly was
purchased by an Australian (Dan. Cooper), thomrii it was
the bidding of Britishers that made him pay so death Bu-
ms prize. By the way, Mons Meg, or “ Muckle Mou’d
Meg,” is the name of an ancient piece of ordnance one
of the “lions” of Edinburgh Castle.
* # *
The latest newspaper stupidity of yelling out
100 to 1 on an indifferent fighter simply because he
sprouted from the writer’s particular dumdiill received a
severe set-hack on New Year’s Eve at the Australian
Athletic Club (Sydney), when Mr. George Seale actinias
referee, humanely stopped the Baing-Suliivan fight and
awarded it to the Maorilander, who was fresh as paint,
while his foe was, dazed, weak, and despondent. They
fought 43 rounds in three hours, and were just dolin'- the
“ here’s a ban’, my trusty frien’, and gies a ban’ o’ thine ’
when the clocks struck twelve ; but, as a matter of fact*
had not Laing been over-careful the fight should have
been finished any time after the fifth round The
stranger is a well-proportioned half-caste Maori whose solo
physical weakness, apparently, is the shortness of his
arms. He fights in the crouching panther style with his
legs well spread out, his head on one side and a curious
smile on his face. Bring is wonderfully smart for a heavy
man (he scales 12st. 71b.) with both head and feet His
favourite dart was to punch Sullivan on the mark or in
the ribs with his left, and then shoot the right for the
point of the jaw, invariably drawing off then no matter
what eventuated. He got home so often that it seemed
wonderful that the Barrier pet’s head was not jerked off
hut he should have finished the business in the
fifth round, when Sullivan, silly and stupefied
from a couple of jaw-breakers, was at his mercy
The Maorilander, however, for once got Hurried
and let the show go by, hut Sullivan never had
the slightest chance afterwards. The Barrier man stands
about six inches higher than his opponent, hut is very
lathy and weighs about ten pounds less. During the
fight he did not get m r e than three blows of conseiWcncc
home, and seems to have been cured of his rushing pro-
pensities by the slogging administered Ivy Jem Hall tu-
be never attempted to fight, and simply stood up to he
belted. How he heat Hali ls a mystery. If the rumours
concerning the affair are incorrect, then the latter must
have gone weak with thumping Sullivan’s cast-iron mu ■■
The kilter may be a game man, but his reputation as'a
fighter, a newspaper-made one, is now completely dis-
pelled

i hat sterling mare, Yespasia, had terribly bad
luck in running second for two such stakes as the Summer

## ' *
Upon the Summer Cup running it would not
seem that “ touting” is of much value. Mr. Rouse, the
owner of Vespasia, said that it he had been asked
to ngme a horse which had no show of winning
he should, on the work done on the training-track,
have named the winner. Stockwell won easily, and his
people did not think he'had a ghost of a show, but when
they fancied him for the Carrington Stakes he finished
about 60 yards behind the field, and Brickey Colleys who
had the mount, declares him the worst horse he ever
crossed. All the trainers swore by Venapa, and declared
that with a fair nin she would distance the field, hut
although she was one of the first to leave the post and led
into the straight, Too Soon wore her down and won com-
fortably.

She started in three Selling races and won them a)l.

Joe Burton had Muriel, the winner of Tatt.’s
Cup, iir great form, and the wonder is she did not run
better in the Summer Cup. He was very confident,

said at the scales that the only horse he was afraid of was
Lancer.
V?
Melos has proved one of the most expensive public
horses ever foaled in Australia. Times out of number he
has been hacked for thousands, started first favourite,
and gone down. He ran very badly in the Summer and
Tattersall’s Cups, and if the stable fancied his chance it
does not say much for their judgment.
* # *
“Pendragon” asserts that glove fights are far
more brutal and dangerous than many mills in the old
style. This merely bears out an opinion lengthily ex-
pressed in Tub Bulletin over twelve months ago. Our
reasons for this conclusion were simply these : The gloves
increase the reach and punishing power. Protected by
them, the fist holds good much longer and does not
readily become useless from sprained knuckles, &o. The
naked hand produces a cut when strongly used, the glove
a bruise, and the latter is the worse. In the ring days a
man could slip down and end a round. Now he must
face his opponent, for the lOsecs. afford no relief. In the
old days he fell on grass. Under Queensberry rules he
goes down on hard boards.
* * *
The Breeder and Sportsman asserts that a
certain Australian has been busy buy ing trotting horses
for the Sydney market, which he is desirous of smuggling
over on the quiet or, if possible, without a true list
of performances.
* * *
“Seppy ” Stephen, who had charge of J. White’s
horses in England, says that £2OO, at the outside, would
cover the stable losses over these unfortunate noddies.
He says, amongst a variety of other things, that Dan.
Cooper doesn’t bet.
# * #
The Bulletin is not sorry at the determination
of the Sydney magistrates to put down the totalisator,
which is now being run in all sorts of places by all sorts
of people to such an extent that in some streets every
third shop is a tote in full blast. Women patronize the
machine extensively. They are born gamblers. But isn’t
the totalisator being put down in the bookmakers’
interest ?
# * #
George Ruwald, who beat J. M'Crae and A. P.
Sharland in a sculling race for a trophy over the champion
course, is an of the N.S.W. Postal department,
and is a long native of the Trickett order of architecture.
He rowed the mile in 6min 30sec. and the course in 23min.
19sec. with the tide against him.
* * *
1 'Alick Bannerman sticks in a whole innings, to
be sure, but what becomes of cricket, as a game, apart
from a business? Murdoch learnt this pottering from
Bannerman, and what enjoyment was it to see the pair of
them a whole afternoon slowly scoring at the wickets, or
perhaps Bannerman scoring about three singles per hour?
Why, this guiver is death to cricket, the life-blood of
which is afforded by hitters of the stamp of Massey,
Bonnor and Charley Bannerman. In short, people won’t
pay for Mr. Potter as a bat.
# * #
Payten’s stable was in great form on Boxing-
day, with three wins at Rand wick and one in Melbourne.
However, on the following day three of his horses started
hot favourites, but not one got home.
* * *
Young Fulford would appear to be the crack
pony jockey now. At Sydney Driving Park he rode four
winners, three of them getting home through his good
horsemanship. At Canterbury he piloted four to victory.
* * #
“Taken all through, the books had all the best of
the Randwick meeting, and they got almost a “ skinner ”
on the Summer Cup.
* * *
The American “ correspondent ” of a Victorian
sporting print commences his letter: “Since my last
favour I have been at death’s door.” It is characteristic
of sporting literature that a writer should describe Iris
own shoddy stuff as a “ favour.”
* # *
The dull monotony of a “mugs”-’ billiard-match,
at a certain Brisbane hotel, was suddenly enlivened by
Mr. Spotball knocking his opponent’s ivory clean off the
table. Whereupon Mr. Plainball, with a gleam of triumph
in his bloodshot eye, shrieked ; “Marker, what does the
have done to him for that ? ”

tended to spell for a few months, and then would meet

* # *
Mace sickened Sandy Ross’s seconds at Sara
Matthews’ hail in seven rounds the other night, and by
so doing violated his agreement with the Australian
Athletic Club, and the match with Maher has been declared
off.
* * *
Jem Hall, who lately married a Melbourne girl
(Miss Rose Thompson), was to have sailed for America in
the Monowai ; but, for the second time, he was dis-
appointed. An old flame started some very rough legal
proceedings, but made no appearance in court, and so
Hail was discharged. He turned up at the Australian
Athletic Club the other evening to second Sullivan,
attired in a bell-topper and frock-coat, looking as though
he’d been to America and come back a colonel, instead o*
only across the gangway. He goes by next boat.
# # #
A N. Y. homing-pigeon expedition released six
birds at Paris. One of them arrived home after a year’s
absence.
# * *
The word “ handicap ” is a corruption of the
term “ Hand-in-cap ” used from the drawing of lots, for
I position, out of a hat or cap.
# ■* *
The system of shoeing horses with cow-hide, in-
troduced about the time of the first great exhibition in
London, is again coming into vogue in Europe. This shoe
is much lighter than the iron one, lasts longer, requires
no caulks, never slips on polished pavements, and, it is
said, preserves hoofs from splitting.
# # #
Many members of the Agricultural Society of
N.S. Wales are not at all satisfied with the pony-racing
which seems the society’s main, if illegitimate, means of
revenue. Do the Government give giants on the pound
for pound system to money thus obtained?
# * *
Dutch went under to N.Z. Stephenson over
the Parramatta course for the second time by three
lengths in, the fast'lime of 19min. 58jsec. The tide
was ebb, the wind westerly, so the men had all in their
favour, and the time is second only to that, if we re-
member aright, of the Searle-Stanhury contest.

Bannerrnan made 117 in six hours at Melbourne
last year, this year it took him five and a-half hours to

* * *
There was some wonderful reversal of form at
Randwick, more particularly in the juvenile races. On
Boxing-day, in the Nursery Handicap, Pie Crust won,
running the five furlongs in 1.2 J, but on the next day she
ran almost last in the Lady Carington Prize, won in 1.4.
# * *■
Punters who laid odds on Pie Crust for the
Nursery Handicap on Boxing-day had a narrow squeak.
Eloquence had the race won at the distance, but, jumping
over a piece of newspaper, went out of her stride, and
Hales, riding a desperate race, got past in the last few
strides.
* * *
Since The Bulletin suggested that Lady Lovel
would be “ worth watching,” the mare has won several
races, starting in the first instance at 6 to 1.
* * *
The World, speaking of the sale of Mr. James
White’s horses in England, says “it was the universal
opinion that they fetched a good deal more than, judging
them by their looks, they were worth.”
# * #
Morris Jacobs, who has done very little good as
a racehorse-owner since he won the Victorian Derby with
Rufus, sold off his stud last week. Morris still works
away at his “book,’’.however.
# * #
The intercolonial cricket premiership is still in
abeyance. First N.S. Wales beat S. Australia by six
wickets; then Victoria beat N.S.W. by 36 runs; then
S. Australia beat Victoria by an innings and 62 runs. On
paper, S. Australia has somewhat the best of it, however.
Her glorious victory over Victoria was chiefly due to 237
of Geo, Giffen, 53 of J. J. Lyons, 50 by H. Blinmann, and
49 by J. Noel.
* * *
The Sydney Ladies’ cricket match seems to
have been chiefly remarkable for the fact that not one
ball in twenty would have hit the wickets had there been
no obstacles in its path. It was therefore not very
remarkable that the Misses Deane and Gregory, both of
cricketing families, should run their scores into three
figures.
# * *
One of the Sydney lady cricketers is Miss
Tumpowsky. “ Stumpoffsky ”is ominous.
* # *
The Bulletin congratulates the trustees of the
N.S.W. National Park on having so efficient a ranger as
Elias Laycock, a good man on sea and land, to look after
their interests there. The days of illegal netting and
dynamiting in Port Hacking should now soon belong to
the past.
* # *
Mr. Olliffe made a sad bungle in the judging of
the Auction Stakes at Moorefleld on Saturday. The
colours of Strephon and Margett were very much alike,
and Mr. Olliffe hoisted the number cf Margett as the
winner. A wild howl went up from the crowd, and it was
not for some minutes that the mistake was rectified, and
the correct numbers hoisted. Luckily for the judge,
Strephon won by fully a length, for had it been a close
thing, the crowd who backed Margett, the favourite,
would not have accepted the changing of the numbers
quite so kindly. Mr. Olliffe should take his mistake to
heart, and thank his stars he got so easily out of the
difficulty.

THE GREAT REMEDY FOR PAIN.
ST. JACOBS OIL.
WHAT IT IS.
SIX STANDARD VIRTUES.
1st. It is in one word a cure ; it is not merely a
relief, and in no sense a cure-all; it is the product
of scientific research.
2nd. It strengthens while it soothes and sub-
dues, heals, and cures ; it literally conquers pain.
3rd. Its effects are curative and permanent to
the whole group of muscular miseries and nervous
agonies.
4th. It does not. merely irritate the outer
surface, nor does it merely soften or relax a con-
stricted muscle. To its specific action a superior
curative virtue is supemdded.
5th. It penetrates deeply but gently; search-
ingly and surely seeking the pain spot in an effort
to conquer.
Oth. Each constituent of the formula has a
recognised intrinsic virtue to serve most surely
the cure of pain.
SOME AUSTRALIAN EVIDENCE.
The editor of that favorably known and pro-
gressive periodica], the Illustrated Sydney
News, writes that he “lias much pleasure in
giving the proprietors of St. .Jacobs oil his testi-
mony with respect to its wonderful curative
properties. The editor has suffered acutely with
chronic sciatica, and has found the greatest relief
by the application of St. Jacobs oil to the affected
parts, and has no hesitation in recommending it
to sufferers.”
The lady who edits the Band op Mercy
(Sydney), also states that she “can testify to the
value of St. Jacobs oil, as it is used in her family
with great benefit.”

Notice to Artistic and Literary Contributors
Strictly Orkhnal matter contributed to The Bulletin
■will be paid for. The Editor will be glad to receive and
consider contributions as under ; —
1. Original humorous or political matter, illustrated or
otherwise. (Drawings must he made in clean lines
of Indian ink on white paper or Bristol-board.)
3. Unpublished anecdotes of living celebrities, or people
■who ought to be celebrated.
3. Humorous poems (from eight to forty-eight lines pre-
ferred).
f*. Serious poems, of similar or greater length, cspccialii,
those suitable for pictorial illustration.
S. Short Australian or other stories, up to, say, 3000
words. About half that length, or even less, will
be preferred.
All Contributions must be Written Leuirly, on One
Side Only of the paper, and folded, not rolled. 'I he
name and address of the writer must appear, in all
cases, on the MS. itself, and not mereh on an accompany-
ing letter or paper. The Proprietors do not hold them-
selves responsible for Rejected Contributions; but, if
accompanied by a stamped and addressed envelope or
■wrapper, every effort will be made to return them.
The Editor does not undertake to enter into any
correspondence respecting rejected Manuscripts.

The cost of firing a single shot from the
British 110-ton gun is £353 for material and wear-

A proposal is made to change the name of
Homebush to Strathfield, and the latter to the
Great Northern Junction. There seems to be a
growing disposition to obliterate all the old Colonial
names—and invariably substitute imported ones.
Change Strathfield if you like, Mr. Eddy, but
leave us Homebush, and all its early associations,
before either railways or cattle-yards disturbed its
peace.
Maggie Flanagan, 13, set f re to the
Oxford Hotel, Maryborough (Q.), because she
disliked one of the boarders, and desired to give
him a foretaste of the hereafter! This case has
its parallel in that of the N.S. Wales buy who the
other day strychnined his employer’s soup,
burned down wheat-stacks and hay-crop, and set
fire to the store-room, “for fun.” Some philan-
thropist ought to bring this interesting couple
together and follow up their history on theRougon-
Macquart principle.

A big section of the press continues to
allude to the release of Arthur Rae, the Hay
N S.W. Shearers’ Union Secretary, imprisoned in
default of paying fines imposed upon him for
telling shearers to strike, as “an act of clemency !”
So far as Rae was concerned there was no clemency
about it; the Ministry were only clement to them-
selves, for if they had dared to keep Rae in prison
for a single hour over one month they would
have brought about a social cataclysm to which
the late strike would have been as the song
of a mosquito to the echo of a thnnderblast. In
manufacturing 50 or 70 offences out of Rae’s two
single acts, and ordering him to gaol tor 128
weeks instead of for two fortnights, the law
brought itself into hatred and contempt with every
intelligent citizen.
Now that a satisfactory strike balance-
sheet has been published by the N.S.W. Labour
Defence Committee, the daily newspapers, which
during the conflict industriously hinted, and in
some,cases boldly stated, that the strike-leaders
were receiving weekly sums varying from £(> to
£20 for their services, are, without exception, very
crestfallen, and, with one exception, too dishonest
to acknowledge their mischievous blunder.
Sydney Daily Telegraph admits, unreservedly,
that: “ the public accountants’ report will com-
pletely silence detractors,” and asserts that; “it
will surprise most people to learn the pheno-
menally small amounts that were paid as salaries,”
moreover talks in eulogistic terms of “self-
saeriflee, due care and economy in dealing with
the strike fund,” and ere escaping by a side issue
for slangwhanging purposes, states that: “Many
charitable committees and other bodies concerned
in the collection and distribution of contributions
might learn a valuable lesson from the balance-
sheet issued by the Labour Defence Committee.”

The Reverend Henry
Jordan, B.A.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Somewhere in the Seventies, I, a newly-
fledged medico, was acting as junior partner in a
practice in a thickly-populated English manufac-
turing district which we may call Westshire. The
work was hard, the pay was poor (a shilling a
visit, medicine included), and the people as rough
a set as one could wish to meet, but needs must
when necessity drives, and without some capital a
medical man can no more hope to enter the secure
haven of decent fees and select society than the
common or garden fanatic has of entering—ah,
well! never mind which place. However, I was
young and full of work, and I dispensed the
rhubarb-and-magnesia with an enthusiasm at
which I have often since shuddered.
Now it happened that my partner had a wife,
and that his wife usually had a curate—that is to
say, being an active young woman with nothing
particular to do, her superfluous energy found its
vent, as superfluous feminine energy often will,
in (iiiufch gossip and that eager enquiry
into the religious condition of all her poorer
neighbours usually known as district-visiting.
All this, of course, would have been somewhat in-
complete without clerical assistance (male), and
accordingly the curate for the time being was
always a welcome and expected guest at the sub-
stantial nine-o’clock supper which, the day’s work
then usually over, formed our principal meal.
He, on his side, finding both the fare and the
society a welcome change from the meagre board
and still more meagre conversation of three old
maiden ladies with whom he was by traditional
custom bound to reside, scarcely ever failed to
put iu an appearance. Accordingly, month after
month, a succession of curates came and went,
said their little grace, ate their little supper,
and every now and then departed to a less un-
regenerate district, a successor appearing in due
course.
The usual course of events varied little. Dixon,
my partner, and myself usually made our appear-
ance a little before nine, and we always found the
young evangelist installed in an arm-chair dis-
cussing with much unction the last sermon or the
lost souls of the benighted wretches who were
then trying to keep themselves and their
families on something less than ten shillings a
week, and who had neither time nor stomach
to think of their eternal salvation. During
supper we carefully saw that the conver-
sation took a mundane turn, and then we two
unconverted ones took our way to the snuggery,
where, with tobacco and whisky, we solaced our-
selves, leaving our guest and his hostess in com-
munion of the soul till half-past ten, after which
hour the three virgin ladies who took charge of the
curate’s morals never allowed him to be out. The
clergymen were all very mild young men, some
milder than others, but a typical “curatical”
mildness pervaded all. Their eyes were mostly
weak, their walk more or less a shuffle, and their
clothes built with clerical amplitude and dis-
regard of wrinkles. One we had who once, in an
unguarded moment, confessed to us a weak desire
to throw off the thraldom in which he was held by
the three virgin sisters aforesaid, so one night, in
the absence of my partner’s wife, we seduced him
into the snuggery, mixed his punch, and kept him
till midnight. But our efforts in the good cause
were unavailing. He was not allowed out for a
week, and then appeared with an expression which
plainly showed that he was more firmly under the
yoke than ever.
* * *
It so happened that L had been absent on
a short holiday, and on my return my partner was
called away on business. Being very busy, I saw
none, of the household till evening, when, on
making my appearance in the dining-room, Mrs.
Dixon greeted me with
“Do you know, we have a new curate?”
This did not particularly interest me, for they
were all the same, but I politely asked, “How do
you like him ? ”
“ Well,” was the answer, “ I am not quite sure.
He is different from the rest, but I have asked him
to come in as often as he can, for really there is
nowhere else for the poor man to go.”
At that moment a knock at the door announced
the arrival of the divine, and a tall man,
apparently about thirty, was introduced to me as
the Reverend Henry Jordan. During supper if
had an opportunity of noticing my new acquaint
ance more closely. Ho had a face almost im
possible to describe, since there was nothing
tangible to be seized on; in fact, the only
characteristic traits were a pair of piercing eyes,
spoilt by their extreme shiftiness, and a certain
restlessness of manner which might have been
due to nervousness. But about one thing there
was no doubt, and that was- his conversational
ability. He could talk well, and we soon had the
ball merrily rolling. At last, during a pause in
the conversation, he said ;
“Do you know, Mrs. Dixon, I shall have to
take extreme measures with those old tabbies.
They won’t give me a latch-key.”
I give you my word he called them “old
tabbies, ” the three respectable spinsters who had
watched over so many generations of curates.
Mrs. Dixon blushed, looked a little uneasy and
finally smiled, for after all they were dear friends
of hers, and not altogether ungiven to gossip. My
heart was ah-eady warming to the man, but the
worst was yet to come.
Supper finished, I rose, and prepared to enjoy
my well-earned pipe, but ere I could leave the
room the Rev. Henry Jordan caught my eye.
“ My dear fellow,” he said, “are you going to
have a smoke? I must come with you. I hope
you will not think me rude, Mrs. Dixon, but it is
an absolute necessity for me. Of course I only do
it under medical advice, but the fact is I suffer so
from nervous palpitation ” —and with a low bow,
a white hand pressed gracefully over the region of
the heart, as indicating the place where the
nervous palpitations were supposed to occur, lie
gracefully glided from the room. I did not stop
to look at Mrs. Dixon, but made all haste to the
snuggery.
“Now, then,” said the Rev. Henry Jordan,
lighting a well-seasoned briar-root and mixing
himself a stiff nobbier from the jar of Kinahan’s
best, “this is what 1 call comfort. That is the
worst of women,” he pensively continued, “they
want religion in season and out of season. Now
I like medical men —they generally take their
religion weak and their whisky strong.” Then
with a quick look at me and a laugh, “Don’t
misunderstand me ; it is true I am a clergyman,
but even clergymen require a little relaxation
sometimes.”
So we relaxed.
At half-post ten he rose to go. “ I am making,”
he said, “some little allowance for the prejudices
of the three ancient ladies with whom I reside.
In a new place, and when one has a reputation to
uphold, it is necessary to move carefully. But,
mark my words, I shall have a latch-key before
long. ”
And he was as good as his word, for abqut a
week afterwards, he said one night—
It is sometimes better to compromise matters
than to gain an absolute victory, especially when
one is a minister of the gospel. I have not got a
latch-key, but I have got this.”
And ho produced a huge door-key, some nine
inches long, which he deposited on the table with
a loud clump and a deep sigh of satisfaction.
In the meantime, many a good night in the
snuggery had succeeded the first, and they were
now usually prolonged into the small hours/ Each
I night after supper my partner and myself escaped
j with an air of conscious guilt, but the Rev, Henry
j Jordan paused a few moments, made the politest
| of adieux to his somewhat crestfallen hostess, and
[ walked away with the air of one proud of doing
I I)is duty.
His conversation was charming and full of a
large-hearted toleration.
“You two,” lie said to us one night, helping
himself to another nobbier, “say that you are
Agnostics. Brought up in the school of Huxley
and Darwin, you have taken these great men as
your guides. Well, it is not for me to say you are
necessarily wrong. Because I believe one thing,
is it necessary that 1 should be continually trying
to make you believe the same? No, my dear
fellows, there is good in all things. One of our
greatest authorities lias even said that he can
understand a man’s belief in Mahomet, and if in
Mahomet, why not in Huxley ? It has always
been my boast that my entire sympathies are with
the Broad Church party. By the way, Dixon,
what was that good story you had about that girl
in Weaver’s Lane ?”
And yet, the next day, passing him in the
street when he was walking with his rector, I
could have sworn that I heard him say :
“As you were remarking, my dear sir, we can-
not be too careful about resisting the onslaughts
of a too-enquiring spirit. As you justly observe,
it is to the High Church party that belongs the
credit of preserving in its pristine purity our noble
religion.”
And the same evening, pausing for a moment
on leaving the dining-room to take something
from a rack in the hall, I heard him remark,
without a tremor in his voice, as he prepared to
follow :
“My dear Mrs. Dixon, you will, I am afraid,
think me very rude always to run away like this, j
but I feel sure that you understand my motives.
Men of scientific attainments such as your hus-
band are apt to look too much to the materialistic
side of life, and a few words in season from a
humble worker such as myself are often of benefit
to a mind in doubt. These evenings afford me the
only chance I have to exert any good influence.
Some day you will, I think, rejoice that I have
been given this opportunity. Good night, my
dear Mrs. Dixon.”
But that night we discovered to our horror that
there was absolutely no whisky in the house, the
fresh supply ordered not having arrived. Con-
sternation was expressed on all our faces.
Dixon said, “ Well I’m d— —d !”
I echoed the sentiment.
But the Rev. Henry Jordan, after thinking for
a few moments, pensively remarked,
“ I think I have heard that it is the custom of
you medical men to keep what you call absolute
alcohol, the purest form of spirit, in your surgery.
If I might venture to suggest it, possibly a small
modicum, with the addition, say. of a little
syrup of lemon, and perhaps a clove, might, with
some sugar and hot water, make a not altogether
unpalatable drink.”
Out spirits rose, for we had not thought of this.
I hauled down the necessary jars, and, with the
skill of a master, his reverence concocted the brew;
and many is the shilling I have since paid for a
nobbier of whisky that could not compare with
it.
# # #
In the meantime, there was one thing
upon which all the inhabitants of W were
agreed—that never had so estimable a man and so
splendid a preacher been known in the town.
Mrs. Dixon, sore as she felt at his desertion of
herself, was forced to admit that his equal in the
pulpit she had never heard. A neighboring living
falling vacant, it was at once offered to him, but
declined ; the grounds being, as he confided to me
in confidence, that “my views are too broad, my
dear fellow, and I feel that I must seek a wider
sphere of activity, perhaps beyond the seas, in
one of our great polonies or in America.”
80 far I had never heard him preach, having
neither the time nor the inclination to go to
church; but one night, impelled by curiosity, I
took a seat in time for the sermon. His reputation
was certainly not undeserved for he gave one of the
best sermons I have ever heard. Standing erect
in the pulpit, his restless eyes seeming to seek out
every member of the congregation in turn, he
preached without book or note and he preached in
earnest. There was no whine, there was no pray-
ing, but a clever and effective denunciation of every
vice dr weakness he could imagine before him.
Once towards the end I fancied that he faltered
for a moment, but immediately after the sermon
was over and the audience filed out, the usual con-
cluding hymn being that night unaccountably
omitted by the minister.
# # #
A quarter-of-an-hour afterwards I was
standing at the surgery door when I saw the
curate coming towards me. He carried a small
portmanteau and looked hurried.
“ Will you do me a great favour?he said. “ 1
am going into B by the night train and I do
not wish anyone to know. If you are asked will
you say that I told you I was going to walk over
to C ? ”
I looked him in the face and said, “ Yes.'*
He shook my hand and hurried away.
Five minutes afterwards, when I was still smok-
ing at the same place, two men came up to me.
“ The parson here?” they said.
“ What parson ?”
“ Mr. Jordan, as he calls himself.”
“No.”
“ Have you seen him ?”
“ Y r es.”
“When?”
“Five minutes ago.”
“ Where has he gone—which way?”
I heard the whistle of the train away down to
the left as I pointed to the right, and said, “He
is walking over to C ”
“Come along, Bill,” said one, “we have him
this time, shifty as he’s shown himself.”
But they were wrong, for they never saw the
Rev. Henry Jordan again.
How he got the warning which enabled him to
escape, or what he was exactly wanted for, we
never heard. Forgery it was supposed. All that
we knew for certain was that this was no clergy-
man at all, and that the real Rev. Henry Jordan,
whose name and degree had been borrowed, was a
modest curate residing in a remote corner of
Cornwall. His horror at discovering the liberty
which had been taken with his name is supposed
to have given him a shock from which he never
recovered.
Did Ido wrong ? For my part I think not, for
this 1 know, he was the only oarson I have ever
been able to get on with.
* # #
Some years afterwards 1 was driving
hurriedly in a cab to catch a steamer in one of the
capital cities of Australia. Suddenly I saw
coming along the footpath a tall figure in clerical
garb. It looked familiar to me from the first,
but, as it approached closer, there was no mis-
taking the expressionless face, with the piercing,
shifty eyes. He caught sight of me, there was an
instantaneous flash of recognition, and then the
cab bore me out of sight. I have never since set
foot in that city, but I have often wondered under
what denomination and under what name he is
delighting a cultured audience. Perchance should
he catch sight of this he may remember the night
that he brewed the grog in the doctor’s shop in
England.
GEORGE DANT.
Bishop Stanton announces his intention of
training shepherds at Newcastle for subsequent
transportation to N.Z.
W. G. Spence testifies that the A.S. U.
has been instrumental in lifting shearing-rates in
N.S.W. by at least 3s. 6d. per hundred, which
means an increase of £5 per annum per shearer.
It now transpires that the awful smash of
the Union Bank of South Africa was brought
about by one affable customer, who called round
and procured an overdraft of £554,455. He seems
to have put the money in his inside pocket and
gone away, and a few weeks later the bank closed
up for want of it.
The notorious Albanian bandit, Hadji
Tillman, before he was led to execution, expressed
a last wish to make a meal of curried mutton.
But as the Turkish cook did not understand how-
to prepare the dish, the culprit pushed back his
plate, saying : “ If I ate any of that stuff I should
have dyspepsia for a week at the very least.”
A London magistrate has been hauled over
the coals for inviting four young lordlings to take
seats on the Bench at the first hearing of the
Hampstead murder case. These beaks are terrors
for introducing the majesty of the Law to good
society. There was Panton-de-Beerthir.st, for
instance—but it’s time that that long slender arm
of justice had a rest.
Rev. James W. Ford, for more than half
a century a missionary in China, and alleged
founder of the first Christian church in that
country, says : “After studying the Chinese lan-
guage for ten years, I translated the New Testa-
ment and other works. I began to preach, in a
way, immediately after I entered Siam, but
though I have studied for fifty years, I do not yet
think that I have mastered the Chinese language.”
This should be immensely encouraging to the
uninstructed and commonplace persons who, with
a view to converting John Chinaman to Jay-
bullism, left Australia the other day by the Men-
muir.
Premier Parkes doesn’t know everything,
and he put his foot into it terribly when, in Par-
liament the other day, he quoted* Melancthon as
an upholder of divorce. Cardinal Moran points
out —an unquestionable and easily-ascertainable
historical fact—that Melancthon, while denying
that a man had any right to divorce his wife, on
more than one great occasion lent his sacred smile
to the act of taking a second wife while the first
was still alive. And now somebody points out,
for the benefit of Cardinal Moran, that the then
Pope’s share in the divorce of Henry VIII. from
Ids brother’s widow, whom he married by a Papal
dispensation, won’t stand examination from the
point of view of those who alike object to bigamy
and to divorce.
The Colonial Ice Co., Limited, are to be
congratulated on their phenomenal success and on
the firm and substantial support they are meeting
with. This result is to be attributed both to the
purity of the ice supplied and to their care and
promptitude in executing their orders daily. The
Company’s office and works are in Regent-street,
Redfern, and will amply repay a visit. Here can
be seen the manufacture of ice from its inception
and a glance into the enormous store-room
(capable of holding several hundreds of tons)
makes one feel as if he had collided against an
iceberg. The cooling-room is also a notable feature,
and is already largely utilised by butchers, &c.,
for preserving meat during the hot season. This
room is divided into compartments each with a
separate door, the tenant of each holding a key,
at a very moderate rent; a uniform temperature
is maintained and the cleanliness and absence of
all unpleasantness is most apparent. The present
plant and staff of the Company are taxed to the
utmost in fulfilling their orders, and it is the in-
tention to largely increase the accommodation at
no distant date to meet the increasing require-
ments of the trade.*

graduate as Doctors; then have their name en-
graved on a brass plate, placed outside of a door
in College-street.

to your office as one who understood your busi-
ness.” Doctor (smiling) “Let me see your
tongue. Hum; appetite good?’ “ First-class. ”
“Digestion all right?” “Yes. Digested Ja.ck
Want’s Sunday Bill, and one of O’Connor’s Pill-
her-boxes last night.” “ Good. Are you troubled
with sleeplessness?’ “Never; I am a Member
of Parliament.” “ Great Scott, what an affliction !

at any first-class hotel in Sydney : Sarsaparilla
and Ironwater, or St. Louis Lager Beer —both
grand tonics.”*

Tales of "The System."
No. XXV. (and Last).—Marooned on the
Grummet.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Rain in torrents, rain in sheets—and two
hundred convicts out in the storm. Huddled
together, with heads bent on their breasts so that
their faces at least shall be protected from the
deluge, they stand in the main barrack-yard as
they have stood for 30 minutes past and will con-
tinue to stand for another 120 unless Heaven or
its Sarah Island representative inclines to mercy.
And the cutting, salt rain dashes upon them and
soaks them through and through and leaves never
a dry stitch among the lot.
# * *
“Grand rain,” remarks Captain Bankes,
Commandant, as ho chalks his cue in the billiard-
room thrown out (at His Majesty’s expense) from
the gable-end of Officers’ Quarters.
“\fes,” rejoins Lieutenant Darrell, his oppo-
nent, as he asks Forger Joe, the marker, for the
score and prepares for his shot; “grand rain—
’ll do a lot of good. It’ll bring up that young
wheat in No. Nine paddock splendidly.” Ho
cannons., “If we get a small harvest on that
experimental crop I’d suggest—damn, what d’ye
mean, pris’ner, coming in like that?” (Ho has
missed his stroke.) “I’ve a good mind to give you
twenty, sir, interfering with my stroke!”
The intruding convict-servant salutes and
makes humble and trembling reply : “I knocked,
sir, I did. Thought you told me to come in, sir.
I came to tell you, sir, as Church was waitin’.”
“Ob, damn Church, and 1 don't believe you,
pris’ner. You didn’t knock, and if you tell me t o
again I’ll give you lifty for lying. Now, did you
knock ?”
The poor half-witted fool instantly impales him-
self upon the dilemma so ingeniously offered him
by Lieutenant Darrell.
sir—l didn’t knock.”
“ Then why did you say you did? You area
liar, then, are you? Come up for punishment to-
morrow morning and evening—a ‘dozen’ each
time. D’ye hear?”
The convict servant retires, knowing he is
wronged, and yet understanding, too, that lie has
blundered somehow. Lieutenant Darrell, whose
turn it is for Muster and Church duty, throws
down his cuo grumbliugly, and dons his i belts.
Captain Bankes, glancing through the window,
resolves that ho won’t do inspection-rounds to-day.
The grand rain shall not fall to-day upon the Just,

J ONES: “Hullo, mate! Things seem better with you than they were.”
SMITH: “By Jove, they are, old chappie. Starving in Sydney, I came to
Protectionist Melbourne, got a good billet, live well, and as I
buy my clothes at Crawford, King & Co.'s wholesale store, I can
turn out decently for about three notes.
It must content itself with the Unjust.
“ 1 won’t inspect this morning, Darrell. Live
’em short Church and half the Regulations, and
let “ Complaints ” stand over. Come back quickly
and we’ll finish this game before lunch. \V hats
the score, marker? ”
“ Forty—thirty-eight, sir,” respectfully answers
Forger Joe, “ And, bog pardon, Mr. Darrell, but
Birch did knock, sir. 1 heard him.” .
“ What’s that ? What right have you to inter-
fere? Out to Muster, my man, at once! Wo
don’t own any favourites here, and don’t permit
shirking by the billiard-room fire when Church-
H J
parade’s on.”
And out,in the storm of rain and sleet, from his
snuggery in the billiard-room, went Forger Joe.
Though the outer world was bleak and damp,
nevertheless Forger Joe should not have come to
the r solution which he then and there did. If a
man interferes on behalf of truth ami justice, he
should not be surprised at receiving the reward
customarily granted by society for such conduct.
Those who bemoan the thorns of the martyr’scrown
should not commit the deeds which win them a
place in .the martyrology. And Forger Joe,
though he did not regret that he had made his
interjection in the interest of billet-servant Birch,
I lamented in his'heart the loss of the warm shelter
in which ho had expected to pass the Sunday.
« •
For, of course, you will have understood
by this time that this particular day was Sunday.
Intensely solicitous as the System was for the
religious and moral development of its Children,
it stands to reason that it could not spare time for
prayers except on Sundays. And so devoted to
duty were Messrs. Bankes and Darrell that by the
time they had got through their week-day round
of supervision and chastisement, they were, as a
rule, too weary even for billiards. Thus their
combats with the cue were generally reserved for
the Sabbath-day.
Before muster and after lunch on Sundays, the
click-click of the balls was wont to sound merrily
from the square-roofed annexe to the barracks,
and Forger Joe had been greatly envied of his
brother-felons. The opportunity of witnessing
pretty play between the superior officers of the
Settlement was an inestimable privilege, to say
nothing of the cigar-ends that Captain Bankes —
not Darrell, you may be sure!—was so forgetful
about; or of the circumstance that the marker was
“ free of Muster.” Not that he was exempted by
Regulation. That could hardly be, inasmuch as
billiard-tables and markers wore unknown to the
King’s Regulations. He was permitted to absent
himself by grace of the Commandant or the
Lieutenant. Which quality generally superseded
the Regulations when it was a matter of meeting
the officer’s convenience.
No, Forger Joe did not feel pleased when, by
the fiat of that Angel of Justice, Darrell, he
was driven from the Paradise of the billiard-
room to the outer world where there was gnashing
of teeth and shaking of chilled limbs. And the
devil coiled himself down into Joe’s breast, and
doubtless laughed gaily.
* * *
The rheumatic tremors which had seized
the majority of the “congregation ” in “ Church’’
momentarily gave place to a thrill of genuine

excitement as they saw Joe emerge from the
billiard-room and slouch over the soddened gravel
to join them.
“What’s up?” went round in eager whispers.
“ Joe’s in for it!” A score or two wretches
instantly made up their minds to fill the marker-
ship, vice Joe, dismissed, if they could.
* * *
The muster began. Under the eaves of
the narrow verandah stood a row of soldiers.
Behind these the mustering Overseer, other
subordinate officials, and that great man, Lieu-
tenant Darrell. Out in the rain, the convicts.
The rain plashed and splashed as the voices
rang out in call and ansvver. By number, and
name and ship was each convict called, and a
simple '‘Here !” from the man himself, when the
man was present, constituted the response. When
the man was not present, if his whereabouts were
known, a warder answered for him. If they were
unknown, then the presumption was that he had
escaped, and “ Absconded ” would be pencilled
opposite his name.
The absent prisoner might still be within Set-
tlement boundaries ; he might be away on some
officer’s message, the answering warder being in
ignorance of the fact; he might actually be an
absconder; but whatever was the occasion of his
absence it would be all one in the result. Down
would go “ Absconded !” opposite his name.
Now at this period, and for years after, for that
word to be placed over or against one’s name was
to be literally under sentence of death.
£'<
The muster continued somewhat in this
way :
“Bond, John, 440, Asia?”—“Here!”
“ Fulbert, Erie, 12,930, Marquis of Hastings?”
“Billet ” (from the warder).
“Gordon, Charles, 17,211, Pestonjee Boman-
jee ?” “ Gaol—condemned cell. ”
“Irvine, William, 103, Ocean?”—“Gaol- Hos-
pital.” (Irvine was suffering a recovery from a
flogging.)
“Jones, Robert, .9439, Sussex ’’’--“Refractory
cell, Grummet Island.”
“Judson, Joseph, 11,889, Royal Sovereign?”
No answer. Only a movement of heads.
“ Call him again,” shouted Darrell. And once
more, overbearing the rush and dash of the
incessant rain, Joseph Judson’s name was called.
Again there was no answer. Again there was
no sound except the falling rain and the rustle of
heads craning and moving sideways to catch a
glimpse of Forger Joe.
“Call him, the third time,” said Darrell, “and
if he fail to answer, report him as absconded.”
For the third time, in a still higher key, the
Overseer uttered: “Judson, Joseph, 11,889,
Royal Sovereign,” and still Joseph Judson, alias
“Fprger Joe,” was mute. Unaccountably so!
One would think Joe really desired to entrust
himself to the tender mercies and polite ministra
tions # of Brother Dougherty, the Hobart Town
hangman.
“Joseph Judson, absconded, ” cried the Over-
seer, and turning to Lieutenant Darrell he con-
tinued : “I report Joseph Judson, No. 11,889,
ship Royal Sovereign, as absconded, sir. Usual
instructions ? ”
“Usual instructions. Inform the guards. If
the absconder is found within the bounds, chal-
lenge once, and in default of immediate surrender,
fire. If without the bounds, fire.”
1 “ And then challenge,” said a voice in the felon-
throng, “Scarborough fashion !”f
“ Who said that?” repeated Darrell. For reply,
the throng opened, and closed again, and Forger
Joe, who had taken his place on the right wing,
was now standing in the centre of the crowd.
Darrell stepped into the front—out into the
heavily-beating rain. His face bore the look
which nearly every convict dreaded—the look of
irresponsible power mad for theassertion of its rights
and lustful of revenge ; such a look as John Price
used to wear in the ’Forties and ’Fifties—as he
wore the day he was killed at Williamstown Pier.
“Who said that?” Darrell asked again. And
then a prisoner—one of the aspirants for Judson’s
billet—whimpered : “ Forger Joe, sir.”
“Forger Joe!” —the Lieutenant admirably
simulated a start of surprise. Forger Joe—
Joseph Judson here? Joseph Judson, the
absconder? Ah, I see him!” With that, he
drove himself, wedge-like, into the massed con-
victs, and, seizing Joe by the collar of his coat
pulled him from their midst to the shelter of the
ranged muskets. So rapid was the action —it
seemed as.though it had taken place between the
falling of one rain-drop and of another—that the
prisoners did not realise its happening till they
saw Joe lying in the puddles by the soldiers’ feet.
“It was you, was it, Marker Joe?’ said the
officer. “You found your voice, then, did you ?
You couldn’t answer the muster-call, though!
Don’t you know, you fool, what your silence
means ? Don’t you know you’re an absconder-
condemned to death ! That’s what you’ve got for
sulking, my man!”
Forger Joe tried to rise. He was prostrate in
a couple of indies of water, and the overflow from
the eaves fell copiously upon him. At a signal
from Lieut. Darrell a couple of guards thrust him
back with the butts of their muskets.
“ Keep him down!” ordered Darrell; “ bayonets
downwards, and, Overseer, proceed !”
They finished the calling-over, Convict Judson
cooling his temper the while in the puddles and
underneath the steel-points.
“Any complaints?” called the Overseer. A
dozen hands were thrust forth.
“ Complaints must wait till next week,” said
Darrell. ,
Then they had prayers.
Dor, you will remember, this little episode
occurred in “Church.”
* * *
Half-an-hour later Lieutenant Darrell re-
ported to Captain Bankes, who was seated by the
billiai’d-room fire reading from a twelve-month-
old copy of the London Magazine.
“Ah, Darrell, back ?”
“ Wsir. ’Report, sir , ”
“Oh, hang report. Just listen here. It’s a
letter from that fellow, James Elia to Judge
Field. Delightfully rich ! Wonder how the
Mancipists ’ll like it !” And Captain Bankes,
who was careful to keep up his reading in the in-
tervals of flogging prisoners, and prided himself
equally upon his literary taste and his capacity for
making the System pay, extracted the plums from
Charles Lamb’s communication to Barron Field.
“ I cannot imagine to myself whereabout you are. . . .
Sometimes you seem to be in the Hades of Thieves. I
sec Diogenes prying among you with his perpetual fruit-
less lantern. What must you be willing by this time to
give for the sight of an honest man ! You must almost
have forgotten how we look ”
“That’s deuced insulting to the Staff,” said
Darrell. “That damned scribbler forgets there
are English gentlemen holding official positions in
the colonies.”
Bankes went on :
“And tell me what your Sydneyites do? Are they
th**v ng all day long ? Merciful Heaven ! what property
can stand against such a depredation ! ... Is there
much difference to see between the son of a th**f, and
the grandson? or where does the taint stop?”
“Splendid !” exclaimed the Commandant. The
Lieutenant grinned a“Ha ! Ha ! That shot’ll go
home ! ”
“ That’s more than the ’mancipists can do,
then,” chuckled the Superior as he ended his
quotations :
“Do you bleach in three or four generations ? Do you
grow your own hemp? What is your staple trade, ex-
clusive of the national profession, I mean? Your lock-
smiths, 1 take it, are some of 30U1* great capitalists.”
“’Gad!” exclaimed Bankes, throwing the
periodical down and leaning back in his chair to
laugh more at ease, “That’ll do for Field in the
old town. He won’t write many more ‘ Botany
Bay Flowers ’ there, I should say !”
But that’s not half a bad hint about the hemp,
sir,” suggested Lieutenant Darrell, in all serious-
ness. “ And I don’t see why we shouldn’t make
a trial of it here. I feel sure we’ll never succeed
with gram, and we really ought to cultivate more
ground. Now, there’ll always be a big demand
for hemp in the colonies.!
. <£ Fll think of it,” replied Bankes, and, to anti-
cipate a little, we may say that in the following
year No. 9 paddock was sown with hemp. The
crop, however, fortunately for Charles Lamb’s
fame, was a failure. Nature did a lot to accom-
modate the System. Tommy Townshend, after-
wards Lord Sydney, was quite convinced there
was a Divine leading ”in the discovery of the
Australias, just yvhen Howard had kicked up such
a row over the condition of British prisons ; and, in
the words of Colonel Sorell, “Providence must
have designed Macquarie Harbour for a prison.”
Governor Arthur said much the same thing res-
pecting Tasman’s Peninsula generally and Port
Arthur in particular ; and John Price was almost
equally as emphatic in his approval of the wisdom
which had so completely isolated Norfolk Island.
Nature (in the opinion of the System) was partial
to it, and yet, singular to state, she drew the line
at hemp. To this day the Australias are obliged
to import their hangmen’s ropes.
* * *
Commandant Bankes, having thus inti-
mated that he would keep an eye on the desira-
bility of meeting the System’s requirements, asked
for the Report.
The Report was the document which reflected
the_“ strength ” of the Settlement and the dis-
position of tlie prisoners and guards.
When he came to the column headed “Ab-
sconded since last report,” he was surprised into
“ Hello ! Forger .1 oe bolted ! The beggar lost no
time. I s’pose that’s because you ordered him to
muster.”
“Look at next column, please, sir,” said
Darrell complacently. For, of course, Darrell,
being a very exact book-keeper and a model of
method, had also entered Joe’s name in the
“absconders recaptured since last report ” column.
“Ah ! That’s good ! You lost no time either.
Now, what shall we do with him ? Of course, it’s
death.”
“Yes, it’s death. I’ve put him in the Con-
demned Cell.”
“ So I see, ’ the Commandant replied, for he had
noticed that the “Condemned Cells ” column had
been increased by one. The total was now 7 ;
yesterday it was only G.
“Those cells are getting crowded, sir,” re-
marked Darrell. “Shall we hand him over to
Skeleton Johnny here,)! or wait for the schooner
and send him to Dougherty 7 ? She won’t be down
for a month yet.
“ H’m ! Ah! Don’t know, I’m sure. You see,
Darrell, it doesn’t do to hang too many here. An
odd one now and then doesn’t matter when the
Cyprus is away and the gaol’s full, or even half-
a-dozen in an emergency; but we gave Johnny two
last month, y’know. No; I don’t think we’ll
hang our marker this time. Suppose we maroon
him? Three months at the Grummet’ll be a
change from his billet here.
“It’s as you please, sir.” And in this solemn
manner as suggestive of the majesty of law as of
the incorruptibility of justice, was the fate of
Convict Joseph Judson, alias Forger Joe. No.
11,889, per Royal Sovereign, decided.

It pains us to have to record that the event
(Continued on page 24.)
'This is not sarcasm. Dogherty, or Dougherty - the
former spelling follows the Gazette, the latter of his
death-warrant—was the most gentlemanly of the many
functionaries who in the Australian provinces have
politely invited their fellow creatures to step out and be
hanged. One man round whose neck Dougherty had just,
knotted the rope informed (after his reprieve on the
scaffold) the late excellent T. .1. Crouch, of Hobart, that
‘‘a lady couldn't have done it nicer,” and this person’s
judgment should be regarded as decisive, as on two former
occasions he had been the recipient of delicate attentions
from an executioner. It is pleasing to know that
first-hand testimony corroborates the statements of two
distinguished historians, the Reverend John West and Mr.
James Bonwick, respecting this useful and genial official.
“There was no amateur gaiety in his (Dougherty’s)
manner,” writes Mr. West; “no harshness in his speech.”
And Mr. Bonwick says : “ It was his trade to give the lash
and tie the last knot; but he did the one with gentleness
and the other with decent solemnity. ... He sought
to drown the remembrance of his professional duties’ in
the cup of intoxication ; the day following an execution
he always spent in a drunken stupor. Disgusted with his
trade he contemplated retirement from public life ; but the
multiplicity of applications for Ids position restored his
sef-respect, and reconciled him to the degradation of his
ojjice.” The italics are mine, not Mr. Bonwick’s. Dougherty
did eventually retire from public life—suddenly.
t “Scarborough fashion’’—First knock a man down
then bid him stand.

dip of ink with which he had just written “Let him be
hanged ” against a criminal’s name.
||“ Skeleton Johnny’’—“Jack Ketch,” or the hangman.
“ To give him to Johnny ” was to hand a man over to the
hangman.

was calculated to give the lie to Shakespeare. The
Commandant's deed of mercy doubtless blessed
the doer, but it certainly did not bless the re-
ceiver. Jhe dew of Heaven never fell upon a rock
more obdurate than the heart of Forger Joe.
He proved himself distinctly insensible to Captain
innu 8 lna o na -nimity in reprieving him.
When they came on the Monday morning to
remove him to the boat which was to bear him to
the Grummet Rock, the islet in the Harbour
where the Administration was wont to lodge
refractories,’lf and informed him of his destina-
tion, he stormed and raged. It was just like that
; " card Harrell that it was, to revenge himself
in that mean way. What the devil did they think
he d shut Ins mouth for at Muster if it wasn’t to
be sent to Hobart Town as an absconder under
sentence of death ? He was tired of this hole, ho
was, and wanted a change. Bankes and Harrell
were afraid of his letting a little light in on their
proceedings, and that’s why they objected to his
getting a change to the city. They didn’t want
J”?®. Hobart Town people to know about their
billiard-room and their women. And more im-
potent rigmarole to the same effect.
Speech of this kind was not only impolitic, but
absurd and wicked. Impolitic, because an intelli-
gent man like Forger.loe should have reflected
that Messrs. Bankes and Darrell’s administrative
proceedings would be certain to meet with
approval from the Authorities, and that being so,
the two officers need not care the snap of a finger
for the trumpery public opinion of the capital.
Absurd, because the billiard-table had been
charged by the military chest to the Settlement
not as a billiard-table, but as a comprehensive
assortment of commissariat stores, which in-
cluded boots and shirts, yellow-and-black suits,
leg-uons, handcuffs and basils, Sydney meal and
West India rum. ( English gentleman on the Staff
were given to little dodges of this sort). And
wicked, because, though Messrs. Banks and
Harrells Unisons with the female transports were
unblessed by the Church, they had nevertheless
been favoured with the sanction and example of
Governor Scroll.
* * *
He was landed on the Grummet at mid-
day- fettered and manacled. The boat, however
carried his handcuffs back to Sarah island The
chains they loft him. The stock of “darbies”
was rather low, but of chains the Establishment
had a very ample supply. It would not matter
very much if he did throw his leg-irons into
the sea.
With the body of Forger Joe the boat-overseer
deposited on the rock the material nouishment for
its sustenance during the period of the sentence.
As follows:
1 bag maize-meal.
1 bag salt.
1 keg of water.
X blanket.
1 pannikin.
1 Bible.
The bag of meal weighed (ii)lbs. ; that of salt,
11,,1 b5.; the keg’s contents measured 12 gallons,
these quantities were to keep the life in Convict
dud son for the period of 92 days for which he
was to be marooned, and were computed accord-
ing to the gaol-ration scale of 12ozs. meal, 2ozs.
salt, and 1 pint of water daily. You will observe,
it you take the trouble to check the calculation of
the Settlement commissariat-clerk, that he Aas
liberal in the matter of water. Water at the Set-
tlement cost nothing, or less than nothing, to pro-
cure : only the groans and sighs of the whipped
cask-earners ; hence the System could afford to be
liberal. It was not quite so generous with respect
to tno meal and the salt, inasmuch as it weighed
the “bag in,” and the convict was therefore
W™ an equivalent to one day’s ration.
Which the System, in its peculiarly playful
fashion, did not think a point worth consideration
Supposing a prisoner did go for a day without
food—if meal and water mixed could be termed
food—it was only a fast, and fasting was good for
the soul; and that the System did not neglect the
maroon s soul is proved by “ 1 Bible.” If, on the
other hand, he objected to fast, he was at liberty
to eat the bag. And then the System would flo"
him for destroying Government property.
* * #
Furnished with his supjilies, Forger Joe
waded through the surf and was welcomed by his
fellow-maroon, Robert Jones, No. 9439, per Sussex.
“Hello, Joe ! old boy, wot brought yer ’ere?”
“The boat,” answered Joe, grumpily.'
“So I see. But don’t yer think as yer’d better
be perlite, Joe? Yer out o’ favour with the
Com dant’ or yer ’udn’t be ’ere, so yer needn’t be
tofhsli, Joe.”
The new-arrival failing to reply, the affable
Jones continued : “We’ve ter be mates, Joe
for nother three months at least—’ow lonir’r ver
fur?” ° J
Joe seated himself on his meal-bag and, seizing
a loose piece of rock, began to “oval” his chains.
T 1 ’’ 'I Ol }° S ' ar ff umellt was not without force. As
they had to be mates, their relations would not
bo worse for a display of. cordiality on his part,
so, thawing, he told Jones of the circumstances
leading to his marooning.
“Ah! I thought they ’udn’t give yer less’n
quarter quid. An’ now, matie, I’ll do th’
onners. ”
* # *
With the graciousness of a well-mannered
host, Jones showed Joe over the rock—first' assist-
mgto free Joe’s feet from his chains. Not'a very
u when there was ample rock about.
Ere s yer quarters, Joe,” he said, pointing to
a wave-worn recess a couple of feet above high-
water-mark. “Yer won’ be so bad, ’cepting
wen the sou’-wester’s on. Then the sea’s over
ev ry d inch o’ th’ rock. Yer get a ’eap o’ the
sand up, wrap yer toes in yer jacket, an’ yer body
in th blankut, an’ smuggle down into the sand,
an there yer are, as right as tuppence. An’ ver
kin sleep as long’s yer like. There’s no mornin’
beli. .Leastways, as long’s the waves ’nil let yer,
an there s no sou’-wester more’n three or four
times a-week. No mornin’ bell, Joe, an’ wot’s
better, .Joe, no silunse bell either. Sumtimes
though, w’en the winds off-shore, an’ I’m tucked
up in the cave, I jist ketch the sound o’ the
silunse bell. An’ then wot does I do, d’ye think’”
“ What?” queried Joe interested, and anxious
to loam the ms and outs of life on the Grummet.
I gets up, 1 comes out, I ’alloos and shouts at
th’ top o’ my voice, I defies ’em tor silunse me, and
I puts my fingers ter my nose and tells th’
orthoritfea ter go to th’ devil. Nex’ day, Joe, I
don t mind th waves, even if they does go over
th’ rock. Fur I’ve ’ed my larf at th’ Systum, Joe.
And, Joe, m’ boy, that’s wuth ’eap's ! ”
* * #
One week passed, two, three in this com-
panionship. The marooned mates had their laugh
at the System together when over the. waves
sweetly stealing into their ears with the rustle of
the fresh land breeze, there came the silence bell •
together they bore the heat of the day, and the
dreadful burden of the stormy nights'when the
sea-horses dashed their manes of foam into their
sleeping caverns; and when the elements shrieked
and roared about the rock- as though the lost
spirits of that other world so faithfully repre-
sented on earth by Macquarie Harbour Settle-
ment had m very truth forced their way through
Hell s Gates; together they mixed their cold meal
and water, and together they nourished their
shrunken carcases upon it; together they rose to
salute the pilot-boat as it flew by on patrol duty ;
together they shook each other’s clenched fists at
it as it disappeared behind Bloody Point—it was
not safe to do so till she had rounded the cliff, for
the boat-constable had a good glass, and the
A run no strati oll was apt to view impertinence
with displeasure ; together they laughed and sang
when they felt their freedom ; together they cried
amd cursed, and together they trembled and
shivered when the angry sea drenched them with
us spume and isolated them with its horror-
together they mocked Heaven, and together they
read the Bible.
Bor three weeks, then—
One night, waking from a dream of love and
light—-a phosphorescent gleam from a midnight
wave had touched his eyelids and brought him to
consciousness—Borger Joe, over the bulwark of
stones which he had erected in the mouth of his
cave, saw the figure of his mate outlined against
the grey walls of the sky.
He rose noiselessly, and watched Jones turn
over the small rock with which they had closed
the recess where they kept their meal-barns. Into
his—Joe’s—bag went Jones’ hand ■ then ‘ into bis
own. Once, twice was this done. Then Jones
returned to his cave to sleep the sleep of the
innocent.
* * #
At least; he said he was innocent when, the
next morning, Borger Joe taxed him with tho’deed
winch was not so much a conteniptib’e theft as a
deadly treachery.
“ We’ll soon sec, ’’ said Joc. “ We’ll measure
And ration by ration Joe weighed out Ids
meal in Ins hands. He had emptied the bag on his
jacket, and as ho balanced each handful replaced
it in the canvas pocket.
i da y s - Twenty rations gone—72
should be left.” '
There were not GO. Jones must have been at
the business of transfer for several iiGhts Of
course he would not take too much at once. "
You’ve been living well, Jones ! I thought
you were keeping up flesh remarkably well
Vonre a nice ’un now, aint you ? To starve me—
that was your little game. Or make rao signal the
boat for more tucker, and get as an apoctiser
with each new ration, twenty lashes for extrava-
gance. Now, Jones, the Grummet’s not big
enough fer you and me. We’ll toss up who "oes
over to the white fins. ” r . j,
Convict Jones demurred to this proposition, and
declined to submit his precious life to the hazard
ot the die. He put his objection into the shape
of a blow-. They grappled—wrestled—Borger Joe
knew a trick or two with his heels and threw Ids
teJlow-maroon on his back. And Jones’ back lav
on the two meal-bags, which, under the pressure
spurted out their contents over the rocky e<Go to
water-level. Very literally had they cast their
bread on the waters.
Convict Judsqn took his knees from his mate’s
chest, and Convict Jones arose, saving :
. “I’ll toss now. As well be "thrown to the
sharks or auged, as starve ’ere tor death or bo
hogged ter bits. iN-Ty curse on yer, Iforger JoeI J>

Joe prepared five smoothish pebbles. On
one side of each he marked, respectively, with the
point of another and sharpened stone :
X XX XXX xxxx xxxxx
“ Why don’ yer put Aggers ? Wot’ro puttin’
kisses fur ? ”
“They’ll do. ’Sides, we’ll both be kissed short'v
by Death.
“Yer funny,” said Jones.
“ Highest out of three throws kills,” suggested
Joe.
Jones nodded.
Jones took his cap and shook the stones together.
Draw,’ said he, “for first throw. Lowest ’as
it.”
Joe, looking over the sea, dipped his hand in
and drew the three. Jones drew the four.
Joe, with a murmur, clasped the five bits and
tossed them up. They fell, chinking,-three
marked, two blank ;5,1, 4 ; total, 10. His second
cast was (i; his last, four blanks and 1.
“Luck’s out,” he said, “You’ll beat that
17 easily. ”
“ Orter,” said Jones. But he threw 9 and 7
and then faltered, “ Sixteen, too ! ” Bor the first
time, lie quavered.
“You’ll throw four blanks—see if you dont ”
laughed Joe.
Hp the stones went propelled by a very shaking
hand. And, true enough, four blanks did fall.
But the faced stone was two. Eighteen !
“I’ve won, I’ve won ! ” he shouted.
“Yes,” rejoined Borger Joe, “the Devil gene-
rally looks after his own.”
* * #
Joe went into his cave. When he came
out again he had his Bible with him. “ I’ll be
ready in a few minutes, Bob.” And, seating him-
self calmly, he read a chapter.
“I’m ready, Jones,” ho said, when he closed
the Book. “Look, the white fins are waiting!”
He spoke the truth. Two vultures of the deep
were sheening their fins within musket-shot.
You re funking, Jones. I’ve no objection to
make it suicide just to oblige .you
And Jones, evidently disliking the privilege lie
had won, assenting mutely, Zorger Joe “ made
it.”
Nevertheless Captain Bankes hanged Convict
Robert Jones, No. 9439, Sussex. But it is doubt-
ful whether it was on suspicion of Judson’s murder
or for destroying two meal-bags. Most likely it
was the latter : Bankes once hanged a man for
flattening out a tin-pannikin.
* * *
The pilot crew caught a shark a few
months afterwards. There was no evidence that
the man’s jaw found in its interior was Joe’s, as
several other Children of the System had pre-
ferred the white-fins to the Grummet.

* “ Weighed out: ” Old convicts, to whom an ounce of
meal or meat was a thing of importance, developed so
extraordinary an accuracy of discernment that they could
gauge to a quarter of an ounce the weight of an unboiled
ration, and almost to the same degree food that had

Verse We Have Read.
Bereaved.
lyet me come in where yon sit weeping—aye,
Let me, who have not any child to die,
Weep with you for the little one whose love
I have known nothing of.
The.little arms that slowly, slowly loosed
Their pressure round your neck—the hands you
used
To kiss. Such arms, such hands I never knew,
May I not weep with you ?
Fain would I be of service—say some thing
Between the tears that would be comforting,
But ah ! far sadder than yourselves am I,
Who have no child to die.
JAMES WHITCOME RILEY.
Christmas is over, and the tough, cold,
cheerless, ungainly Christmas fowl has ceased
from troubling for another year. Once every
twelve months there flits across the mental horizon
a species of spectral ostrich —a long, lank, leathern
bird, with blue legs covered with a shiny incrusta-
tion like smashed cockroaches, and a broken tail
that hangs down like the blown-up banner of a
defeated army ; and it is the purpose and business
t his torn and routed animal to diffuse peace on
earth in a general sort of way, and goodwill
towards men. lie is generally bought about a
fortnight before the day of execution, and in the
interval ho fasts and shrieks in the yard, and
gallops round in the vain search for a way of
escape. Every now and then the peaceful in-
habitant, lurking in the shadow of his hash-
fortress, is roused by a dissonant howl that
seems to come from the sink at the back of
the wash-house, or else there goes forth a
long and fear-cine cry like the crash of a
fading star, and in either case he knows that the
Christinas fowl is enjoying itself in its own gently
unobtrusive way, and is waiting for the great
change and the ensuing resurrection. At other
times he observes a glazed and filmy eye looking
gloomily out from the recesses of the ash-bucket,
an 1 a faded, tattered Pompadour comb shaking
in the breeze, and he recognises by these signs that
the fowl is prospering and. getting ready to be a
joyous and fragrant delicacy. In between its
other occupations the bird usually goes out and
hghts with the dogs and eats, and generally it
winds up, through the mercy of a beneficent
Providence, in being stolen by some passing
Chnihman, to whom it becomes a curse, a
tidh. and an eyesore, while its legitimate owner
is left to dine on blasphemy and cold ham.
Occasionally, however, the spectral songster re-
fuses to be embezzled and turns up in a cooked
condition on the table, and then the most reason-
able way to meet the difficulty is to stand with
one foot on a chair, and hold down the boiled and
stuffed incubus by putting the other hoof in the
plate, after which it is advisable to welt the
alleged victual with an adze. Por our own part,
wd spent several of our earlier years in the mining
industry,, and the experience which we then
acquired in blowing up rocks with dynamite, and
blasting tons'of hungry quartz, has done us good
service in our combats with more than one old and
hide-bound emblem of Christmas festivity. The
method of killing thisescaped albatross arc various;
one is to grasp him by the head and shake him
convulsive y in the air, and another, and an easier
device, is to guillotine the dejected article of food
—after which he usually escapes into the house
without his head, and rushes madly all over
the furniture, and is ultimately found with
his corpse jammed among the spoons. His busi-
ness, whim lie is dead, is to disseminate joyous
hilarity with his bones and his scaly legs,
and to implant the seeds of good fellowship
in the human soul and stamp them down with
his big, coarse feet; he is an indigestible object
that falls with a dull, sickening thud on the bright
lexicon of youth, and he rolls through the waste
places of cookery and gnashes his teeth as he
rolls, and even after he is eaten he leaves behind
him a memory of a huge blazing fowl that rushes
with a whizz and a hiss through a land of dreams.
He is no visible good in his deceased state, and he
is no good when he is not deceased except to dig
up largo areas of ground with his cumbrous hoofs,
and look for worms. Possibly he was meant as
firewood, and even viewed as building material ho
has many advantages, but somehow the world has
resolved to consider him in the light of an article
of diet, and it refuses to recognise how infamously
it has been deceived.
